==PAGE:1==
Degradation of Pharmaceutical Compounds
in the Grindsted Factory Plume
- Tools and Assessment
Master’s Thesis at the Technical University of Denmark
A project submitted at the
Department of Environmental Engineering
Presented by
Helene Draborg
DTU Environment
Technical University of Denmark
January 2021
==PAGE:2==
Abstract
Contamination of groundwater is a widespread environmental problem due to human activities and
emphasizes a need for eﬃcient remediation and protection strategies. In order to assess possible micro-
bial remediation strategies, it is crucial to evaluate the occurrence and extent of natural biodegradation.
Knowledge about the potential for microbial degradation of pharmaceuticals is very limited, especially
concerning the formation of degradation products. This project examines the degradation of pharma-
ceuticals in a contaminant plume by applying traditional assessment methods such as hydrological,
geochemical and chemical measurements as well as selected tools for in-situ degradation assessment
- characterisation bacterial degraders, metabolite analysis and use of inherent tracers.
The distribution and composition of a multiple contaminant plume discharging from Grindsted factory
site were outlined in means of pharmaceutical compounds. Based on existing and newly acquired data,
it was conﬁrmed that the distribution of pharmaceuticals in the plume is complex with diﬀerences
in dominating substance groups along the plume and over depth as well as considerably changes and
variances in concentration levels of individual compounds within a few years.
The contaminant plume consists of 20 key pharmaceuticals including eight barbiturates, nine sul-
fonamides, N-N-diethylnicotinamide, ethyl urethane and meprobamate. Among these compounds,
the microbial degradation of sulfonamides was best described including several identiﬁed microbial
degraders and metabolites while information about the biodegradability of the remaining pharmaceu-
ticals was limited, contradicting and inadequate.
Two metabolites of pharmaceuticals were detected in the Grindsted aquifer providing documentation
for degradation. The occurrence of degradation in the plume was further supported by trends in the
concentrations of potential metabolites and by relationships between the pharmaceuticals and two
inherent tracers, bromide and benzene. Furthermore, potential for biodegradation was indicated by
the presence of three bacterial genera of relevant pharmaceutical degraders in the aquifer, however, the
presence of speciﬁc bacterial degraders and their activity have not yet been demonstrated. Supporting
investigations including additional sampling locations, speciﬁcation of the microbial characterisation
and reﬁnement of the metabolite screening are proposed to strengthen the degradation assessment.
Based on the results from the applied degradation assessment tools as well as the described geology,
hydrogeology and contaminant distribution, a simple conceptual model of the plume was proposed.
This model implies the existence of diﬀerent degradation zones in the plume, whereof degradation
products formed by reduction are mainly generated centrally in the plume while degradation products
from oxidative processes are mainly formed closer to Grindsted stream and close to the factory site.
This project demonstrated potential for monitored natural attenuation (MNA) as a viable remediation
approach at the Grindsted site, however, pharmaceuticals are continuously discharging to Grindsted
stream and optimal conditions for degradation of the compounds have not been demonstrated. Thus,
MNA is not a recommended strategy for remediation of the plume of pharmaceuticals based on
ﬁndings in this project, however, further research is required to fully assess the potential. The work
conducted in this project will be a part of a scientiﬁc background for future investigations of the
contaminant plume of pharmaceuticals from Grindsted factory site and contribute to the evaluation of
long-term remediation strategies at the site.

==PAGE:3==
Preface
The work presented in this master thesis, entitled Degradation of Pharmaceutical Compounds in the
Grindsted Factory Plume - Tools and Assessment, was carried out at the Department of Environmental
Engineering (DTU Environment), Technical University of Denmark. This project was conducted
under supervision of Professor Poul Løgstrup Bjerg and Associate Professor Mette Martina Broholm.
Technical guidance was received from Head of Laboratory Section Jens Schaarup Sørensen, Field
Technician Flemming Møller and Research Engineer Mikael Olsson. This master thesis was completed
in the period from August 2020 to January 2021 and accounts for 30 ECTS points.
Technical University of Denmark, 24th of January 2021
(Helene Draborg, s154315)
ii
==PAGE:4==
Acknowledgements
First of all, I would like to thank my two supervisors, Associate Professor Mette M. Broholm and
Professor Poul L. Bjerg, for their highly valuable supervision throughout my thesis, and for continu-
ously pushing the project forward and in new directions. I am grateful for our helpful and enriching
discussions of the ﬁndings and other aspects of the project. Also, sincere thanks to Postdoc Cecilie B.
Ottosen who provided me with data and general insights about the study site in the early stages of my
work, and later participated in the discussions and meetings about the project.
I am thankful to Lone Dissing and Jørn K. Pedersen from the Southern Region of Denmark for sharing
literature and general knowledge about the study site and supporting the project in general.
My gratitude also goes to technicians Jens Schaarup Sørensen and Flemming Møller for guidance and
assistance during the ﬁeld investigations, and to Mikael Olsson for preparation and development of
the analytical methods and performing the actual analysis.
Lastly, I am thankful for all help and guidance received during the preparation of this master thesis;
DTU staﬀmembers including PhD students, research assistants and administrative employees for their
practical help and general advice, and my fellow students also involved in projects at the Grindsted
site for sharing data and insights about the site.

Chapter 1
Introduction
Contamination of groundwater with organic substances is a widespread environmental problem and
is constituting a serious risk to the water supply, which in Denmark depends solely on groundwater
(Jorgensen and Stockmarr, 2009; Lapworth et al., 2012; Navarrete et al., 2008). Various point sources
including former industrial sites, gasoline stations, and old landﬁlls are contributing to contamination
of groundwater aquifers (Bedient et al., 1999; Lapworth et al., 2012). This emphasises a need for
eﬃcient remediation of the aquifers and protection strategies to ensure the availability of groundwater
as a clean resource for future generations.
Conventional remediation technologies, such as pump and treat, can be expensive, demanding, and
in some cases fail to reach the goals within a reasonable time frame (Bombach et al., 2010; Reitzel,
2005). Hence, interest in alternative remediation solutions has grown over the years. One of these
strategies is monitored natural attenuation (MNA) which has shown great promise for a cost-eﬀective
removal of a wide variety of organic contaminants. MNA is a passive in-situ remediation approach
that relies on natural attenuation processes to remediate the contaminated site (Bjerg et al., 2011;
Jørgensen et al., 2010; USEPA, 1999).
Natural attenuation processes are attributed to sorption,
volatilization, dilution, dispersion, biodegradation and abiotic degradation. Although the term MNA
suggests that all attenuation processes are accepted, the degradation processes are considered the
most important as they are the only truly destructive processes. Microbial degradation may lead to
complete mineralization of the compounds depending on the environmental conditions while abiotic
degradation in many cases only leads to a transformation of the compounds (Caracciolo et al., 2013;
Reitzel et al., 2004). Thus, microbial degradation of the contaminants is preferred in MNA as the
abiotic degradation products may still pose an environmental risk (Jørgensen et al., 2010).
Interest has grown for selection of MNA as an eﬀective remedial strategy at sites contaminated with
‘emerging contaminants’ such as pesticides, pharmaceuticals and consumer care products (Aldas-
Vargas et al., 2019; Bjerg et al., 2011; Ottosen et al., 2020; Reitzel et al., 2004). Thus, the eﬃciency of
the natural attenuation processes needs to be demonstrated. The common approach is to use three lines
of evidence; (I) ﬁeld-scale documentation of mass removal processes, (II) demonstration of ﬁeld-scale
degradation based on in-situ indicators, and (III) supportive evidence such as microbial degradation
characterisation (Wiedemeier et al., 1999; Jørgensen et al., 2010; USEPA, 1999). Currently, the major
barrier to acceptance of MNA as an eﬀective remedial strategy for emerging contaminants is the
diﬃculty in demonstrating that reductions in the contaminant concentration occur due to degradation
rather than non-destructive attenuation processes (Aldas-Vargas et al., 2019; Bombach et al., 2010).
Therefore, tools for assessment of in-situ biodegradation have become a highly relevant ﬁeld of
research (Bombach et al., 2010; Ottosen et al., 2019, 2020).
This thesis will focus on contamination with pharmaceutical compounds which is of increasing
1
==PAGE:16==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
environmental concern for groundwater systems (D’Alessio and Ray, 2016; Sayadi et al., 2010). Phar-
maceuticals have been extensively used in the past decades and are often persistent. Besides, they may
have adverse ecotoxicological and pharmacological eﬀects (Sayadi et al., 2010; Balbarini et al., 2020).
Despite their importance, studies which address the transformation of pharmaceutical compounds
in groundwater or sediment systems have only recently emerged and tracing of the degradation of
the compounds in the environment is often diﬃcult. Many intermediates and metabolites are still
unknown or not even detectable as they are further transformed (Bottoni et al., 2010; D˛ebska et al.,
2004). In general, the environmental fate of pharmaceuticals is far from being well understood.
Contamination with pharmaceuticals has mainly been related to wastewaters where various pharma-
ceuticals widely used by humans are excreted and continuously discharged, however, contaminated
sites including landﬁlls and industrial sites cam also lead to contaminant plumes with high concen-
trations of pharmaceuticals (Sayadi et al., 2010; D’Alessio and Ray, 2016). In Denmark, Grindsted
factory site is the source of a groundwater plume consisting of various pharmaceutical compounds in
high concentrations along with chlorinated ethenes (tetrachloroethylene, PCE, and trichloroethylene,
TCE) and BTEX compounds (BTEX is an acronym for benzene, toluene, ethylbenzene and xylenes)
(Rønde et al., 2017). All the contaminants originate from manufacturing of pharmaceutical products
such as sleeping pills and antibiotics. Chlorinated solvents and their degradation products, pharma-
ceuticals, and BTEXs have all been detected in the stream water of Grindsted stream around 1 km
south of the factory site (Balbarini et al., 2020; Rasmussen et al., 2016; Sonne et al., 2017). Studies
have shown that there is a signiﬁcant natural degradation in the plume of chlorinated solvents and
BTEXs, while the composition of pharmaceutical compounds in the plume also has changed over the
monitoring period from the late 1980s to 2017 indicating natural attenuation (Balbarini et al., 2020;
Frederiksen et al., 2018). This suggests that MNA may be a viable remediation approach at the site
but the eﬃciency of natural attenuation processes of the pharmaceuticals in the plume needs to be
demonstrated.
This thesis aims to contribute to the understanding of natural attenuation and especially degradation
of pharmaceuticals in the contaminant plume discharging from the factory site in Grindsted and the
governing natural attenuation processes of key pharmaceuticals.
The speciﬁc objectives of the thesis are to: (1) review natural attenuation processes of selected
pharmaceuticals occurring in the plume, including their potential for degradation; (2) identify, develop
and assess tools for in-situ degradation assessment of pharmaceutical substances in the plume; (3)
interpret the distribution and composition of pharmaceutical compounds and link to hydrogeology
and redox conditions in the plume; (4) apply selected tools for in-situ degradation assessment and
evaluate whether MNA is a viable remediation strategy at the site; and (5) elaborate on perspectives
and implications of microbial remediation approaches in the Grindsted plume.
In this study, the term pharmaceutical compounds refers to all compounds which are related to
pharmaceutical production. Thus, the term covers both active pharmaceutical ingredients, which
have pharmacologic eﬀects, and excipients, which are included in the manufacturing process or are
contained in the ﬁnished pharmaceutical products. This study does not distinguish between active
ingredients and excipients in the investigation of the compounds.
2
==PAGE:17==
Chapter 2
Site Description - Grindsted stream area
This chapter provides a brief description of the study site in Grindsted in terms of geology, hydro-
geology, and the history of contamination from the factory site. Furthermore, the speciﬁc locations
considered in this study will be presented.
The town of Grindsted is located in the central part of Jutland in Denmark (see Figure 2.1B), within
the Southern Region of Denmark. Grindsted stream passes through the town with a westward ﬂow
direction (Sonne et al., 2017; Rasmussen et al., 2016). Two major sources of contamination are
considered to aﬀect the stream; to the north, a large factory site (see Figure 2.1A), and to the south, an
old landﬁll (not shown in Figure 2.1) (Rambøll, 2020). This thesis will only assess the contamination
from Grindsted factory site and will focus on the area from the factory site to Grindsted stream as
well as a stretch along the stream in the western direction. A main discharge area (red rectangle
in Figure 2.1A) of the contamination linked to the factory site has been determined in previous
studies, and will be referred to as the main contaminant discharge area throughout this thesis. Within
this area, high groundwater concentrations of chlorinated ethenes (mainly cis-1,2-dichloroethene and
vinyl chloride), pharmaceutical compounds and benzene are discharging to Grindsted stream. Outside
this area, chlorinated ethenes only enter the stream in low concentrations while the pharmaceutical
compounds discharge along a 2 km stretch of the stream and in higher concentrations than usually
observed in groundwater (Sonne et al., 2017; Rasmussen et al., 2016; Balbarini et al., 2020).
2.1
History of contamination from the factory site
Chlorinated ethenes, pharmaceutical compounds, and aromatic hydrocarbons have been detected in the
aquifer system beneath the town of Grindsted, (Rambøll, 2020; Rasmussen et al., 2016; Sonne et al.,
2017). This contamination has been related to Grindsted factory which is a former pharmaceutical
production facility located north of the stream. The production consisted of various pharmaceutical
products and grew rapidly in the late 1940s after World War II. The increase in production resulted in
increased environmental impacts, which particularly induced widescale contamination of the ground-
water in the area between Grindsted factory and Grindsted stream. During the 1970s, several initiatives
were introduced to reduce the release of contaminants to the environment (Krüger A.S., 1992).
3
==PAGE:18==
CHAPTER 2. SITE DESCRIPTION - GRINDSTED STREAM AREA
Figure 2.1: (A) Map of Grindsted stream, Grindsted factory site and some wells used in previous
investigations. The main contaminant discharge area is marked with a red box. (B) Location of
Grindsted on a map of Denmark.
The contamination has been monitored for more than 25 years, where the discharge of pharmaceu-
tical compounds from the factory site has decreased considerably over the period while increasing
concentrations are detected close to Grindsted stream (Frederiksen et al., 2018; Rambøll, 2020). In
1988-1990, pharmaceuticals were detected in concentrations up to 32,855 µg/L (sulfanilic acid) just
downstream the factory site in well 114.1425, while in 2012 the highest detected concentration in the
same well was 102 µg/L (sulfaguanidine) of a single compound. In 1990, the highest concentration
of a single pharmaceutical was measured to 35-55 µg/L (sulfanilic acid) in well 114.1495 close to the
stream. In 2015, the highest detected concentration at the stream was 1,600 µg/L (ethyl urethane) in
well 114.1448. The locations of the mentioned wells are shown in Figure 2.1A. These changes in dom-
inating compounds, concentration levels and distribution indicate that measurable natural attenuation
is occurring at the site. Based on recent measurements, the plume of contaminated compounds are
located between well 114.1447 and Grindsted stream rather than close to the source area at Grindsted
factory, however, some compounds are still present in notable concentrations near the factory area
(Frederiksen et al., 2018). It should be noted that only ﬁve diﬀerent pharmaceutical compounds
(ethyl urethane, sulfanilamide, sulfaguanidine, sulfacarbamide and sulfanilic acid) were analysed in
1988-1990 and 2012. Thus, other pharmaceutical compounds may have dominated if analysed.
4
==PAGE:19==
CHAPTER 2. SITE DESCRIPTION - GRINDSTED STREAM AREA
2.2
Regional geology and hydrology
2.2.1
Geology
Grindsted is situated west of the main stationary line of the Weichselian glaciations, thus, the upper
geology is characterised by the sandy deposits of the Grindsted outwash plain (Heron et al., 1998).
The upper 10-12 m of the geology comprises Quaternary meltwater sand with an embedded sand
till layer (Heron et al., 1998; Maurya et al., 2018). Close to the stream, postglacial freshwater sand
and peat deposits overlie the meltwater sand deposits (Balbarini et al., 2020; Maurya et al., 2018).
Below the Quaternary deposits, a 60-m thick succession of Miocene sediments (Odderup formation)
consisting mainly of mica and quartz sand is present(Heron et al., 1998; Maurya et al., 2018). The
Miocene deposit is characterised by alternating sand and clay/lignite beds (Krüger A.S., 1992; Maurya
et al., 2018). The clay and lignite successions are present between 25- and 15-m asl with a decreasing
thickness from 5-m close to the stream to less than 0.5-m at well 114.1447. Close to the stream,
this layer separates the aquifer into a shallow unconﬁned aquifer and a deep semiconﬁned aquifer
while at well 114.1447 and closer to the factory site, only one unconﬁned aquifer is found (Balbarini
et al., 2020; Heron et al., 1998). At around -40-m asl, a regional and thick conﬁning clay layer starts
(Arnum formation) followed by an additional Miocene sand deposit (Bastrup formation) (Balbarini
et al., 2020). This thesis will only focus on the aquifer system in the upper 80 m below ground surface.
Figure 2.2 presents a conceptual geological model for the Grindsted stream area generated by Maurya
et al. (2018).
Figure 2.2: Conceptual geological model for the Grindsted stream area (Maurya et al., 2018).
2.2.2
Hydrology
Based on synchronised sounding of wells in the entire Grindsted area in 2012 by Orbicon, a map of
the regional groundwater level in the upper aquifer was produced (Orbicon, 2013). Figure 2.3 presents
the hydropotential map. There is an overall groundwater ﬂow towards Grindsted stream in the western
direction as indicated by the equipotential lines. As the ﬂow is perpendicular to the equipotential lines
and directed towards lower hydraulic heads (h), the ﬂow of groundwater is considered to be directed
towards the stream from both sides (Frederiksen, 2017).
5
==PAGE:20==
CHAPTER 2. SITE DESCRIPTION - GRINDSTED STREAM AREA
Figure 2.3: Regional hydropotential map of Grindsted stream and the surrounding area (Frederiksen,
2017).
Grindsted stream has a catchment area of 200 km2 and is a gaining stream with an annual average ﬂow
of 2000 L/s (Rasmussen et al., 2016). The width is 8 to 12 m, while the depth is ranging between 1 to
2.5 m (Balbarini et al., 2017; Petersen, 2012). The stream was found to be hydrologically connected
with the groundwater at the local scale by Frederiksen (2017) as there is a considerable correlation
between the stream water level and the hydraulic head in the screens. The correlation was more clear
in the screens above the lignite layer (Frederiksen, 2017).
2.3
Locations considered in this study
Locations, where analyses of pharmaceutical compounds have been conducted in 2019 and 2020, are
included in this thesis and covers the well sites FG1, FG2, FG3, FA1, GÅ1, GÅ2 and GÅ3 and well
114.2568 and mlp-10 (Rambøll, 2020; Ottosen et al., 2020). The water samples, SB1-SB8, are also
included as the samples were collected in March 2019 from piezometers driven into the streambed
(Ottosen et al., 2020). Furthermore, well 144.1447 and 114.2508 are included for a better coverage
of the area of study. The latter wells have data for pharmaceutical compounds from 2016 and 2015,
respectively (GEUS, 2020; Ottosen et al., 2020). All selected locations are outlined in Figure 2.4.
6
==PAGE:21==
CHAPTER 2. SITE DESCRIPTION - GRINDSTED STREAM AREA
Figure 2.4: Overview of the study site. The selected well sites and wells (A) between the factory
site and the stream and (B) close to the stream are underlined in red. (C) The samples from the
streambed. The black crosses in (C) were samples collected in Transect 2 and grey crosses were
collected 1.5 m downstream of Transect 2. The blue arrow indicates the general ﬂow direction below
the stream, from nearly horizontal to almost vertical. Note, that well 114.2508 are shown in both (A)
and (B).
The wells at the seven well sites (FG1-FG3, FA1 and GÅ1-GÅ3) were all established in 2019 by
Rambøll (2020) with the purpose of further investigation of the contamination plume from the factory
site. At each well site, there are three wells with two screens in each. The distance between the wells
is approximately 5 m, however, they are considered as one location with six screens in varying depth.
The depth of the screens at the seven well sites is almost similar. The three well sites, FG1-FG3, are
located just downstream the factory site, while FA1 is considered to be located centrally in the plume.
The well sites, GÅ1-GÅ3, are located along Grindsted stream in the western direction. Geological
descriptions of the well sites are provided in Section 4.3 in Rambøll (2020). Samples from the well
sites had been collected from November 2019 to February 2020 (Rambøll, 2020).
In May 2020, the concentrations of 36 pharmaceutical compounds were measured in water samples
from stream water collected at 10 (ST1, ST4, ST6 and ST8-ST14) out of 14 stations in Grindsted
stream and at three locations in the channel and lake Engsøen (Filip Floks, 2020). An overview of the
locations is provided in Figure 2.5. The results from this investigation are brieﬂy considered in this
study to assess the pharmaceutical compounds detected in the stream water.
7
==PAGE:22==
CHAPTER 2. SITE DESCRIPTION - GRINDSTED STREAM AREA
Figure 2.5: Maps of the locations of the 14 stations in Grindsted Stream and the 3 stations in the
channel and Grindsted Engsø. (A) The entire study site including the location of the factory site and
the landﬁll. (B) Part of the investigated area from Tingvejen to the outlet of lake Engsøen including a
close-up picture of the considered main discharge area of contaminants. The dotted red lines indicate
the main discharge area of chlorinated ethenes.

==PAGE:23==
Chapter 3
Identiﬁcation of key pharmaceutical
compounds
This chapter provides an overview of 20 key pharmaceutical compounds considered to constitute the
bulk of the pharmaceutical contamination from Grindsted factory site. The overview includes their
general properties and degradation potentials.
The groundwater contamination originating from Grindsted factory site composes of many diﬀerent
pharmaceutical compounds, however, 20 key compounds were identiﬁed to constitute the bulk of the
contamination. The compounds were selected based on concentration levels and the number of times
the compound was detected in the most recent measurements (see the included locations in Section
2.3). The following criteria were set up for identiﬁcation of key compounds in this study:
1. Summarised concentrations exceed 1000 µg/L.
2. Summarised concentrations exceed 100 µg/L and the compound is detected more than 10 times.
All key compounds comply with minimum one of these criteria and include barbiturates, sulfon-
amides, N-N-diethylnicotinamide, ethyl urethane and meprobamate. The following sections present
the properties including potential for degradation and transport of the 20 key pharmaceuticals. All
ﬁndings are summarized in Table 3.1 and selected physico-chemical properties are listed in Table A.1.
The physico-chemical data were obtained from DTU Food (2020), while the potential for degradation
of the key pharmaceuticals was assessed by consulting existing literature about degradation of the
speciﬁc or similar compounds in diﬀerent media. Also, the potential for degradation was assessed by
identifying functional groups which are likely to undergo abiotic degradation and may also be involved
in biotic degradation, whereof the latter is mainly based on Sum et al. (2007). Furthermore, possible
biodegradation pathways for each key compound were searched by the EAWAG pathway prediction
system which predicts microbial catabolic reactions using substructure searching, atom-to-atom and
a rule-base mapping (EAWAG, 2020).
3.1
Barbiturates
Barbiturates are active pharmaceutical ingredients that act as central nervous system depressants and
have been widely used as sedatives, hypnotics or narcotics, especially during the 1960s (Peschka et al.,
2006). Barbiturates were the most common active substance in sleeping pills until the 1970s but were
replaced as they have a narrow therapeutic range and pose potential for abuse (Holm et al., 1995;
Peschka et al., 2006). Since the replacement, barbiturates have mainly been used in veterinary drugs
but are still found in a few medical preparations(Peschka et al., 2006).

CHAPTER 3. IDENTIFICATION OF KEY PHARMACEUTICAL COMPOUNDS
Barbiturates are derived from barbituric acid and share a 2,4,6-pyrimidintrione ring with up to three
side chains. The ease of substituting various groups at the C5 atom of the parent molecule yields over
2500 barbiturates, of which only about 60 barbiturates have been produced for commercial purposes
(Peschka et al., 2006). The physical-chemical properties of the various barbiturates depend upon the
side chains attached to the ring. The molecular structures for the selected key barbiturates are provided
in Figure 3.1.
Figure 3.1: Molecular structure of the eight key barbiturates.
The steps in the generation of
the metabolites from biodegradation are marked; a) ethane, b) 2-methyl-2-butanol (amobarbital)
and 2-pentanol (pentobarbital) and c) hydroxyamobarbital (amobarbital) and 5-Ethyl-5-((1R,3S)-3-
hydroxy-1-methyl-butyl)-barbituric acid (pentobarbital).
All the selected barbiturates except isobutyl barbituric acid have log(Kow)-values in the range 0.57-
2.10 which indicates a weak to moderate preference for the non-polar phase. Thus, the barbiturates
are expected to be less mobile than the sulfonamides, ethyl urethane and meprobamate which have
lower Kow-values (Balbarini et al., 2020). According to the mobility classiﬁcation based on KOC
presented in McCall et al. (1980), the mobility of the majority of the barbiturates is high to very
high. Isobutyl barbituric acid has a Kow-value of -0.02, thus, the compound is more mobile than the
other key barbiturates. All barbiturates are highly soluble in water (603-17,170 mg/L) and have low
hydrophobicity. Distribution by volatilization can be excluded as a possible transportation pathway
since barbiturates have low vapour pressures and Henry’s law constants (<0.01 Pa·m3/mol) (Peschka
et al., 2006).
3.1.1
Abiotic degradation of barbiturates
Only a few laboratory studies of barbiturate degradation have been conducted. 5-Allyl-5-isobutyl bar-
bituric acid and pentobarbital were found to resist hydrolysis at pH-values in the range pH=2-12 while
barbital undergoes hydrolysis (Nobay and Acquisto, 2014; Peschka et al., 2006). No information about
degradation by hydrolysis was found for the remaining key compounds, however, due to similarities
in structures with 5-Allyl-5-isobutyl barbituric acid and pentobarbital, they are not considered to be
susceptible to hydrolysis. In addition, the potential for degradation by hydrolysis was found to be low
based on the functional groups of the key barbiturates (Sum et al., 2007).
11
==PAGE:26==
CHAPTER 3. IDENTIFICATION OF KEY PHARMACEUTICAL COMPOUNDS
Regarding photolysis, some of the barbiturates may be susceptible to photolysis by sunlight. 5-Allyl-5-
isobutyl barbituric acid does contain chromophores that absorb at wavelengths >290 nm, and therefore
may degrade by photolysis, while amobarbital does not contain similar chromophores (Nobay and
Acquisto, 2014; PubChem, 2020a). Concerning pentobarbital, Hofmann et al. (2020) states that the
compound has photosensitizing potential while Nobay and Acquisto (2014) states that the compound
is unlikely to undergo photolysis. In a study by Barton et al. (1982), barbital was found to undergo
photochemical ring opening. It was not possible to ﬁnd any information about the photodegradation
of the remaining compounds, however, allobarbital may be susceptible to photolysis as the compound
has C-C double bonds which are considered to be a light absorption chromophore.
3.1.2
Biodegradation of barbiturates
In relation to biodegradation, barbiturates are considered not to be biodegradable (Balbarini et al.,
2020; Peschka et al., 2006). However, in a tank experiment with groundwater contaminated with
pharmaceuticals of wastewater origin, phenobarbital was removed at a rate of 0.002 d-1 under anoxic
conditions and 0.001 d-1 under oxic conditions (Burke et al., 2014). It should be noted that phe-
nobarbital is not among the eight key barbiturates at Grindsted, but has similar physico-chemical
properties (Balbarini et al., 2020). In studies by Moss (1965), Freudenthal and Carroll (1974) and
Hermann (1976), the major metabolites of the key barbiturates, amobarbital and pentobarbital, were
outlined along with the main metabolites of other barbiturates. Table 3.2 gives a summary of the main
metabolites of mephobarbital, phenobarbital, pentobarbital, amobarbital, butobarbital, secobarbital
and allobarbital as well as the known degrader species. Phenobarbital is the initial metabolite of
mephobarbital which can be further metabolized to the compounds listed in Table 3.2 as metabolites
for phenobarbital. In a study by Kim and Fulco (1983), 13 barbiturates were found to induce monooxy-
genase from a speciﬁc bacillus species, Bacillus megaterium ATCC 14581. The barbiturates found
to induce monooxygenase activity included the following barbiturates detected at the Grindsted site;
allobarbital, amobarbital, butabarbital, hexobarbital, pentobarbital and secobarbital (Kim and Fulco,
1983).

letters (a), (b) and (c) refer to the markings in Figure 3.1.

It should be noted that some commercially available barbiturates are racemic mixtures of enantiomers,
thus the individual metabolites can diﬀer in conﬁguration (Freudenthal and Carroll, 1974). Among the
barbiturates presented in Table 3.2, three of the compounds are chiral; mephobarbital, pentobarbital,
and secobarbital.
Numerous pathways for microbial degradation of barbiturates at level 1 were predicted using the
EAWAG pathway prediction system (description of tool in EAWAG (2020)). The main pathways
for all key barbiturates were either (1) breakage of the ring structure into ends of a carboxylate
and a primary amide or (2) hydroxylation of the aliphatic side chain. Figure 3.2 presents the two
mentioned degradation pathways at level 1 with barbital as an example. At the following degradation
levels, several pathways were predicted to be likely. Some of the metabolites listed in Table 3.2 were
conﬁrmed by the EAWAG pathway prediction system.
Figure 3.2: Two main biodegradation pathways for barbital at level 1; (1) breakage of ring structure
into ends of a carboxylate and a primary amide or (2) hydroxylation of the aliphatic side chain.
Predicted by the EAWAG pathway prediction system (EAWAG, 2020).
3.2
Sulfonamides
Sulfonamides are broad-spectrum bacteriostatic antibiotics which inhibit bacterial growth (Bhattachar-
jee, 2016a). They were the ﬁrst chemically synthesized antibiotics and are one of the most commonly
consumed antibiotics in the EU (Bhattacharjee, 2016b; Białk-Bielińska et al., 2012a; Boreen et al.,
2004). About 100 derivatives of sulfonamides have been produced commercially since the 1930s
(Holm et al., 1995). Currently, only two sulfonamides, sulfamethizole and sulfamethoxazole, are
registered for medical use in Denmark (Engberg and Frimodt-Møller, 2020). Sulfamethizole is a part
of the identiﬁed key sulfonamides at the Grindsted while sulfamethoxazole has not been included in
the analyses.
The sulfonamides have a common structure but diﬀer in the N-bound substituent of the sulfonamide
linkage (Boreen et al., 2004). The molecular structures for the selected nine key sulfonamides are
provided in Figure 3.3.
13
==PAGE:28==
CHAPTER 3. IDENTIFICATION OF KEY PHARMACEUTICAL COMPOUNDS
Figure 3.3: Molecular structures of the nine key sulfonamides.
Sulfonamides are stable under typical environmental conditions with a long half-life (Białk-Bielińska
et al., 2012a). Of the nine key sulfonamides investigated in this study, ﬁve are characterized by negative
log(Kow)-values ranging from -2.16 to -0.62 which indicates negligible sorption to sediment. The
four remaining sulfonamides with an additional heteroaromatic group have higher log(Kow)-values
ranging from -0.09 to 0.54, thus, they are expected to have a slight preference for the non-polar phase
which may result in lower mobility than the other ﬁve compounds. However, according to the mobility
classiﬁcation based on KOC presented in McCall et al. (1980), the mobility of all of the sulfonamides
is very high. Volatilization of the sulfonamides is not considered relevant, since the Henry’s law
constants are lower than 0.01 Pa·m3/mol. Similar to the barbiturates, the majority of the sulfonamides
are also highly soluble in water (1,050-12,500 mg/L) except sulfadiazine (77 mg/L) and sulfathiazole
(373 mg/L) which have moderate solubility.
Sulfonamides have both acid and base properties. The value of pKa and pKb for each key sulfonamide is
presented in Table A.1 in Appendix A. The pKb-value describes the equilibrium between the positively
charged, protonated amino group of the compound and its electrically neutral conjugate base while
the pKa, in turn, describes the deprotonation of the SO2NH moiety (all except sulfanilic acid) or
the SO2OH moiety in sulfanilic acid (Białk-Bielińska et al., 2012a). At around neutral pH, which is
often found in natural soils and aquifers, sulfanilic acid will be deprotonated while sulfanilamide and
sulfaguanidine exist as neutral molecules. The remaining key sulfonamides exist as either neutral or
deprotonated species depending on the speciﬁc pH.
14
==PAGE:29==
CHAPTER 3. IDENTIFICATION OF KEY PHARMACEUTICAL COMPOUNDS
3.2.1
Abiotic degradation of sulfonamides
Sulfaguanidine, sulfanilamide, and sulfanilic acid are all possible degradation products of heterocyclic
sulfonamides (Holm et al., 1995). In the study by Boreen et al. (2004), sulfanilic acid was identiﬁed as
the main degradation product resulting from the photochemical degradation of ﬁve other sulfonamides.
Two of these sulfonamides, sulfamethizole and sulfathiazole, have been identiﬁed as key compounds
in this study. The rate of direct photolysis of these compounds is dependent upon the heterocyclic
side chain and the pH of the solution (Boreen et al., 2004).
In general, sulfathiazole degrades
relatively quickly while sulfamethizole degrades at a moderate rate (Boreen et al., 2004). Sulfonamides
including sulfacetamide, sulfathiazole, sulfamethoxazole, and sulfadiazine can undergo photocatalytic
degradation(Ying et al., 2013; Baran et al., 2006; Hofmann et al., 2020). Sulfanilamide, sulfanilic acid
and aniline compounds were mentioned as organic degradation products along with hydroquinone,
quinone, carboxylic acid and dicarboxylic acid (Baran et al., 2006; Birkigt et al., 2015a; Boreen et al.,
2004). Table 3.3 summarises the possible metabolites from photolysis of sulfonamides, however,
additional metabolites are likely to exist.
Table 3.3: Summary of the metabolites from photochemical degradation of sulfonamides.
Metabolites from photolysis
• Sulfanilic acid
• Hydroquinone
• Sulfaguanidine
• Quinone
• Sulfanilamide
• Carboxylic acid
• Aniline compounds
• Dicarboxylic acid
The results from studies of degradation of sulfonamides by hydrolysis are somewhat conﬂicting. Ac-
cording to some studies, the sulfonamide bond in some sulfonamides can break to produce sulfanilic
acid during acidic hydrolysis, while other studies state that some sulfonamides including sulfachlor-
pyridazine, sulfadimethoxine, and sulfathiazole are unlikely to undergo hydrolysis at low temperatures
and require high concentrations of strong acids or bases (Loftin et al., 2008; Ying et al., 2013). Among
the mentioned compounds, only sulfathiazole is a key sulfonamides in this thesis. Another study by
Białk-Bielińska et al. (2012b) showed that under typical environmental conditions sulfonamides are
hydrolytically stable with a long half-life. In addition, hydrolysis of sulfonamides is not expected
based on the structural features of the investigated sulfonamides (Sum et al., 2007; Loftin et al., 2008).
3.2.2
Biodegradation of sulfonamides
Biodegradation of sulfonamides has been observed in both natural and engineered ecosystems, under
either aerobic or anaerobic conditions (Chen and Xie, 2018; García Galán et al., 2012; Göbel et al.,
2007; Jin et al., 2017). The majority of the studies concern biodegradation in wastewater, sludge
and manure while literature about biodegradation of sulfonamides in soil and groundwater is limited,
particularly regarding high concentrations in a mixed plume, and in the context of aquifer redox
conditions (Balbarini et al., 2020). Several studies indicate that microbial degradation occurs (Chen
and Xie, 2018). Under aerobic conditions in activated sludge, diﬀerent sulfonamides showed similar
biodegradation patterns and the degraders of a certain sulfonamide could easily degrade other sulfon-
amides. This suggests that biodegradation of sulfonamides depends on similar degraders or is induced
by bacterial adaptation to other sulfonamides (Chen and Xie, 2018; Ingerslev and Halling-Sorensen,
2000; Pérez et al., 2005).
Various microbial populations involved in sulfonamide biodegradation are summarized in Chen and
Xie (2018). The majority of sulfonamide-degrading strains are isolated from activated sludge, wastew-
15
==PAGE:30==
CHAPTER 3. IDENTIFICATION OF KEY PHARMACEUTICAL COMPOUNDS
ater, and contaminated soil and seawater. The microbes are considered to utilize sulfonamides as
sources of either carbon, nitrogen or sulfur, however, sulfonamides are not the preferred source but
can be utilized in the absence of a better alternative (Bennett et al., 2017; Balbarini et al., 2020).
Several investigations concerning sulfonamides mineralization (especially regarding sulfadiazine and
sulfamethoxazole), transformation products and metabolic pathway have been conducted. The main
intermediates of sulfonamide biodegradation are typically generated by hydroxylation and acetylation
reactions at either the aniline or the aminated heteroaromatic moiety of sulfonamides (Chen and Xie,
2018). Figure 3.4 illustrates hydroxylation and acetylation of the common structure of sulfonamides
at the aniline moiety.
Figure 3.4: Acetylation and hydroxylation of sulfonamides at the aniline moiety.
Table 3.4 gives a summary of the main metabolites and the responsible bacterial species of the
biodegradation of sulfadiazine, sulfadimidine, sulfamethizole, sulfadimethoxine and sulfamethoxa-
zole. The major degradation pathways of these compounds are presented in Figure A.1 in Appendix
A. Table 3.4 and the associated references are an extension of the supplementary material of the
article by Chen and Xie (2018). Note, that the table only lists the major metabolites and contains
sulfonamides which are not identiﬁed as key compounds in this thesis. A more detailed summary of
sulfonamide-degrading bacterial strains and major bacterial groups for selected sulfonamides can be
found in Table 4 in Chen and Xie (2018).
The draft genomes of Arthrobacter sp. D2, Arthrobacter sp. D4, Microbacterium sp. C448, and
Microbacterium sp. strain BR1 have been sequenced, and they shared high genomic similarities (Chen
and Xie, 2018; Deng et al., 2016; Martin-Laurent et al., 2014; Ricken et al., 2017). Ricken et al. (2017)
proposed three sulfonamide catabolic genes; two favinmonooxygenases (SadA and SadB) genes and
one FMN reductase (SadC) gene. The SadA gene-encoded monooxygenase drives the initial ipso-
hydroxylation, while SadB and SadC are responsible for the further transformation of sulfonamides.
All three genes were widely distributed in Actinomycetes including Microbacterium sp. strain BR1,
but also detected in three other sulfonamide-degraders (Arthrobacter sp.
D2, Microbacteriu sp.
SDZm4, Microbacterium sp. C448).
For all key sulfonamides except sulfanilic acid, microbial degradation at the ﬁrst degradation level
was predicted to be possible using the EAWAG pathway prediction system (description of tool in
EAWAG (2020)). The main pathway for the compounds was predicted to be cleavage between the
sulfonyl group and the amine group creating sulfanilic acid as one of the intermediates. Microbial
degradation of sulfanilic acid was predicted to be unlikely by the EAWAG pathway prediction system.
No information about the inﬂuence of the acidic properties of sulfanilic acid on its biodegradability
was found.

In an experimental study, the compound sulfamethoxazole was observed to display reversible trans-
formation to the form 4-nitro-sulfamethoxazole under denitrifying conditions (Barbieri et al., 2012).
Although sulfamethoxazole is not among the 20 key pharmaceuticals in this thesis, it indicates that
sulfonamides are susceptible to changes in redox conditions and that many factors can aﬀect the results
obtained from degradation experiments (Balbarini et al., 2020).
3.3
Ethyl urethane
Ethyl urethane, also mentioned as ethyl carbamate or simply urethane in literature, is an ethyl ester of
carbamide. The compound was used as a hypnotic in humans and an anaesthetic in animals during the
1940s as well as in the production of the sedative meprobamate (see more details about meprobamate
in Section 3.4) (Holm et al., 1995; Ryu et al., 2015). Ethyl urethane was found to be genotoxic and
carcinogenic in 1943 (Ryu et al., 2015). The compound is naturally formed in fermented foods and
alcoholic beverages during the fermentation process and during storage, however, the health risks
associated with intake have been evaluated by experts to be of low concern (FAO and WHO, 2005).
Figure 3.5A presents the molecular structure of the compound.
Figure 3.5: Molecular structure of (A) ethyl urethane, (B) meprobamate and (C) N-N-diethylnicotinamide.
Ethyl urethane does not contain any ring structures, in contrast to the barbiturates and sulfonamides.
The compound is the smallest of the investigated pharmaceuticals with a molar mass of 89.1 g/mol and
has the second highest water solubility of 480,000 mg/L. The compound has a low logKow of -0.15,
thus sorption to soil is unlikely and the mobility of ethyl urethane is expected to be high. Volatilization
of ethyl urethane is not considered relevant, since Henry’s law constant is less than 0.01 Pa·m3/mol.
The compound has the highest vapour pressure among the key compounds.
According to Robles (2014), ethyl urethane is considered resistant to hydrolysis under environmental
conditions with estimated hydrolysis half-lives of 3300 and 330 years at pH 7 and 8, respectively.
Similar, degradation by photolysis is not expected as the compound does not contain chromophores
that absorb at wavelengths >290 nm (PubChem, 2020e). Some biodegradation studies using activated
sludge indicates that ethyl urethane may biodegrade slowly (Robles, 2014). Based on the EAWAG
pathway prediction system, hydroxylation of the aliphatic end of the compound is possible.
No literature on the degradation or the occurrence of ethyl urethane in groundwater has been found.
This may be because the compound is not a common groundwater contaminant (Balbarini et al., 2020).
3.4
Meprobamate
Meprobamate is an ester identiﬁed to have antianxiety activity and was introduced for medical use of
humans in the 1950s (Gorodetsky, 2014; Kraemer and Maurer, 2008). It rapidly became one of the
most widely used psychotropic agents. Currently, the compound is rarely used as knowledge about
18
==PAGE:33==
CHAPTER 3. IDENTIFICATION OF KEY PHARMACEUTICAL COMPOUNDS
the precise mechanism of action of meprobamate is still limited. Meprobamate also has major abuse
potential and many cases of dependency, habituation and overdose have been reported (LiverTox,
2020). Meprobamate is produced by the reaction of 2-methylpentanal with formaldehyde, and then
with ethyl urethane (Holm et al., 1995). The molecular structure of the compound is presented in
Figure 3.5B.
Similarly to ethyl urethane, the molecule of meprobamate does not contain any ring structures. The
molar mass of the compound is similar to the other investigated key compounds. Meprobamate has a
logKow-value of 0.7, which also is within the range of the barbiturates and sulfonamides, and hence
the compound could be expected to show similar mobility. The distribution by volatilization can
be excluded as a possible transportation pathway since meprobamate has a low vapour pressure and
Henry’s law constant (<0.01 Pa·m3/mol), however, the compound is highly soluble in water.
Hydrolysis is not expected to be an important environmental fate process as meprobamate lacks
structural features which can be readily hydrolysed. However, its primary hydrolysis product, 2-methyl-
2-n-propyl-1,3-propanediol (MPP), was observed in a study by Holm et al. (1995) (See structure of
MPP in Figure 3.6). The information about photolysis of meprobamate is contradicting. According to
(PubChem, 2020b), the compound is not expected to undergo direct photolysis as the compound does
not contain light absorption chromophores while Hofmann et al. (2020) states that meprobamate has
photosensitizing potential.
Figure 3.6: Molecular structure of 2-methyl-2-n-propyl-1,3-propanediol (MPP).
The biodegradation of meprobamate is not well understood. Several studies have assessed the rate of
biodegradation of meprobamate. The results are diverging regarding the persistence of meprobamate
in surface water and groundwater, with degradation rate constants in the range of 0.0001-0.3 d-1
(Greskowiak et al., 2017).
Other experiments also show conﬂicting results; Burke et al. (2014)
found meprobamate to be stable under both aerobic and anaerobic conditions while Lin et al. (2011)
found meprobamate to degrade with a half-life of 33 d and 55 d under oxic and anoxic conditions,
respectively (Balbarini et al., 2020). The latter experiment was conducted with microcosms. Removal
of meprobamate was observed using loamy sand, while no removal was observed with a sterilized
setup using the same soil type. This indicates that microbial activity plays an important part in the
degradation of meprobamate (Balbarini et al., 2020).
Based on the EAWAG pathway prediction system, hydroxylation of the aliphatic sidechains of meproba-
mate is possible.
3.5
N-N-diethylnicotinamide
N-N-diethylnicotinamide is a stimulant which mainly aﬀects the respiratory cycle (PubChem, 2020c).
It is commonly known as nikethamide or coramine by its former trade names. The compound was
used in the mid-twentieth century as a medical countermeasure against drug overdoses but adverse
19
==PAGE:34==
CHAPTER 3. IDENTIFICATION OF KEY PHARMACEUTICAL COMPOUNDS
reactions are related to N-N-diethylnicotinamide including coughing and nausea, thus, the compound
should not be used (U.S. Department of Health & Human Services, 2020; Aronson, 2016). The
molecular structure of the compound is shown in Figure 3.5C.
Similarly to barbiturates and sulfonamides, the molecule of N-N-diethylnicotinamide contains a ring
structure. The molar mass of the compound is similar to the other investigated key compounds. It has
a logKow-value of 0.33, which is within the range of the barbiturates and sulfonamides, and hence this
compound can be expected to show similar mobility. The distribution by volatilization can be excluded
as a possible transportation pathway since N-N-diethylnicotinamide has a low vapour pressure and
Henry’s law constant (<0.01 Pa·m3/mol). The compound has the highest solubility in water of the key
pharmaceuticals.
Based on the similarities in functional groups between N-N-diethylnicotinamide and some of the
sulfonamides, degradation of the compound by hydrolysis or photolysis may occur, however, no
literature on the environmental degradation of N-N-diethylnicotinamide was found. Regarding the
occurrence of the compound in groundwater, N-N-diethylnicotinamide was detected in groundwater
in a study in Ukraine but literature is very limited (Tang et al., 2020). The lack of literature is likely
because the compound is not a common groundwater contaminant. Based on the EAWAG pathway
prediction system, both hydroxylation of the aliphatic sidechains of the compound or formation of
acetaldehyde and a N-substituted amide are possible.
20
==PAGE:35==
Chapter 4
Evaluation of tools for in-situ degradation
assessment
This chapter presents an evaluation of selected tools for in-situ degradation assessment based on the
information the tools provide and their applicability in assessing the degradation of pharmaceutical
compounds.
Various methods have been developed to assess the degradation processes of organic compounds
occurring in-situ at contaminated sites (Bombach et al., 2010; Ottosen, 2017; Reitzel, 2005). Based
on Bombach et al. (2010), the methods can be grouped into hydrochemical methods and micro-
bial/molecular methods. Hydrochemical tools are applicable for monitoring and quantifying microbial
transformation processes and include; metabolite analysis, usage of tracers, geochemical approaches,
usage of enantiomeric ratios, and compound-speciﬁc isotope analysis. The microbial and molecular
tools provide information about the composition and structure of microbial communities including
their potential catabolic functions regarding contaminant degradation. These tools include laboratory
and in situ microcosms as well as analysis for speciﬁc microbial degraders and functional genes
(Bombach et al., 2010).
Table 4.1 gives an overview of the tools, the information they provide and their application in
assessment of the degradation of pharmaceutical compounds at the Grindsted stream area.
The
following sections will outline each method in more detail and discuss the applicability and limitations
of the mentioned tools; both in general and in relation to this thesis.
4.1
Metabolite analysis
Detection of metabolites formed by microbial degradation of contaminants can be used as an in situ
indicator of biodegradation. Likewise, increasing concentrations of metabolites with distance from
the source area can also indicate degradation (eg. Holm et al. (1995) and Reitzel et al. (2004)). Ideal
characteristics for metabolites to serve as indicators for in-situ biodegradation includes (I) unequivocal
and unique relationship to the parent substrate; (II) release from the microbial cell to the extracellular
medium; (III) biological and chemical stability; (IV) no natural, commercial, or industrial sources; (V)
water solubility; and (VI) generation as an intermediate of mineralization rather than as a product of co-
metabolism (Bombach et al., 2010; Reitzel, 2005). The following sections will discuss characteristic
(I), (III), (IV) and (VI) in relation to the study area in Grindsted while it is assumed that the two
remaining characteristics are fulﬁlled.
Unique relationship to the parent substrate (I) and impurities (IV)
Some metabolites have an unequivocal relation to a single compound, while others can be related

CHAPTER 4. EVALUATION OF TOOLS FOR IN-SITU DEGRADATION ASSESSMENT
to the microbial degradation of a variety of compounds or may have other sources than degradation
(Bombach et al., 2010; Reitzel, 2005). For both, barbiturates and sulfonamides the metabolites from
abiotic and microbial degradation are overlapping and no clear division exists. For the key barbiturates
included in this study, the metabolites from ﬁrst biodegradation level can be considered to be unique
to the parent compounds (see the main pathways in Section 3.1.2), thus, their detection would indicate
degradation.
However, due to limited research of the degradation of barbiturates, it is unknown
whether these metabolites also can be formed by abiotic degradation or exist as impurities together
with the parent compounds. For pentobarbital, three impurities were identiﬁed; 6-imino-5-ethyl-5-
(1-methylbutyl) barbituric acid (<0.2 wt%), diberal (<0.3 wt%) and isomebumal (<0.1 wt%) (NIH,
2020). The molecular structures of the impurities and pentobarbital are shown in Figure 4.1. These
impurities only constitute a small part of the compound and none of the identiﬁed impurities is similar
to the listed metabolites for pentobarbital in Table 3.2. Yet, it indicates that impurities exist in the
barbiturates.
Figure 4.1: Molecular structures of 6-imino-5-ethyl-5-(1-methylbutyl) barbituric acid, diberal and
isomebumal.
Concerning sulfonamides, the metabolites from ﬁrst biodegradation level are considered to be unique
to the sulfonamides (see the main pathway in Section 3.2.2). In addition, some of the mentioned
metabolites of both abiotic and microbial degradation are already included in the 46 pharmaceutical
compounds which were analysed at the study site between November 2019 and February 2020. The
included metabolites are; sulfanilic acid, sulfanilamide, sulfaguanidine, and aniline (not analysed in
well 114.2568 and 114.2508). All the compounds, except aniline, are also commercially produced
pharmaceuticals. Aniline is used as an intermediate in the production of pharmaceuticals and may
also exist as an impurity if the pharmaceuticals are manufactured as technical grade compounds
(Gad, 2014). Thus, detection of sulfanilic acid, sulfanilamide, sulfaguanidine and aniline can not
sorely be ascribed to degradation. Also, some of the metabolites are likely to exist as impurities
in the sulfonamides. Impurities were identiﬁed for sulfamethoxazole including sulfanilamide and
3-amino-5-methylisoxazole which are also metabolites of the compound (see Table 3.4) (PubChem,
2020d).
Besides the mentioned metabolites, the degradation of sulfonamides will generate sulfur, which may
be oxidized and observed as sulfate in the plume (Holm et al., 1995).
Thus, an increase in the
concentration of sulfate in the aquifer can indicate degradation.
Degradation of sulfonamides is
not a dominant source of sulfate and are not considered to generate a measurable increase in sulfate
concentration at Grindsted stream area. Various other factors can inﬂuence the concentration of sulfate
in the plume; (1) SO42- may be consumed as an electron acceptor (see Section 4.3); (2) the plume may
23
==PAGE:38==
CHAPTER 4. EVALUATION OF TOOLS FOR IN-SITU DEGRADATION ASSESSMENT
have a high concentration of sulfate due to the former production of sulfonamides at the Grindsted
factory site and; (3) aqueous pyrite oxidation generates acid (H+), sulfates (SO42-), and aqueous Fe(II)
ions (Dos Santos et al., 2016). Pyrite has been detected in the Grindsted sediments (Albrechtsen et al.,
1999; Heron et al., 1998).
As outlined in Chapter 3, no metabolites of ethyl urethane, meprobamate and N-N-diethylnicotinamide
have been identiﬁed in literature. All the compounds are commercially manufactured pharmaceuticals
but as mentioned in Section 3.3, ethyl urethane is also used in the production of meprobamate. Thus,
the compound may be an impurity of meprobamate or a degradation product if the degradation pathway
or part of it is the reversed synthesis reaction.
Stability of the metabolites (III)
To be able to detect a metabolite, it needs to have suﬃcient stability (Reitzel, 2005). The generated
compound does not need to be persistent but needs to exist for a longer period. Knowledge about
the stability of the speciﬁc metabolites of the key sulfonamides was not found. However, studies
have shown that the degradation products from heterocyclic moieties of sulfonamides are stable and
will typically accumulate, independent of the parent sulfonamide or conditions provided (Deng et al.,
2018). The aniline moiety of sulfonamides is considered less stable (Bouju et al., 2012; Deng et al.,
2018).
No information about the stability of the metabolites of barbiturates, ethyl urethane, meprobamate and
N-N-diethylnicotinamide was found.
An intermediate of mineralization rather than co-metabolism (VI)
It is ideal if the contaminants are degraded by mineralization to non-harmful end-products, rather
than transformed into potential toxic intermediates Reitzel (2005). However, mineralization is not a
requirement for using metabolite analysis as a tool for in-situ degradation assessment (Beller, 2000).
As stated in Table 3.4, sulfamethoxazole and sulfamethizole can be degraded by Rhodococcus
rhodochrous by co-metabolism in the presence of a readily degradable carbon source such as glucose
while in other studies Rhodococcus rhodochrous demonstrated the ability to use sulfonamides as the
sole carbon source (Gauthier et al., 2010). Thus, some of the key sulfonamides may be depleted by
co-metabolism depending on the conditions and bacterial composition (Chen and Xie, 2018). No
knowledge about co-metabolism of the remaining key compounds was found in literature.
Sulfonamide mineralization is possible but the ﬁnal extent in pure cultures is generally limited by the
degradation abilities of the bacterial degraders (Deng et al., 2018). A few studies have experimentally
proven the carbon mineralization of the aniline moiety of sulfonamide to CO2 and biomass (Bouju
et al., 2012; Larcher and Yargeau, 2011; Reis et al., 2014; Ricken et al., 2013). It is not known whether
mineralizations of barbiturates, ethyl urethane, meprobamate and N-N-diethylnicotinamide occur.
Evaluation of the applicability of metabolite analysis
In summary, detection of intermediate compounds of barbiturates and sulfonamides is considered
to be a useful tool to give an indication of degradation in the Grindsted stream area.
However,
several diﬃculties are associated with the tool including limited knowledge about the production and
degradation pathways of the contaminants and the stability of their metabolites. In addition, the
metabolites are intermediates in the degradation pathways and may only exist for a short period or at
low concentrations which can hinder their detection (Bombach et al., 2010).
24
==PAGE:39==
CHAPTER 4. EVALUATION OF TOOLS FOR IN-SITU DEGRADATION ASSESSMENT
4.2
Inherent tracers
In general, tracer testing is a very eﬃcient and multipurpose tool to characterize subsurface properties
and to investigate the spreading of both non-reactive and reactive contaminants in groundwater.
Assessment of tracers can also be used to diﬀerentiate between in situ biodegradation and other
attenuation processes aﬀecting the concentration of contaminants (Bombach et al., 2010; Reitzel,
2005). An important prerequisite is the existence of an inherent, suitable tracer, which migrates
conservatively and is measurable in signiﬁcant concentrations after dilution compared to background
values (Reitzel, 2005). In this thesis, bromide, lithium and chloride are assessed as possible tracers. In
addition, selected trace metals; arsenic (As), lead (Pb), cadmium (Cd), chromium (Cr), copper (Cu),
mercury (Hg), nickel (Ni), and zinc (Zn) are also considered as tracers.
Bromide
Bromide is used in the production of pharmaceuticals, thus, it is considered to be relevant as a tracer
of the contamination originating from the factory site in Grindsted. Bromide has been used as a tracer
in various studies as it is conservative (non-reactive) and has a low natural background concentration
of typically 0.1-0.5 mg/L (Flury and Papritz, 1993). Based on data collected between 1993 and 2013,
the concentration of bromide in groundwater is below 0.2 mg/L in the southern part of Jutland, where
Grindsted is located (Thorling et al., 2014).
At the investigated locations in Grindsted, the detected concentrations ranged between 0.032 to 1.36
mg/L for bromide.
Thus, the compound was present in elevated concentrations at some of the
investigated locations. It should be noted, that bromide has not been analysed at well 114.2508,
mlp-10 and in the samples (SB1-SB8) from the streambed.
Lithium
Similar to bromide, lithium is used in the manufacturing production of pharmaceuticals which makes
it relevant to consider as a potential inherent tracer.
Lithium has several attributes that make it
particularly useful as a tracer in hydrological systems including that it is highly soluble, does not
degrade and has low background concentrations (Stewart and Kszos, 1996). The natural lithium levels
in Danish groundwater ranges from approximately 2 µg/L in some areas to nearly 30 µg/L in others
(Kessing et al., 2017). Unfortunately, the lithium ions have aﬃnity for ion exchange which will cause
retardation (Bjerg, 1992; Wodschow et al., 2018), making lithium a less ideal tracer under certain
environmental conditions.
At the investigated locations in Grindsted, the detected concentrations of lithium ranged between 0.53
to 23 µg/L. Thus, the compound was not detected in notably elevated concentrations at the study site.
It should be noted that lithium has not been analysed at well 114.2568, 114.2508, mlp-10 and in the
samples (SB1-SB8) from the streambed.
Chloride
Chlorine compounds have been used in pharmaceutical manufacturing and formulations for many
years.
The production of many pharmaceuticals relies on chlor-alkali chemistry as part of their
production process to control of pH. Chlorinated solvents, also detected in Grindsted, are examples of
chlorine compounds used in the production (Euro Chlor, 2019). Thus, chloride is expected to be part
of the contamination originating from the factory site in Grindsted.
Chloride is a commonly used natural environmental tracer in groundwater as dissolved chloride (Cl-)
exhibits a lack of adsorption in its anionic form, exists at a low background concentration in Danish
groundwater, and has a high solubility in natural waters (Hendry et al., 2000; Kristiansen et al., 2011).
The background concentration of chloride was found to be 12-30 mg/L in the upper aquifer south
25
==PAGE:40==
CHAPTER 4. EVALUATION OF TOOLS FOR IN-SITU DEGRADATION ASSESSMENT
of Grindsted stream by Bjerg et al. (1995) and this background level expected to be similar at the
study site north of the stream. At the investigated locations in Grindsted (see Figure 2.4, the detected
concentrations of chloride ranged between 16 to 360 mg/L, thus, concentrations above the natural
background concentration are observed. It should be noted, that chloride has not been analysed at
well 114.2508 and mlp-10 and in the samples (SB1-SB8) from the streambed.
In this thesis, chloride is not considered a suitable tracer due to the existence of several sources to
chloride in the Grindsted stream area. As stated in Sonne et al. (2017), various larger roads cross or run
close to the study area, thus, road salting (NaCl) for deicing purposes is a potential source to chloride
(Cooper et al., 2014). Chloride is also generated by the degradation of chlorinated ethenes which have
been conﬁrmed to occur at the site (Ottosen, 2017), however, the impact from this source is considered
to be limited. In addition, chloride can occur at elevated concentrations due to geogenic contamination
including natural weathering of bedrocks, natural brines, saline intrusions and atmospheric deposition
(Mullaney et al., 2009; Panno et al., 2006).
Trace metals
Table 4.2 presents the natural levels of the selected trace metals in Danish groundwater and their
detected concentration levels in the study area in Grindsted. No background concentration of chrome
in Danish groundwater was found.

the background levels, thus, they are not deemed suitable as inherent tracers in this thesis.
Evaluation of the applicability of inherent tracers
In summary, assessment of bromide may be a useful tool to diﬀerentiate between in situ biodegradation
and other attenuation processes in the work linked to this thesis. The relationship between detection of
bromide and pharmaceutical compounds will be assessed in Chapter 7. Apart from bromide, benzene
will also be assessed as a tracer as limited degradation of benzene has been observed at the study site
(Frederiksen et al., 2018).

==PAGE:41==
CHAPTER 4. EVALUATION OF TOOLS FOR IN-SITU DEGRADATION ASSESSMENT
4.3
Geochemical approaches
Geochemical parameters including redox conditions can be used for understanding and assessing the
subsurface microbial processes at the site (Bombach et al., 2010). When organic compounds such
as pharmaceuticals leak into an aquifer, the geochemistry of the aﬀected groundwater changes as a
result of utilization of naturally occurring electron acceptors by microorganisms during metabolism
of the organic contaminants (Bombach et al., 2010). Thus, documenting the consumption of the
electron acceptors (O2, NO3-, Mn4+, Fe3+, SO42-, and CO2) and the generation of their associated
reduced redox compounds (NO2-, NH4+, Mn2+, Fe2+, S2-, and CH4) along the contaminant plume
gives valuable information about the governing redox processes in the subsurface (Alvarez and Illman,
2005; Bombach et al., 2010). There is a diﬀerence in energy yield of the diﬀerent redox reactions.
The sequence of preference is presented in Table 4.3. It should be noted that nitrogen has several
oxidation states including NO3- (+V), NO2- (+III), N2 (0) and NH4+ (-III).
Table 4.3: Sequence of preference of microbial redox processes
The process of microbial degradation is expected to start with the most easily biodegradable organic
compounds such as hydrocarbons including BTEX compounds (Balbarini et al., 2020; Essaid et al.,
2003).
As the contamination at Grindsted consists of a mixture of chlorinated ethenes, BTEX
compounds and various pharmaceutical compounds having diﬀerent inclinations for biodegradation,
it is not possible to provide direct evidence for in-situ microbial degradation of pharmaceutical
compounds based on redox species (Bombach et al., 2010; Rønde et al., 2017). In addition, the
electron donor/electron acceptor balances for the turnover of a speciﬁc compound or compound
group will be uncertain due to simultaneous occurrence of various degradation processes (Bombach
et al., 2010). However, documentation of the concentrations of electron acceptors and their reduced
redox species may provide useful information on their current bioavailability. Also, it may provide
information about favourable redox conditions for pharmaceutical degradation.
A number of the listed redox-sensitive compounds have recently been measured at all the investigated
locations by Rambøll (2020) and Ottosen et al. (2020). These measurements have been performed
concurrent with the investigations for pharmaceutical compounds and are considered suﬃcient to
assess the redox conditions in the plume from the Grindsted factory site.
Evaluation of the applicability of knowledge on redox conditions
In summary, assessment of the redox species is not considered to be a useful tool to assess the
degradation of pharmaceutical compounds at Grindsted stream area. Yet, selected redox parameters
will be outlined in Chapter 7 to document the governing redox processes in the subsurface and to
investigate its connection with potential degradation products.
27
==PAGE:42==
CHAPTER 4. EVALUATION OF TOOLS FOR IN-SITU DEGRADATION ASSESSMENT
4.4
Compound-speciﬁc isotope analysis
Compound speciﬁc stable isotope analysis (CSIA) is another hydrochemical method that has the
potential to assess in-situ degradation. In CSIA, the changes in the stable isotope composition of the
parent contaminant are monitored during biotic and abiotic transformation processes, and the isotope
enrichment of the compound is considered to be evidence for its degradation (Birkigt et al., 2015a;
Elsner, 2010; Elsner et al., 2012).
Many organic contaminants including pharmaceuticals mainly consist of carbon and hydrogen where
both elements exhibit two stable isotopes (Bombach et al., 2010). The isotope enrichment occur as
isotopically lighter (e.g 12C, 1H) molecules are preferably degraded, thus, the residual contaminant
fraction becomes isotopicallyenriched withthe heavierisotope (13C, 2H)during degradation(Bombach
et al., 2010; Broholm et al., 2009; Hunkeler et al., 2008). The isotopic fractionation can be quantiﬁed
as 훿sample (‰), where the stable isotope ratio of the compound (R) is related to a standard (Rst) by
Equation 4.1.
훿sample = ( 푅
푅st
−1) · 1000
(4.1)
The 훿is speciﬁed with the least abundant isotope (eg. 훿13C or 훿2H). Changes in the isotope ratios
of contaminants result almost entirely from biodegradation processes and, if at all, only marginally
from physical processes like sorption, dissolution, dispersion and volatilization (Harrington et al.,
1999; Huang et al., 1999; Meckenstock et al., 2004; Slater et al., 2000). Sorption-associated isotopic
shifts have only been observed at the front of expanding plumes (Kopinke et al., 2005; Van Breukelen,
2007). Thus, changes in isotope ratios are considered reliable indicators of in-situ biodegradation.
The stable isotope fractionation can be quantitatively described by the Rayleigh equation given as
Equation 4.2. The Rt is the isotopic ratio at time t and R0 is the initial isotop value, while f is the
fraction of substrate remaining (C/C0) and 훼is the isotope fractionation factor (Elsner, 2010; Elsner
and Imfeld, 2016; Hunkeler et al., 2008).
