The fractionation factor can be used to express the enrichment factor (휖), which is 휖= (훼-1). Both the
isotope fractionation factor and enrichment factor can be determined under representative conditions
in laboratory experiments using the linear regression expressed in Equation 4.3 (Elsner, 2010; Elsner
and Imfeld, 2016). The enrichment factor reﬂects the extent of isotope fractionation per increment of
transformation (Elsner et al., 2012).
ln( 푅t
푅0
) = (훼−1) · ln( 푓) = 휖· ln( 푓)
(4.3)
A detailed guide about the method of CSIA and assessment of biodegradation using stable isotopes is
provided by Hunkeler et al. (2008).
Previous CSIA investigations of pharmaceuticals
In a study by Elsner and Imfeld (2016), CSIA was concluded to have great potential to evaluate
degradation of micropollutants including pharmaceutical compounds in the environment.
28
==PAGE:43==
CHAPTER 4. EVALUATION OF TOOLS FOR IN-SITU DEGRADATION ASSESSMENT
In the study of biodegradation of sulfanilamide in contaminated groundwater by Bennett et al. (2017),
the enrichment in 13C was determined to 8.44‰ from the contaminant source to 300 m downstream of
the source. In another study by Birkigt et al. (2015a), carbon isotope fractionation of sulfamethoxazole
during biodegradation by Microbacterium sp. strain BR1 and upon direct photolysis was investigated.
The carbon isotope enrichment factors for sulfamethoxazole (휖C) were determined to -0.6 ± 0.1‰ for
biodegradation and -2.0 ± 0.1‰ or -3.0 ± 0.2‰ for direct photolysis, depending on the pH (Birkigt et al.,
2015a). The enrichment factor for biodegradation of sulfamethoxazole is in the low end compared to
enrichment factors for degradation of typical groundwater contaminant including chlorinated ethenes
which are between -5‰ and -30‰ for anaerobic dehalogenation reactions (Meckenstock et al., 2004).
Other studies, including Maier et al. (2014) and Schürner et al. (2016), used the carbon and nitrogen
isotope ratios of the pharmaceutical, diclofenac, to successfully assess its degradation. These studies
also illustrated the advantages of investigating isotope changes in multiple elements (Maier et al.,
2014; Schürner et al., 2016).
Although only sulfanilamide is among the identiﬁed key pharmaceuticals, it suggests that CSIA
may be applicable for other sulfonamides considered in this thesis. Structurally, sulfanilamide is
the simplest key sulfonamide and has several similarities with the remaining key sulfonamides. In
addition. sulfamethoxazole (see molecular structure in Figure A.1 in Appendix A) has similarities
with the heterocyclic key sulfonamides, containing an aniline moiety and a heterocyclic moiety.
Especially, the aniline moiety of both sulfamethoxazole and sulfanilamide is expected to degrade
similarly for all sulfonamides while the heterocyclic moiety of sulfamethoxazole is expected to have a
more compound-speciﬁc degradation pathway.
No literature concerning CSIA investigations of any of the remaining identiﬁed key compounds was
found.
Applicability and limitations of CSIA
Although CSIA has become an established tool to evaluate degradation of organic contaminants and
its application to micropollutants including pharmaceuticals is emerging, there are several limitations
of the CSIA method which need to be considered when evaluating the applicability of the method in
this project (Elsner and Imfeld, 2016).
One of the limitations of CSIA is that the method can not be applied if only a small proportion of a
contaminant has been degraded and/or if the reaction mechanism of the biodegradation only results
in a minor isotope fractionation (Hunkeler et al., 2008). As outlined in Chapter 3, none or only
limited information about the biodegradation of the identiﬁed key pharmaceuticals in groundwater
exists. Thus, the extent of degradation of pharmaceuticals at the site is unknown. In addition, studies
have shown that the process of hydroxylation of other compounds including toluene yields only minor
isotope fractionations, thus, it may also be the case for the hydroxylation reactions of pharmaceuticals
(more information about the reactions in Chapter 3) (Vogt et al., 2008).
Another limitation is that CSIA using carbon isotopes is more eﬀective to provide evidence for in
situ biodegradation for small molecules than for larger molecules as larger molecules undergo smaller
shifts in stable isotope ratios during biodegradation because the reactive atom is only one out of many
other atoms (Bombach et al., 2010; Hunkeler et al., 2008). In this case, multi-element isotope analysis
can be beneﬁcial as pharmaceutical compounds often contain additional elements in their structure.
Other available stable isotopes include nitrogen (15N/14N), oxygen (18O/16O), sulphur (34S/32S) and
chlorine (37Cl/35Cl). For the key pharmaceutical compounds in the study, it is considered necessary to
conduct a multi-element isotope analysis if the method of CSIA is applied. It is suggested to include
carbon and nitrogen in the analysis. It should be noted, that nitrogen isotope analysis requires larger
sample amounts and is less precise compared to using carbon isotopes (Schürner et al., 2016; Sessions,
29
==PAGE:44==
CHAPTER 4. EVALUATION OF TOOLS FOR IN-SITU DEGRADATION ASSESSMENT
2006). It might be helpful to include hydrogen isotope analysis as this process is more sensitive to
biodegradation reactions (Bombach et al., 2010).
Another important aspect is that the contamination sources can have diﬀerent isotopic signatures
(Hunkeler et al., 2008). This is very likely at Grindsted where the contamination is assumed to consist
of several sources and result from diﬀerent spill events over time yet from the same type of production.
According to Hunkeler et al. (2008), multielement isotope analysis also provides an opportunity to
circumvent this problem. During the CSIA method, it is possible to separate organic analytes from
complex mixtures and to determine their individual stable isotope ratios, thus, it is not considered a
limitation that the contamination from Grindsted factory site consists of many diﬀerent substances
(Elsner et al., 2012). Finally, the polarity of micropollutants can be a major analytical challenge as it
requires a selective sorbent phase before separation by gas chromatography (Elsner and Imfeld, 2016;
Hunkeler et al., 2008).
4.5
Speciﬁc degraders and functional genes analysis
Knowledge about the biodegradation potential of an aquifer can be obtained by analysis for the
speciﬁc degraders and functional genes capable of biodegradation (Bombach et al., 2010).
An
essential prerequisite for application of this approach is knowledge of the degradation pathway of the
contaminant and the associated genetics, which are limited for pharmaceuticals (Balbarini et al., 2020;
Bombach et al., 2010; Bottoni et al., 2010; D˛ebska et al., 2004). Another considerable challenge of
the functional gene approach is the design of ideally one primer set capturing the natural sequence
diversity of the target gene (Bombach et al., 2010).
Table 3.2 and 3.4 in Chapter 3 provide overviews of the degraders of barbiturates and sulfonamides
described in literature, respectively.
The speciﬁc strain is also listed for some of the degraders.
Concerning functional genes, the favinmonooxygenases (SadA and SadB) encoding genes and the
FMN reductase (SadC) encoding gene were found to be sulfonamide catabolic genes by Ricken et al.
(2017). These genes are present in several of the sulfonamides degraders (see Section 3.2.2). No
speciﬁc degraders or functional genes involved in the biodegradation of ethyl urethane, meprobamate
and N-N-diethylnicotinamide were identiﬁed.
Next generation sequencing (NGS)
One method to assess the presence of speciﬁc degraders is next generation sequencing (NGS). NGS
refers to a group of technologies which can perform and capture data from millions of sequencing
reactions simultaneously – also called massively parallel sequencing (Illumina, 2020; Ilyas, 2018;
Kulski, 2016). NGS has several advantages including the ability to sequence multiple targets in one
reaction as opposed to the “one target per reaction” limitation of standard sequencing technology
(Kulski, 2016). Figure 4.2 illustrates the diﬀerence between NGS and standard sequencing.
30
==PAGE:45==
CHAPTER 4. EVALUATION OF TOOLS FOR IN-SITU DEGRADATION ASSESSMENT
Figure 4.2: The diﬀerence in number of targets sequenced in one reaction between NGS and the
standard sequencing technology (Ilyas, 2018).
Typically, the NGS workﬂow comprises three steps; library construction, sequencing reaction, and data
analysis. First, a library containing the DNA samples of interest is prepared. The sequencing libraries
are typically created by fragmenting DNA with specialized adapters added (Illumina, 2020; Kulski,
2016). The next step is the sequencing step where the library is loaded onto a ﬂow cell and placed
on the sequencer. The clusters of DNA fragments are ampliﬁed in a process called cluster generation
which results in millions of copies of single-stranded DNA (Illumina, 2020). The following sequencing
process depends on the technology but may include binding of chemically modiﬁed nucleotides with
a ﬂuorescent tag to the DNA template strand (Illumina technology) or release of H+ due to addition
of a dNTP (ion torrent technology) (Slatko et al., 2018). Lastly, the data is analysed and interpreted.
In this thesis, NGS will be used to assess whether the identiﬁed degraders of sulfonamides are present
and whether there is a correlation between the degraders present and the composition of pharmaceutical
compounds.
4.6
Supporting ﬁeld and lab experiments
4.6.1
Laboratory microcosms
The occurrence of a particular degradation process at a ﬁeld site can be demonstrated using laboratory
microcosms based on site material (Caracciolo et al., 2013; Bombach et al., 2010). The microcosms
are constructed using either groundwater or aquifer material from the ﬁeld site as inoculum and the
relevant contaminants are added (Caracciolo et al., 2013). The degradation is assessed as decreasing
contaminant concentrations. The advantages of microcosm studies include distinguishing between bi-
otic and abiotic chemical reactions, studying single biochemical processes and evaluation of the impact
of electron acceptors or donors on biodegradation rates (Bombach et al., 2010). The major limitations
of laboratory microcosms are that the collected samples used for constructing the microcosms may
not be representative of the entire aquifer, that complex interactions occurring in the environment are
simpliﬁed, and the composition and activity of the microbial community may change during sampling
31
==PAGE:46==
CHAPTER 4. EVALUATION OF TOOLS FOR IN-SITU DEGRADATION ASSESSMENT
and incubation. Thus, transferring the results from a laboratory-obtained degradation proﬁle to ﬁeld
studies is highly limited (Bombach et al., 2010).
In 1999, laboratory microcosms experiments investigating the fate of some xenobiotic compound
were performed in the anaerobic leachate plume downgradient of the Grindsted Landﬁll (Bjerg et al.,
1999). None of the involved xenobiotics was pharmaceutical compounds but a similar experiment
could be performed with selected pharmaceuticals and aquifer material from the study site. In studies
by Caracciolo et al. (2013) and Grenni et al. (2012), the potentials for microbial degradation of selected
pharmaceutical compounds were successfully studied by laboratory microcosms. None of the key
compounds in this thesis was part of the investigated pharmaceuticals, however, it supports that this
tool can be used to address the microbial degradation of pharmaceuticals (Caracciolo et al., 2013;
Bombach et al., 2010; Grenni et al., 2012).
The laboratory microcosms can also be suitable for demonstrating speciﬁc contaminant degraders of a
contaminant or contaminant group of interest by cultivation and identiﬁcation of the microbes present
when the relevant contaminants are added to the microcosms (Bombach et al., 2010).
4.6.2
In-situ microcosms
In-situ microcosms studies provide knowledge about the degradation activity of microorganisms
directly in the ﬁeld (Bombach et al., 2010; Nielsen et al., 1996). Thus, the issues associated with
laboratory microcosms such as the cultivation of microorganisms and controlling environmental
variables (e.g., temperature, light, humidity and so on) are minimized (Bombach et al., 2010). Several
types of in-situ microcosms exist including enclosed cylinders in the aquifer loaded with the relevant
compounds (eg.
Gillham et al. (1990) and Nielsen et al. (1996)) and enclosed tubes ﬁlled with
activated carbon pellets which get loaded with 13C-labeled substrates (eg. Kaestner and Richnow
(2010) and Peacock et al. (2004)). Similar to the laboratory microcosms, this approach estimates the
potential for degradation rather than actual degradation rates, and the extrapolation of the results to an
entire aquifer is complicated (Bombach et al., 2010).
In-situ microcosm experiments with enclosed cylinders loaded with xenobiotic compounds were
performed in 1999 by Bjerg et al. (1999) to investigate the fate of the compounds in the leachate from
Grindsted Landﬁll. A similar experiment could be performed in the plume of pharmaceuticals from
Grindsted factory site. In a study by Lhotský et al. (2017), the in situ microcosms approach was found
suitable for monitoring remediation in a pilot remediation test of a plume containing pharmaceuticals
along with BTEXs and chlorinated pollutants.
None of the identiﬁed key pharmaceuticals was
included in the study, however, it suggests that in situ microcosm experiments are applicable to plumes
containing pharmaceuticals.
A similar approach is the push-pull experiment which consists of a controlled injection of a prepared
test solution into the aquifer followed by extraction of the solution from the same location. The
degradation rates are determined as the mass of reactant consumed and/or product formed as a
function of time (Haggerty et al., 1998; Istok et al., 1997).
4.6.3
Injection of tracer
If no suitable, inherent tracer associated with the contaminant source is found, tracer injection of
a suitable compound can be performed (Bombach et al., 2010; Reitzel, 2005). Similar to inherent
tracers, some general properties of tracers for injection exist including conservative migration and low
natural background concentration. Besides, the tracer needs to be available, aﬀordable and nontoxic
32
==PAGE:47==
CHAPTER 4. EVALUATION OF TOOLS FOR IN-SITU DEGRADATION ASSESSMENT
(Shook et al., 2016). In addition, this type of tracer experiment typically involves both an injection
of a test solution containing a conservative tracer such as bromide or chloride and an injection of
the pollutant of interest as a reactive tracer (Ptak et al., 2004). However, extensive monitoring and
construction of groundwater monitoring wells are required in connection with the injection tests which
limit the application of tracer tests by injections (Bombach et al., 2010).
In 1999, a ﬁeld injection experiment with 18 xenobiotic compounds and bromide as tracers was
conducted in the leachate plume downgradient from the landﬁll in Grindsted (Rügge et al., 1999). None
of the involved xenobiotics was pharmaceutical compounds but a similar experiment with selected
pharmaceuticals could be performed. In another study by Haack et al. (2012), sulfamethoxazole was
injected together with the conservative tracer sodium bromide (used as a source of the bromide ion Br-),
and the concentration histories for sulfamethoxazole closely tracked those for bromide. Therefore,
sulfamethoxazole undergoes subsurface transport similar to conservative, nonreactive tracers, which
may also be the case for some of the key pharmaceuticals in this thesis.
4.6.4
Use of enantiomeric ratios
Several contaminants including pharmaceuticals are chiral and exist in pairs of enantiomers. Even
though enantiomers typically have identical physical properties, enantioselective degradation has been
observed for several chiral compounds. Thus, a change in enantiomeric composition along a ﬂow line
can serve as an in-situ indicator of degradation. The method is outlined in more details in Reitzel
(2005).
The tool was applied in a ﬁeld-injection experiment by Rügge et al. (2002). Measurements of the
(R)- and (S)-enantiomers of the herbicides meta-Chlorophenylpiperazine (mCPP) and dichlorprop
were conducted to evaluate whether enantioselective degradation of these herbicides occurred in
groundwater under ﬁeld conditions. The study found that no enantioselective degradation of the
compounds occurred but that the preferential degradation of enantiomers of phenoxy herbicides is not
fully understood (Rügge et al., 2002).
Among the pharmaceutical compounds identiﬁed as key compounds in this thesis, only pentobarbital
contains a chiral carbon atom. Thus, the use of enantiomeric ratios is not considered a useful tool to
assess the degradation of pharmaceutical compounds in the Grindsted stream area. Besides, it is not
known if the degradation of pentobarbital is enantioselective and supporting microcosms experiments
would be needed to estimate the enantioselectivity.
4.7
Summary of tools for in-situ degradation assessment
Table 4.4 outlines the arguments for and against the application of each tool for assessment of in-
situ degradation of pharmaceutical compounds in this thesis. In conclusion, several chemical and
microbial tools exist to assess in situ degradation of contaminants including pharmaceuticals, each
with advantages and limitations, but no completely reliable tool exists. Therefore, to create a more
reliable and robust assessment of the degradation in the plume of pharmaceutical compound from
the factory, various tools will be combined. The tool combination consists of metabolite analysis,
characterisation of microbial degraders and usage of the inherent tracers, bromide and benzene. In
addition, the redox conditions at the site will be outlined, though not applicable as evidence for
degradation of pharmaceuticals.

The external testing services provider ALS Denmark was contacted regarding a metabolite screening as

The second step was to select the analytical method which should be further developed and imple-
mented in the laboratory. In this thesis, it was chosen to only apply LCMSMS positive and negative
electrospray as it is the method covering most metabolites and the metabolites of several sulfonamides
detected in high concentrations. It was chosen to include the parent compounds, sulfanilamide and
sulfadiazine, in the analysis to also determine their concentration levels. Note, that sulfanilamide and
sulfadiazine were also analysed by ALS Denmark (see Section 5.1.4).
37
==PAGE:52==
CHAPTER 5. INVESTIGATIVE APPROACH - METHODS AND ANALYSIS
The third step was to run preliminary tests using LCMSMS to determine whether it was required
to up-concentrate the samples by solid phase extraction. Sulfanilamide, sulfadiazine and benzene
sulfonamide were detected in some of the samples during the preliminary tests. Sulfonamide benzene
was the only metabolite detected and the concentrations were close to the detection limit, thus, it
was chosen to perform solid phase extractions. The last step was to run the actual analysis of the
up-concentrated samples (more details about the principle, chromatography, detection and settings
are provided in Appendix B.3). During the analysis, it was not possible to analyse hydroquinone
and p-phenylenediamine in all samples due to the high interference from the sample matrix. These
compounds elute very early and their ionizations are most likely aﬀected by the high content of
dissolved organic matter in the samples. Furthermore, some of the samples could not be analysed for
p-benzoquinone due to high interference from the sample matrix. All three metabolites have relatively
low molecular masses which makes them diﬃcult to detect. It may be possible to optimize the method
by using another column chemistry but it was not possible within the time frame of this project.
Samples used for the LCMSMS analysis were collected during the ﬁeld investigation in December
2020. Further details about the ﬁeld methods are provided Appendix B.
5.1.3
Microbial degraders and functional genes analysis
The analysis for microbial degraders was conducted by Orvion, which is an environmental biotechnol-
ogy company in the Netherlands, and consisted of scanning and studying the whole biodiversity of the
groundwater at the ﬁve selected sampling locations (see details about the sampling locations in Sub-
section 5.1.1). The analysis aimed to identify bacterial species, which correlate with the contaminants
present and are known degraders of pharmaceuticals, to show the natural degradation potential and
the possibilities for stimulation of the degradation. The analysis was targeted on bacterial degraders
of the key pharmaceuticals of this thesis but covered the entire biodiversity in the samples.
The actual analyses were conducted using NGS (see more details about NGS in Section 4.5). The
samples were processed with the QIAamp Powerfecal pro DNA kit from QIAGEN for DNA isolation
and prepped with the Oxford Nanopore Technology SQK-LSK109 kit before the actual sequencing
was performed. When the sequencing was complete, the raw data was base-called and quantiﬁed
via bioinformatic DNA sequencing software analysis (see more information about the kits and the
procedures in Section B.3.2 in Appendix B). All samples were successfully sequenced except the
reference sample from screen F4 of well site GÅ3 where the concentration of DNA was too low for
analysis.
After sequencing, the raw data were base called and the results are compared to a database to identify
the microbes.
The results were provided at genus level in an interactive ﬁle created by Orvion.
Note, that the applied NGS technique gave results on relative abundance, meaning the abundance of
each identiﬁed bacterium was given relative to the total identiﬁed bacteria in the sample. Thus, no
quantiﬁcation of bacteria in cells/mL of the samples was conducted.
The samples for analysis were collected during the ﬁeld investigation in December 2020 and were
obtained using the sampling material provided by Orvion. Samples intended for analysis were also
collected in November 2020 but the DNA isolated from the ﬁltered groundwater from all samples
were too low to complete the NGS protocol and analysis.
No analysis for functional genes capable of biodegradation of pharmaceutical compounds was con-
ducted in this thesis, thus, it will not be further discussed.
38
==PAGE:53==
CHAPTER 5. INVESTIGATIVE APPROACH - METHODS AND ANALYSIS
5.1.4
Chemical data from November 2020
As a part of the application of degradation assessment tools, samples from the ﬁve selected sampling
locations (FG1-F5, 114.1147-F2, 114.2508, GÅ1-F4 and GÅ3-F4) were analysed by ALS Denmark
for various chemical parameters to have data which is contemporary with the metabolite analysis
and characterisation of degraders.
The chemical parameters included pharmaceuticals, anilines,
chlorinated solvents and degradation products and hydrocarbons (including BTEX). Details about the
collection of samples are provided in Appendix B.
As part of the analyses, selected redox parameters were included; ammonium, manganese, iron, nitrite,
nitrate, sulfate, hydrogen sulﬁde and methane. The concentration of oxygen was measured manually
by a dissolved oxygen meter in a ﬂow cell in the ﬁeld in December 2020 (see results in Appendix C).
In addition, bromide was included in the analyses but with a considerable higher detection limit of
0.50 mg/L due to the choice of external analysis provider compared to previous investigations where
the detection limit of bromide was 0.01 mg/L.
5.2
Acquisition of additional hydraulic data - hydraulic heads
and conductivities
Hydraulic heads in the screens at the seven well sites (FG1-FG3, FA1 and GÅ1-GÅ3) have been
measured in March 2020 by Rambøll (2020), while additional manual measurements of hydraulic
heads at wells FG1, FG2, FG3, FA1, 114.1447, 114.2508, GÅ1 and GÅ3 were performed during the
ﬁeld investigations in October and December 2020. The method behind the latter measurements is
outlined in Appendix B.
Horizontal hydraulic conductivity values for the Grindsted aquifer near the factory site (based on well
sites FG1, FG2 and FG3) and centrally in the plume (based on well site FA1) were estimated from
slug tests performed in 12 screens in October 2020. The data were collected in collaboration with
Haugsted and Lade (2020) while the later processing of the data was as a part of this thesis. Data
collection and processing are described in Appendix B.
5.3
Correlation matrices
Correlation matrices were used in this project to assess the correlation between various variables.
A correlation matrix is a (K × K) square and symmetrical matrix whose ĳ entry is the correlation
between the columns i and j of X (Brown et al., 2010; Herkenhoﬀand Fogli, 2013). Large values
in the matrix indicate high collinearity between the variables involved while low values indicate low
collinearity (Brown et al., 2010). Collinearity refers to the condition in which two or more predictors
can express a relationship in a regression model.
The correlation was determined using two correlation coeﬃcients; the Pearson correlation coeﬃcient
(rP) and the Spearman rank correlation coeﬃcient (rS). The fundamental diﬀerence between the
coeﬃcients is that the Pearson coeﬃcient is a measure for the linear correlation between two variables
whereas the Spearman coeﬃcient works with monotonic relationships between the variables (Russell
and Cohn, 2012). Both correlation coeﬃcients range from -1 to +1, where ±1 indicates perfect agree-
ment or disagreement and 0 indicates no relationship (Herkenhoﬀand Fogli, 2013). The Spearman
correlation was used in the majority of the assessments in this thesis, while the Pearson correlation was
only applied a few times when a linear relationship was expected. When constructing the Spearman
39
==PAGE:54==
CHAPTER 5. INVESTIGATIVE APPROACH - METHODS AND ANALYSIS
rank correlation matrices, all concentrations below the detection limit were set to half the detection
limit.
A guiding list about interpretation of strengths of relationships based on the Pearson’s Correlation
coeﬃcients is given in Yule (1912). This list is represented in Table 5.4 and is also applicable for the
Spearman coeﬃcient. Note, that these are only suggestions for interpreting strengths of relationships.
Table 5.4: Guiding list for interpretation of the strengths of relationships between two variables
based on the Pearson Correlation coeﬃcient (Yule, 1912).
Pearson’s Correlation coeﬃcient (rP)
Interpretation
+.70 or higher
Very strong positive relationship
+.40 to +.69
Strong positive relationship
+.30 to +.39
Moderate positive relationship
+.20 to +.29
weak positive relationship
+.01 to +.19
No or negligible relationship
0
No relationship
-.01 to -.19
No or negligible relationship
-.20 to -.29
weak negative relationship
-.30 to -.39
Moderate negative relationship
-.40 to -.69
Strong negative relationship
-.70 or higher
Very strong negative relationship
The correlation coeﬃcients were also compared to a critical value to assess whether the observed
correlations are statistically signiﬁcant. The critical values of the Pearson correlation coeﬃcient and
the Spearman rank correlation coeﬃcient are outlined in Figure D.4 and Figure D.5 in Appendix D,
respectively. The critical value depends on the number of data points, type of hypothesis (1-tailed
(directional) or 2-tailed (non-directional)) and the value of signiﬁcance (p) considered. In this project,
the tests are one-tailed and considered at a level of signiﬁcance of 0.01 while the number of data points
varied among the diﬀerent assessments.

==PAGE:55==
Chapter 6
Characterisation of contaminant distribution,
hydrogeology and redox conditions
This chapter provides a site characterisation including assessment of site hydrogeological data, redox
conditions and a detailed review of the current distribution and composition of pharmaceutical
compounds in the plume from the Grindsted factory site. The chapter is concluded with a linkage of
the distribution of pharmaceutical contaminants to the site hydrogeology and redox conditions. This
chapter is based on existing and newly acquired data.
6.1
Hydrogeology
6.1.1
Hydraulic heads and vertical gradients
The vertical distribution of hydraulic heads is presented in Figure 6.1 at the three well sites located
close to the factory site (FG1-FG3), at FA1 and 114.1447 placed centrally in the plume, and at the
three wells sites located along Grindsted stream in the western direction (GÅ1-GÅ3). The temporal
changes in groundwater heads are also illustrated in Figure 6.1 as selected well sites were measured at
multiple ﬁeld investigations. The hydraulic head in well 114.2508 was measured to 34.5-m asl but is
not included in the ﬁgure. The speciﬁc water levels of the wells are outlined in Table I.1 in Appendix
I.
At the well sites close to the factory site, the vertical hydraulic gradient is slightly downward between
the screens, of which the downward gradient is more distinct at FG2 and FG3 compared to FG1, The
small diﬀerences in groundwater head are likely caused by minor, discontinuous clay and lignite layers,
thus, the meltwater sand and the Miocene sand formation are yet considered to be well connected
at these locations.
Towards the stream at well 114.1447, a very weak upward vertical hydraulic
gradient is observed which suggests that the diﬀerent geological formations are also well connected
at this location. At FA1, which is placed centrally between the factory site and Grindsted stream, a
slightly upward vertical hydraulic gradient is observed. The hydraulic head in the meltwater sand is
0.22-0.28-m lower compared to the hydraulic head in the Miocene formation below several clay layers
at 1.4-5.9-m asl, which suggests that the two layers are hydraulically disconnected to a certain extent.
At the wells along Grindsted stream, the hydraulic heads are notable higher below the lignite/clay
layers at 18-20.2-m asl at GÅ1, 16.6-17.6-m asl at GÅ2 and 12.6-14.6-m asl at GÅ3. It suggests
that clay and lignite layers embedded in the Miocene sand are a hydraulic barrier for groundwater
ﬂow near the stream. Based on the measurements from December 2020, a slightly upward vertical
hydraulic gradient is observed between the screens (F1-F5) below the lignite layer at GÅ1, while at
41
==PAGE:56==
CHAPTER 6. CHARACTERISATION OF CONTAMINANT DISTRIBUTION,
HYDROGEOLOGY AND REDOX CONDITIONS
GÅ3 a notable diﬀerence in hydraulic heads of 0.19-0.35-m asl is observed between the two deepest
screens (F1 and F2) and the three screens above (F3, F4 and F5) in the Miocene sand. It suggests, that
the clay layers embedded in this part of the Miocene formation may also act as a hydraulic barrier for
groundwater ﬂow.
Figure 6.1: Vertical distribution of hydraulic heads at the three well sites located close to the factory
site, at FA1 and 114.1447 placed centrally in the plume, and at the three wells sites located along
Grindsted stream in the western direction. The lithological logs from Rambøll (2020) are included
for all wells except 114.1447.
It is important to mention, that the ﬁve deepest screens at well sites GÅ1, GÅ2 and GÅ3 were artesian
at the ﬁeld investigation in Marts 2020 by Rambøll (2020). The majority of the screens had overﬂow,
thus, the heads were assumed to be at ground level. It is acknowledged that this assumption masks
and minimises the potential diﬀerences between hydraulic heads along the depth. It would have been
ideal to extend the well pipes and measure the actual heads in the screens. At the ﬁeld investigation
December 2020, overﬂow was not observed at GÅ1 and GÅ3 while GÅ2 was not investigated.
In general, the trends in the hydraulic heads observed in this thesis are consistent with the trends
described in Balbarini et al. (2020).

==PAGE:57==

HYDROGEOLOGY AND REDOX CONDITIONS

The derived K-values were consistently in the range 10-5 to 10-3 m/s. In the ﬁeld investigation of the
landﬁll site south of Grindsted stream in 1995, the geometric mean of the hydraulic conductivity in
the Miocene sand deposits was found to be 0.9E-4 m/s (Bjerg et al., 1995). Thus, the derived K-values
are in good accordance with former investigations near the study site. The derived K-values are also
consistent with the typical hydraulic conductivity values for various sedimentary materials outlined
in Domenico and Schwartz (1990).
Figure 6.2A displays the derived K-values (red triangles) as a function of elevation of screens provided
in metres above sea level (m asl). The horizontal hydraulic conductivities outlined in the supporting
information of Balbarini et al. (2020) (black triangles) were also included in Figure 6.2 in order to
cover the upper Quarternary sand deposits which were not assessed in the slug tests conducted in this
project. With a few exceptions, the hydraulic conductivity was observed to be highest in the midmost
part of the Miocene deposits but additional slug tests in the deeper wells are recommended to support
this observation.
6.1.3
Flow, transport and pharmaceuticals
Estimation of groundwater ﬂow velocity
The groundwater ﬂow velocity in the Miocene deposits from the factory site to Grindsted stream
was estimated by Darcy’s Law using the geometric mean of the estimated K-values (Table 6.1), and
a average horizontal gradient of 0.035‰. As mentioned in 6.1.2, the hydraulic conductivity was
observed to be higher in the midmost part of the Miocene deposits, thus, the investigated part of the
aquifer was divided into three zones; +22 to +10 m asl, +10 to -10 m asl and -10 to -26.5 m asl.
Table 6.2 presents the estimated groundwater velocities as well as the applied K-values (Kgeo) and
associated variance in ln(K). According to Bohling et al. (2012), 휎2ln(K) < 1 indicates mild hetero-
geneity in the aquifer while 휎2ln(K) > 2 suggests moderate to high heterogeneity. The determined
variances suggest that the deepest zone is most heterogeneous and a mean of the K-values may not be
appropriate.

CHAPTER 6. CHARACTERISATION OF CONTAMINANT DISTRIBUTION,
HYDROGEOLOGY AND REDOX CONDITIONS

means of the K-values and the associated variances in ln(K).

The distance from the factory site to the main contaminant discharge area at Grindsted stream was set
to 1,150-m while a mean porosity of 0.37 was selected as representative for the aquifer based on Rügge
et al. (1999). Hence, it is expected that the non-degradable pharmaceuticals present at the factory site
will reach the stream after around 30-50 years given the estimated ﬂow velocities and the properties
of the pharmaceuticals. It is important to highlight that these estimated values of ﬂow velocities are
highly sensitive to changes in the hydraulic conductivity in the aquifers. The estimated groundwater
ﬂows were within the same order of magnitude as the simulated groundwater ﬂow velocities presented
in Balbarini et al. (2020) which at average were 32 m/year in the semi-conﬁned aquifer.
Connection between hydraulic conductivities and concentrations of pharmaceuticals
The connection between the derived K-values and the total concentrations of pharmaceuticals in the
investigated screens was assessed. Higher concentrations may be observed in geological layers with
lower hydraulic conductivities compared to layers with high K-values due to a lower pore water velocity
in these layers. Figure 6.2B presents measured concentrations of pharmaceuticals prior to this project
as a function of elevation of screens. By comparison with Figure 6.2A, no clear connection between
low hydraulic conductivity and high concentration of pharmaceuticals exists. However, the majority
of the highest total concentrations of pharmaceuticals were detected between 20 and 30-m asl where
the lowest hydraulic conductivities are also observed.
Figure 6.2: (A) Hydraulic conductivity as a function of elevation of screens. The K-values has been
derived from slug test performed as part of this project (red triangles) and by Balbarini et al. (2020)
(black triangles). (B) The sum of pharmaceuticals, total barbiturates and total sulfonamides as a
function of elevation of screens.

==PAGE:59==
CHAPTER 6. CHARACTERISATION OF CONTAMINANT DISTRIBUTION,
HYDROGEOLOGY AND REDOX CONDITIONS
6.2
Current distribution and composition of the plume
The current distribution and composition of pharmaceutical compounds were assessed based on data
from water samples collected from year 2015 to 2020 at the locations shown in Section 2.3, Figure 2.4.
In total, 46 individual pharmaceutical compounds were included in the analysis, however, each sample
was not analysed for all compounds. The speciﬁc compounds in each analysis and the associated
concentrations can be found in the electronic Appendix E1 (see Appendix E).
In the following sections, the terms total barbiturates and total sulfonamides represent the sum of
19 individual barbiturates and 12 individual sulfonamides, respectively. Summarised concentrations
are applied as the compounds in each group are approximately similar in structure, properties and
degradation pathways. A list of the individual barbiturates and sulfonamides is presented in Table
F.1 in Appendix F. Note, that sulfanilic acid has been assessed as an individual compound and is
not included in total sulfonamides as it was found in high concentrations at the site and is a typical
degradation product of the remaining sulfonamides. In addition, it was assumed that the concentration
of sulfonylurea (class of organic compounds) is equal to the concentration of sulfacarbamide which is
the most simple sulfonylurea.
Figure 6.3 displays the distribution of total barbiturates, total sulfonamides, sulfanilic acid, ethyl
urethane, meprobamate and N-N-diethylnicotinamide at each of the locations included in this study.
Figure 6.3: The distribution of total concentrations of barbiturates, sulfonamides, sulfanilic acid,
ethyl urethane, meprobamate and N-N-diethylnicotinamide at (A) the six well sites, well 114.1447
and well 114.2568, (B) well 114.2508 and well mlp-10, and (C) in the streambed.
Each pie chart in Figure 6.3A and 6.3B represents the share of each compound group based on the
summarised concentrations detected in the screens, independent on the distribution in each screen. In
Figure 6.3C, the pie chart includes all the water samples from the streambed (SB1-SB8). The sizes of
the charts relate to the average concentrations of pharmaceutical compounds at the locations, thus, the
45
==PAGE:60==
CHAPTER 6. CHARACTERISATION OF CONTAMINANT DISTRIBUTION,
HYDROGEOLOGY AND REDOX CONDITIONS
summarised concentration of pharmaceutical compounds have been averaged based on the number
of screens or samples at the location. It should be noted that the average concentrations of detected
pharmaceutical compounds are highly dependent on the depth of the wells as the deeper wells have
screens located below the primary contamination plume resulting in lower average concentrations
than the shorter wells. The distribution of pharmaceutical compounds over depth will be discussed in
Section 6.2.1, 6.2.4 and 6.2.5.
Based on previous studies of the plume of pharmaceutical compounds (etc. Frederiksen et al. (2018)),
the highest concentrations in the area close to the factory site were expected to be detected around well
site FG2, however, the highest concentrations were detected at the most southeastern well site FG1. The
contamination at well site FG1 and FG3 was dominated by barbiturates (75% and 89%, respectively)
while a high share of the contamination at FG2 consisted of sulfonamides (62%). The diﬀerences in
dominating compounds among the wells indicate that several contaminant sources exist at the factory
site. In well 114.1447, sulfonamides (76%) represented the major part while barbiturates (65%) were
dominating at well site FA1 but with notable shares of sulfonamides (16%) and ethyl urethane (13%).
Closer to the stream in well 114.2568, both sulfonamides (35%) and barbiturates (51%) constituted
large portions of the total contamination. Near the stream bank in well 114.2508, sulfonamides (86%)
were dominating while at the stream bank in well mlp-10, both sulfonamides (42%) and barbiturates
(42%) constituted large shares. Sulfonamides (79%) were dominating at the streambed in SB1-SB8
where the highest total concentration of pharmaceutical compounds also was detected.
The dominance of sulfonamides at well 114.2508, mlp-10 and in the investigated streambed (SB1-
SB8) suggests that a plume originating from the middle of the factory site (FG2) migrates through well
114.1447 and discharges in the main contaminant discharge area. This is supported by similarities
in pharmaceutical composition along this ﬂow line. It is suggested to support these interpretations
by statistical tools able to group the wells according to similarities in contaminant composition and
concentrations (see a possible approach in eg. Balbarini et al. (2020)).
The distribution of compounds appears to be almost similar among well site GÅ1, GÅ2 and GÅ3 along
Grindsted stream in the western direction. Barbiturates were dominating at these locations, yet the
share of sulfonamides (19%) in GÅ1 was also remarkable. The dominance of barbiturates along the
stream can both be a result of the initial composition at the factory or a result of a higher persistency of
barbiturates compared to sulfonamides (excluding sulfanilic acid), which is consistent with the current
literature within the ﬁeld of study. Sulfanilic acid was also found to constitute from 10% to 18% of the
total concentration of pharmaceutical compounds at these well sites, with the highest share at GÅ3.
It was not possible to determine whether GÅ2 and GÅ3 are located downstream of GÅ1 assuming
that the plume is extending towards the west along the stream or whether GÅ2 and GÅ3 are located
at the outer edge of the plume assuming that the wells sites are located across the plume. It was
beyond the scope of this study to assess the ﬂow direction of the plume in details. Yet, the detection of
pharmaceuticals at these well sites showed that the contaminant plume of pharmaceuticals ﬂows further
downgradient of well 114.1495 as proposed in Balbarini et al. (2020), with decreasing concentrations
towards the west.
In summary, the southeastern part of the plume (FG1) is dominated by barbiturates while the central
part of the plume from the factory site (FG2) towards the stream (114.1447, 114.2568, 114.2508
and mlp-10) and in the streambed (SB1-SB8) is dominated or have high shares of sulfonamides.
Towards west (FA1) and along the stream in the western direction (GÅ1-3), the plume is dominated
by barbiturates. As illustrated in Figure 6.3, the contamination levels are lower at the western well
sites of FG3 and GÅ3 than in the centrally placed wells. The low concentrations at well site GÅ3
may be a result of dilution, degradation or that the plume has not reached the location yet or has
46
==PAGE:61==
CHAPTER 6. CHARACTERISATION OF CONTAMINANT DISTRIBUTION,
HYDROGEOLOGY AND REDOX CONDITIONS
already disappeared. The diﬀerences in composition of the plume may be caused by various sources
of contamination at the factory site, but variations in the properties of the aquifer including geology,
hydrogeology and redox conditions can also result in spatial diﬀerences in the composition of the
plume.
The following sections will outline the distribution and composition of pharmaceutical compounds
at each location and in three transects in more details. A more comprehensive description of the
dominating compounds at the site is found in Appendix G.

At FG1, barbiturates were dominating over the entire depth except in screen F3, however, it should be
noted that the only compound detected in this screen was sulfadimidine (0.18 µg/L). The contamination
was mainly detected in the two screens (F5 and F6) closest to the surface.
In F6, the sum of
barbiturates was more than 2000 µg/L, mainly 5-butylbarbiturate and isopropyl barbituric acid, while
the concentration of sulfonamides (19 µg/L) represented various compounds. Both ethyl urethane and
meprobamate were found in concentrations above 10 µg/L in this screen. In the second screen from
the top, F5, the bulk of the contamination consisted of ethyl urethane (540 µg/L) followed by isopropyl
barbituric acid (190 µg/L), sulfadimidine (130 µg/L), 5-butylbarbiturate (130 µg/L) and allobarbital
(110 µg/L). Sulfanilic acid was only detected above the detection limit in screen F5 (18 µg/L) and
screen F6 (4.1 µg/L) at FG1.
47
==PAGE:62==
CHAPTER 6. CHARACTERISATION OF CONTAMINANT DISTRIBUTION,
HYDROGEOLOGY AND REDOX CONDITIONS
At the centrally placed well site, FG2, the contamination was mainly detected in the four upper screens
whereof screen F5 and F6 contained the highest concentrations. Yet, the level of pharmaceutical
compounds was only around a factor 5-10 lower compared to FG1. The contamination was dominated
be sulfonamides in the uppermost screen, mainly consisting of sulfadiazine (93 µg/L) and sulfathiazole
(100 µg/L), while allobarbital (130 µg/L) was dominating in screen F5. The distribution of compounds
in F4 and F3 appeared similar with sulfonamides as the dominating compound group. The two deepest
screens also have a similar distribution of compounds, where barbiturates, sulfonamides, meproba-
mate and N-N-diethylnicotinamide constituted almost equally to the contamination. In general, the
relative shares of N-N-diethylnicotinamide and meprobamate increased with depth but the highest
concentrations were detected in the midmost screens.
Figure 6.4: The distribution of total concentrations of barbiturates, sulfonamides, sulfanilic acid,
ethyl urethane, meprobamate and N-N-diethylnicotinamide in the screens at well sites FG1, FG2 and
FG3.
At the well site furthest to the northwest, FG3, barbiturates were dominating at all screens while
the share of sulfonamides varied.
The contamination was mainly detected in screen F6 closest
to the surface, where the bulk of the contamination consisted of allobarbital (190 µg/L), N-N-
diethylnicotinamide (19 µg/L) and barbital (10 µg/L). In general, N-N-diethylnicotinamide constituted
considerable portions in the upper four screens. Similar to well site FG2, sulfanilic acid was not
detected at FG3. The diﬀerences in dominating compounds between the individual screens at the well
sites support the presumption that several contaminant sources exist at the factory site. Furthermore,
48
==PAGE:63==

HYDROGEOLOGY AND REDOX CONDITIONS
no clear connection was found between the well sites concerning dominating compound groups at
speciﬁc depths which further supports the existence of several contaminant sources.
6.2.2
Well 114.1447, FA1 and 114.2568: Centrally placed in the plume
Well site FA1 and the wells 114.1447 and 144.2568 are considered to be centrally placed in the plume.
The total concentrations of barbiturates and sulfonamides in the investigated wells are presented
in Table 6.4 along with the concentrations of sulfanilic acid, ethyl urethane, meprobamate and N-
N-diethylnicotinamide. In well 114.1447, the highest concentrations were detected in the second
deepest screen, F2. The primary sulfonamide in the screen was sulfanilamide (460 µg/L) which
was also detected at a remarkable concentration (61 µg/L) in the deepest screen, F1. Barbiturates
and N-N-diethylnicotinamide were found in considerable concentrations in screen F2 and F4, while
meprobamate was mainly detected in F2. Sulfanilic acid was only present in low concentrations at the
well.
Closer to the stream at well site FA1, pharmaceutical compounds were detected at high concentrations
over the entire investigated depth. High levels of allobarbital were found both in the deep screens, F2
(330 µg/L) and F3 (140 µg/L), and closer to the surface in screen F5 (440 µg/L) and F6 (270 µg/L).
High concentrations of ethyl urethane were detected in screen F2 (120 µg/L) and F3 (110 µg/L), while
100 µg/L of sulfanilamide was found in F2. Sulfanilic acid was not detected at this well site.

6.2.4
Well GÅ1-GÅ3: Along Grindsted Stream
At the well sites along Grinsted Stream, the highest concentrations were expected to be detected in the
area around GÅ1 and decreasing in the wells further to the west and further away from the factory site.
The well site GÅ3 was considered to be located at the outer edge of the plume by Rambøll (2020).
The concentrations of selected compounds at the well sites are outlined in Table 6.7.
The highest concentrations were detected at GÅ1 which is the eastern well site. All screens contained
pharmaceutical compounds in notable concentrations. The dominating substances were allobarbital,
which was >100 µg/L in all screens except the deepest (F1), and sulfanilic acid (12-59 µg/L in F1-F6).
Meprobamate was detected in concentrations above 10 µg/L in F2-F6, while ethyl urethane was not
detected.
At the midmost well site GÅ2, the contamination level was low compared to GÅ1. All investigated
pharmaceutical compounds had a concentration below 10 µg/L except allobarbital and meprobamate.
The notable concentrations of allobarbital were detected in F2 (30 µg/L) and F5 (77 µg/L), while 17
µg/L meprobamate was detected in F5. At the well site furthest to the west GÅ3, the pharmaceutical
compounds were mainly detected in screen F2 and screen F6. In F2, the contamination consisted of
allobarbital, sulfanilic acid and meprobamate, while allobarbital was the primary compound in F6.
As expected, the contaminant concentration is decreasing in the western direction based on the results
from GÅ1, GÅ2 and GÅ3, however, the plume is not delineated towards west.

Figure 6.5: The distribution of total concentrations of barbiturates, sulfonamides, sulfanilic acid,

At GÅ1, barbiturates were dominating over the entire depth but considerable shares of sulfonamides
were observed in the four middle screens. The share of sulfanilic acid was increasing with depth at well
site GÅ1 while the share of sulfonamides was decreasing from screen F3 to F1. At GÅ2, barbiturates
were dominating in the majority of screens, but in screens F3 and F4 the major contamination shares
were sulfanilic acid and N-N-diethylnicotinamide, respectively. At the westernmost well site, GÅ3,
the barbiturates were dominating in F6, F3 and F2 with varying shares of sulfonamides, sulfanilic acid,
meprobamate and N-N-diethylnicotinamide. In the three remaining screens of GÅ3, only sulfanilic
acid was detected, thus the compound constituted with 100% of the total concentration in these screens.
The similarity in dominating compounds at the three well sites indicates that the contamination
detected originates from one source or is part of the same plume.

Longitudinal transect from Grindsted stream to the factory site
Figure 6.6 displays the distribution of total concentrations along a 1500-m transect from near the
stream at well site GÅ1 to just downstream the factory site at well site FG2. The sizes of the pie charts
relate to the total concentration of pharmaceutical compounds in the screens.

==PAGE:67==

Figure 6.6: The distribution of total concentrations of barbiturates, sulfonamides, sulfanilic acid,
ethyl urethane, meprobamate and N-N-diethylnicotinamide over depth in the transect from well site
GÅ1 to well site FG2. Note, that the compound concentrations in FG2, FA1 and GÅ1 were measured
in 2019-2020 while the data for 114.1447 are from 2016.
The transect includes GÅ1, FA1, 114.1447 and FG2. The vertical distribution in FG2 and GÅ1
are described in Section 6.2.1 and Section 6.2.4, respectively. In well 114.1447, barbiturates (51%)
and ethyl urethane (37%) represent the major shares in the uppermost screen F1 while sulfonamides
are dominating (77-94%) in the deeper screens. The shares of barbiturates and ethyl urethane are
decreasing with depth. In FA1, barbiturates are dominating over the entire depth with the highest
shares in the upper screens. The shares of ethyl urethane and sulfonamides are increasing with depth.
Based on Figure 6.6, the plume from the factory site to 114.1447 are dominated by both barbiturates
and sulfonamides with an increasing share of sulfonamides.
From 114.1447 to GÅ1, the plume
contains higher shares of barbiturates and sulfanilic acid. Ethyl urethane is mainly observed in the
central part of the plume, while closer to the stream, the compound is present at low concentrations or
has not been detected.
6.2.6
Detection in the stream water
In May 2020, pharmaceutical compounds were analysed in the stream water of Grindsted stream,
whereof compounds were detected from Station 3 and at all investigated stations downstream (see the
locations in Figure 2.5). Thus, the most upstream point of detection was within the considered main
contaminant discharge area (see Figure 2.1), indicating that contaminants are not discharging to the
53
==PAGE:68==
CHAPTER 6. CHARACTERISATION OF CONTAMINANT DISTRIBUTION,
HYDROGEOLOGY AND REDOX CONDITIONS
stream east of this area (Filip Floks, 2020). In total, 10 pharmaceuticals out of 36 were detected in
the stream water. The contamination was dominated by barbiturates, mainly allobarbital and 5-allyl-
5-isobutyl-barbitursyre. All compounds except allobarbital were detected in concentrations below 2
µg/L. The maximum concentration of allobarbital was 8.2 µg/L at Station 12 (see location at Figure
2.5).
The detection of allobarbital in the streamwater is consistent with the detection of barbiturates in
the plume where allobarbital was one of the dominating compounds. Contrary, sulfacarbamide and
sulfanilamide were detected in high concentrations in the streambed but were not detected in notable
concentrations in the streamwater. Similar, sulfanilic acid was detected in considerable concentrations
in the streambed but not in the stream water.

Apart from the increased concentration levels of pharmaceuticals detected in November 2020, the
distribution across pharmaceutical compound groups changed in some of the investigated well screens.
Figure 6.7 displays the share of each compound group of the total concentration of pharmaceutical
compounds in each screen, where the sizes of the pie charts relate to the total concentration levels.
The proportion of sulfanilic acid had increased in all well screens, except in GÅ3-F4 where the
proportion was still 100 %. Similar, the share of ethyl urethane had increased at all but the most
western well site. Barbiturates were still dominating in screen FG1-F5, while sulfonamides were
dominating in 114.1447-F2 and 114.2508-F1. In screen GÅ1-F4, barbiturates were dominating at
earlier measurements but in November 2020 sulfanilic acid constituted the largest share in the screen.

Figure 6.7: Shares of barbiturates, sulfonamides, sulfanilic acid, ethyl urethane, meprobamate and

in November 2020 and at the most recent measurement prior to this project.
The increased shares of sulfanilic acid in nearly all investigated well screens, as well as the increases
in the concentrations of sulfaguanidine and sulfanilamide at GÅ1-F4, indicates degradation as these
compounds are potential degradation products of sulfonamides, however, additional investigations are
needed before concluding this as a general trend. It should also be noted, that considerably variations in
the concentration levels and dominating pharmaceuticals within a year has previously been observed
at the Grindsted site by Filip Floks (2020), which complicates comparison of chemical data from
diﬀerent ﬁeld investigations.
6.3
Assessment of contaminant distribution and composition us-
ing a correlation matrix
A Pearson correlation matrix was designed to investigate whether any correlations exist between
selected parameters related to the composition and distribution of pharmaceuticals in the plume. It
was assessed: (I) if any compounds are likely to be detected together, meaning that they are mutually
correlated; (II) if any individual compounds are correlated with the depth of detection; and (III) if any
individual compounds are correlated with the distance to Grindsted stream.
55
==PAGE:70==
CHAPTER 6. CHARACTERISATION OF CONTAMINANT DISTRIBUTION,
HYDROGEOLOGY AND REDOX CONDITIONS
The constructed correlation matrix is presented in Figure 6.8 (see the matrix at larger scale in Figure
D.1 Appendix D). Only the pharmaceutical compounds identiﬁed as key compounds in Chapter 3 are
included in this matrix. A correlation matrix including all pharmaceutical compounds which have
been analysed in the samples is presented in Figure D.2 in Appendix D. All screens in the wells and
sampling points in the streambed are included.
6.3.1
Correlation between compounds
When assessing whether any compounds are mutually correlated, all compounds which were detected
above the detection limit were denoted 1 for each location and screen while the compounds were
denoted 0 if they were found to be below the detection limit. Thus, the concentrations of the compounds
were not taken into account. As the matrix only consists of binary variables, the correlation coeﬃcient
will be equal to the phi coeﬃcient (φ), which expresses the association of two binary variables (Allen,
2017).
Figure 6.8: Correlation matrix including key pharmaceutical compounds, detection depth and dis-
tance to stream.
Several compounds have a strong (φ = ±0.40-0.69) to very strong (φ = ±0.70-1) mutual relationships
according to the list concerning strengths of relationships presented in Table 5.4. Among the key
barbiturates, all are positively related to one another. The strongest positive correlation is observed
between amobarbital and pentobarbital (φ = 0.90), while 5-Allyl-5-isobutylbarbituric acid has strong
mutual correlations with both amobarbital (φ = 0.68) and pentobarbital (φ = 0.74). A very strong
positive correlation is also observed between isobutyl barbituric acid and 5-butylbarbiturate (φ = 0.78).
Thus, the likelihood of detecting one of these compounds increases, when the other has been detected.
The correlations between the mentioned barbiturates are consistent with the similarities in mobility
of the speciﬁc compounds.
The correlation between the individual sulfonamides is not as strong as among the barbiturates but all
are positively related to one another when excluding sulfanilic acid. The strongest positive correlation
is observed between sulfadimidine and sulfathiazole (φ = 0.63) followed by the correlations between
56
==PAGE:71==
CHAPTER 6. CHARACTERISATION OF CONTAMINANT DISTRIBUTION,
HYDROGEOLOGY AND REDOX CONDITIONS
sulfanilamide and sulfaguanidine (φ = 0.62) and between sulfadiazine and sulfadimine (φ= 0.53).
The results are consistent with similar mobility of the mutual correlated compounds. In addition, the
correlations are consistent with that sulfadimidine, sulfadiazine and sulfathiazole may be degraded
while sulfanilamide and sulfaguanidine are potential degradation products.
Sulfanilic acid is likely to be detected together with sulfacarbamide (φ= 0.68), sulfamethizole (φ =
0.47) and barbital (φ = 0.41). Conversely, the compound is negatively correlated with ethyl urethane,
meprobamate and sulfadimidine. Meprobamate and N-N-diethylnicotinamide have a strong mutual
relationship, which indicates that the likelihood of detecting one of the compounds increases, when
the other has been detected.
Ethyl urethane is also positively related to meprobamate and N-N-
diethylnicotinamide, but the correlations are weaker (φ = 0.26 and φ = 0.36, respectively).
Across the diﬀerent substance groups, sulfanilamide is also moderate to strongly correlated to all key
compounds except sulfamethizole, sulfathiazole and sulfanilic acid where the correlation is weak to
negligible. Several other compounds including 5-Allyl-5-isobutylbarbituric acid, pentobarbital, amo-
barbital and N-N-diethylnicotinamide are correlated to several key compounds from other substance
groups (see the speciﬁc correlation coeﬃcients in Figure 6.8).
Other compounds, which are not identiﬁed as key compounds, showed very strong relationships to
other compounds (see the speciﬁc values in the full correlation matrix in Appendix D). High correlation
coeﬃcients of up to 1 are observed among a number of compounds including butylbarbiturate, idobutal
and apronal as some compounds were only detected and/or analysed at few locations.
6.3.2
Correlation between compounds and depth
When assessing the correlation between individual compounds and the depth of detection, the average
depth of each screen was used. For the water samples taken in the streambed, the depth was set to 0
m.
Based on the correlation matrix in Figure 6.8, all key pharmaceuticals have a negative relationship to
the depth, ranging from -0.0071 to -0.55. It indicates that the frequency of detecting the compounds
decreases with depth, which is consistent with that bulk of the contamination was found in the upper
part of the aquifer.
The compound which had the highest negative coeﬃcient was pentobarbital
(rP = -0.55) followed by 5-allyl-5-isobutylbarbituric acid (rP = -0.52), amobarbital (rP = -0.42) and
sulfathiazole (rP = -0.41). However, it should be noted that many of the correlation coeﬃcients are
interpreted as no or negligible relationships (r= ±0.01-0.19) or as weak relationships (r= ±0.20-0.29)
according to the list presented in Table 5.4.
Other compounds, which are not identiﬁed as key compounds, including 4-chloraniline (rP = -
0.66), secobarbital (rP = -0.56), sulfamerazine (rP = -0.52), sulfapyridine (rP = -0.52) and allyl-n-
butylbarbiturate (rP = -0.48) showed strong (only 4-chloraniline) and moderate negative relationships
to the depth (see matrix in Appendix D).
6.3.3
Correlation between compounds and distance to stream
The distance to the stream was deﬁned as the shortest distance to any part of Grindsted stream. The
distances were estimated by the tools in GeoAtlas Live and the lines used for estimation are presented
in Figure D.3 in Appendix D. It should be noted that the distances have been estimated and the
magnitude is more important than the exact distance. The distance was set to 0-m for SB1-SB8 and
mlp-10.
57
==PAGE:72==
CHAPTER 6. CHARACTERISATION OF CONTAMINANT DISTRIBUTION,
HYDROGEOLOGY AND REDOX CONDITIONS
Based on the correlation matrix, it is observed that the majority of the compounds are negatively
correlated with the distance to the stream. Thus, the frequency of detecting the compounds increases
towards the stream.
Both, isopropyl barbituric acid (rP = -0.56) and sulfanilic acid (rP = -0.61)
have a strong negative relationship with the distance which is consistent with the detection of these
compounds close to the stream, mainly in the well screens of GÅ1-GÅ3, mlp-10 (only isopropyl
barbituric acid), 114.2568 and in the samples from the streambed. Sulfacarbamide was also found to
have a strong negative correlation (rP = -0.55) with distance to the stream. Allobarbital, sulfadimidine
and ethyl urethane have positive, yet negligible (r<0.19) correlations with distance (Yule, 1912).
The strong negative relationships of sulfanilic acid and sulfacarbamide to the distance to Grindsted
stream are in good accordance with the low log(Kow)-values of the compounds. Conversely, isopropyl
barbituric acid also has a strong negative relationship to the distance to Grindsted stream but a notable
higher log(Kow)-value. Thus, no clear connection is observed between the mobility of a compound
and the negative correlation between the compound and distance to the stream.
6.4
Redox conditions
The dominant redox conditions in the plume were determined based on the presence of the following
redox parameters: O2, NO3-, NO2-, Mn2+, Fe2+, SO42-, S2-, and CH4. These parameters were selected
as they were recently analysed at the majority of the investigated locations. The concentrations of
the species are listed in the electronic appendix E2 (see Appendix E). The concentration of oxygen
was measured at all locations except at well site FA1 and some screens at FG2 and GÅ2. All oxygen
concentrations except at FG3-F6 (1.91 mg/L) were found to be below 1 mg/L which was expected as
oxygen is considered to be entirely depleted in the study area given the extensive contamination.
The background levels of the redox parameters in the Grindsted aquifer north of Grindsted stream
were assessed based on screen F4 and F5 at well site GÅ3. This well site is considered to be located
at the outer edge of the plume but since no recent measurements of redox parameters exist for nearby
wells surely outside the contamination plume, the measurements at well site GÅ3 are assumed to be
closest to the background levels. The background parameters are presented in Table H.1 in Appendix
H.
In 1995, the background levels of redox parameters in the upper 10-12 m of the Grindsted aquifer was
outlined in a study of the landﬁll south of Grindsted stream (Bjerg et al., 1995). These background
concentrations are also presented in Table H.1 and are included for comparison reasons. As expected,
the deeper part of the aquifer is more reduced and anoxic than the upper part and has higher background
concentrations of dissolved iron and methane.
Figure 6.9 illustrates the interpreted redox conditions in three transects; (A) along the factory site from
north-west to south-east, (B) from the Grindsted stream to the factory site, and (C) along Grindsted
stream from GÅ3 to GÅ1. It is important to highlight that redox conditions are simpliﬁed in the ﬁgure
and more gradual transitions between zones are observed in reality. Depending on the composition
of redox parameters, each zone has been assigned with an overall characteristic as either nitrate
reducing (NO3- depleted), manganese reducing (Mn2+ above background level), iron reducing (Fe2+
above background level), sulphate reducing (SO42- below background concentration and S2- above
background level), or methanogenic (CH4 above background level). Conclusions on redox conditions
based on the concentrations of sulfate and sulﬁde should be handled with caution as the concentrations
are inﬂuenced by other sources including degradation of sulfonamides and oxidation of pyrite. Thus,
areas marked as sulfate reducing conditions in Figure 6.9 are linked with some uncertainties. The
58
==PAGE:73==
CHAPTER 6. CHARACTERISATION OF CONTAMINANT DISTRIBUTION,
HYDROGEOLOGY AND REDOX CONDITIONS
concentrations of the assessed redox parameters in each transect are outlined in Figure J.2, J.3 and J.4
in Appendix J.
6.4.1
Governing redox processes from the factory site to the stream
At the three well sites near the factory site, the redox conditions go from iron/sulfate reducing
and methanogenic at the well site furthest to the southeast to manganese/iron reducing towards the
northwest (see Figure 6.9A). At FG1, the methane concentrations range from 0.21 to 13 mg/L with the
highest concentrations in the two upper and the two deeper screens while considerable concentrations
of dissolved iron exist in all screens.
At FG2, the conditions are most reduced in the top of the aquifer with methane concentrations of
0.87-1.6 mg/L in the four uppermost screens. Considerable concentrations (0.6-0.7 mg/L) of sulﬁde
are detected in screen F3 and F4 indicating sulfate reducing conditions, while the top of the aquifer
is dominated by dissolved manganese and iron apart from methane. The sulfate concentrations in
the four uppermost screens of FG2 are above the background concentrations presented in Table H.1
in Appendix H. At FG3, manganese and iron reducing conditions are identiﬁed in the four deepest
screens, while the top of the aquifer is less reduced.
The redox conditions centrally in the plume are generally manganese to iron reducing with some zones
with high sulﬁde concentrations indicating sulfate reducing conditions (see Figure 6.9B). At well
114.2568, the conditions were found to be more reduced with methane concentrations between 1.5
and 6.4 mg/L. Closer to Grindsted steam and in the streambed, considerable concentrations of methane
and dissolved iron indicate iron reducing and methane producing conditions. The concentrations of
sulﬁde are below or close to the detection limit which may be due to the precipitation reaction between
dissolved iron(II) and dissolved sulﬁde.
Along the stream in the western direction, iron reducing to methanogenic conditions are present at
GÅ1 while the conditions get less reduced further to the west (see Figure 6.9C). At GÅ1, considerable
concentrations of methane, dissolved iron and dissolved manganese are found over the entire depth
except in the screen closest to the surface. The methane and dissolved iron concentrations vary from
1.5 to 4.4 mg/L and from 43 to 110 mg/L, respectively. The deeper aquifer at GÅ2 is iron reducing
(Fe2+: 17 mg/L in screen F1 and 48 mg/L in screen F2). Likewise, the conditions are iron reducing
in the deeper aquifer (Fe2+: 39 mg/L in screen F2 and 4.1 mg/L in screen F3) at well site GÅ3. Low
concentrations of methane are detected in screen F2 at both GÅ2 (1.1 mg/L) and GÅ3 (1.2 mg/L)
which may be caused by methanogenic conditions upstream.
In conclusion, the redox conditions are mainly iron reducing in the plume from the factory site to
Grindsted stream with strongly reduced zones near the factory site and in the assigned main discharge
area at the stream. The conditions get less reduced in the western direction along the stream. The
strongly reducing conditions are considered to be linked to degradation of BTEXs released from the
factory site as the pharmaceuticals are expected to be less easily biodegradable, and degradation of
chlorinated ethenes does not aﬀect the redox conditions.
In Figure 6.10, the correlation between the detected concentrations of selected redox parameters and
the sum of BTEXs is assessed using a correlation matrix. It was observed that very strong positive
relationships exist between the sum of BTEXs and the concentrations of methane (r = 0.84) and
dissolved iron (r = 0.71). This indicates that the highest concentrations of BTEXs are found in the
more reduced zones near the factory site and in the main discharge area at the stream, with strongly iron
reducing and methane producing conditions. The correlation to nitrate and nitrite were moderately
negative while the correlation to the remaining redox parameters was moderately positive to negligible.
59
==PAGE:74==
CHAPTER 6. CHARACTERISATION OF CONTAMINANT DISTRIBUTION,
HYDROGEOLOGY AND REDOX CONDITIONS
Figure 6.9: Map of the three transects and the redox conditions in these transects; (A) along the
factory site, (B) from the stream to the factory site, and (C) along the stream in the western direction.
60
==PAGE:75==
CHAPTER 6. CHARACTERISATION OF CONTAMINANT DISTRIBUTION,
HYDROGEOLOGY AND REDOX CONDITIONS
Correlation between redox conditions and possible degradation products of pharmaceutical
compounds
A correlation matrix using Spearman rank correlation coeﬃcients was designed to assess the cor-
relation between the detected concentrations of selected redox parameters and the four compounds;
sulfaguanidine, sulfanilamide, sulfanilic acid and aniline which may originate from degradation of
sulfonamides. The correlation matrix is presented in Figure 6.10. The number of data points behind
the correlations ranged from 35 to 58, thus, the critical value was rS = 0.306-0.394 at 훼= 0.01.
Figure 6.10: Spearman rank correlation matrix including selected redox parameters, sum of BTEXs,
sum of sulfonamides and the potential degradation products; sulfaguanidine, sulfanilamide, sulfanilic
acid and aniline.
Based on the correlation matrix, a strong positive relationship exists between sulfanilamide and the
redox parameters; dissolved iron (rS = 0.46) and methane (rS = 0.58). This indicates that the highest
concentrations of sulfanilamide are likely found in the more reduced zones with iron reducing and
methane producing conditions. Sulfanilic acid (rS = 0.3) and sulfaguanidine (rS = 0.3) showed moderate
positive relationships to methane, however, the correlation coeﬃcients were below the critical value,
thus, the correlations are not statistically signiﬁcant at 훼=0.01. The correlation coeﬃcient between
sulfanilic acid and dissolved iron (rS = 0.35) were above the critical value and the correlation can
be interpreted as moderate positive. As the only pharmaceutical compound, aniline has a moderate
positive relationship to sulﬁde (rS = 0.35). No considerable negative correlations were detected for
any of the included pharmaceutical compounds.
The correlation matrix in Figure 6.10 also shows that dissolved iron has strong to very strong positive
relations with methane (rS = 0.71) and dissolved manganese (rS = 0.64) which indicates that high
concentrations of iron are likely to exist together with high concentrations of manganese and methane.
Sulfate is very strong positively correlated with dissolved manganese (rS = 0.7), which indicates that
they are likely to exist together in the aquifer. The correlation between sulfate and dissolved iron
supports that pyrite oxidation is occurring at the site as both compounds are products of the oxidation
reaction (Dos Santos et al., 2016). It also supports the sequence of preference in redox processes
where reduction of manganese and iron is preferred over a reduction of sulfate, however, it can not be
61
==PAGE:76==
CHAPTER 6. CHARACTERISATION OF CONTAMINANT DISTRIBUTION,
HYDROGEOLOGY AND REDOX CONDITIONS
conﬁrmed that this correlation exists as high sulfate concentrations are present in the entire plume due to
other sources of sulfate. Lastly, a strong positive correlation between sulfanilamide and sulfaguanidine
is also observed which indicates that high concentrations of the two compounds are likely to exist
together. This indicates that the compounds follow one another in the plume or are generated under
similar redox conditions. The latter presumes that the sulfanilamide and sulfaguanidine are present as
degradation products.
In conclusion, the highest concentrations of sulfanilamide are present in the strongly anaerobic zones
characterized as methanogenic/sulfate reducing and iron reducing which is consistent with formation
of this metabolite by reductive degradation of sulfonamides. Similar, the weaker positive correlations
of sulfanilic acid to methane, dissolved iron and dissolved manganese compared to sulfanilamide are
consistent with that the compound is formed by oxidation (addition of an OH-moiety) which are more
likely to occur under more oxidised redox conditions. The moderate positive correlation of sulfanilic
acid with methane and dissolved iron may be a result of high concentrations of the metabolite in these
redox zones due to limited degradation. Additional tools would be needed for further substantiation.
As an example, CSIA could be used to assess whether the stable isotope composition of the suspected
degradation products changes along the plume.
6.5
Summary of ﬁndings
The main ﬁndings of the characterisation of the Grindsted site concerning hydrogeology, redox
conditions and pharmaceutical contaminant distribution:
I. Determined vertical hydraulic gradients and horizontal hydraulic conductivities comply with
the properties of the Miocene sand aquifer outlined in previous studies.
II. Non-degradable pharmaceuticals at the factory site are expected to reach Grindsted stream after
30-50 years given the estimated ﬂow velocities and the properties of the pharmaceuticals.
III. Currently, the plume of pharmaceuticals are distributed from the factory site to Grindsted
stream and along the stream in the western direction, with the highest concentrations at the
southeastern part of the plume near the factory site and close to the stream. The pharmaceutical
contamination is primarily present in the uppermost part of the investigated depth (+30 to +10 m
asl) close to the factory site while towards Grindsted stream, the contamination is more equally
distributed over the entire depth (+30 to -30 m asl). Diﬀerences in the dominating substances
groups are observed along the plume and over depth in the individual screens, suggesting that
the contamination originates from diﬀerent sources at the factory site. Furthermore, changes
in the contaminant composition and concentration levels of individual pharmaceuticals occur
within a few years.
IV. The predominant redox conditions in the plume are iron-reducing, with some methanogenic
zones close to the factory site and at Grindsted stream.
V. Metabolites of reductive processes are stronger correlated with methane and dissolved iron
than metabolites from oxidation based on a correlation matrix between the redox parameters
and three potential degradation products of sulfonamides; sulfanilamide, sulfanilic acid and
sulfaguanidine.
62
==PAGE:77==
Chapter 7
Application of tools for in-situ degradation
assessment
This chapter presents the results from the pharmaceutical metabolite analysis, application of inherent
tracers and mapping of bacterial degraders.
Furthermore, an evaluation of the applied tools is
provided. Chemical data from the most recent measurements at the investigated site prior to this
project are used in the assessments unless otherwise stated.
7.1
Pharmaceutical metabolites analysis
The analysis of metabolites included two parts; (1) assessment of metabolites detected in the screening
samples collected as part of this thesis; and (2) assessment of potential metabolites (sulfaguanidine,
sulfanilamide, sulfanilic acid and aniline).
7.1.1
Known metabolites: 2-aminopyrimidine, benzene sulfonamide and ben-
zoquinone
A metabolite screening of the samples collected in December 2020 was conducted (see the sampling
locations in Figure 5.1 in Subsection 5.1.1). The screening included 2-aminopyrimidine, benzene
sulfonamide and p-benzoquinone as well as sulfanilamide and sulfadiazine, whereof the three ﬁrst
mentioned compounds are metabolites of sulfonamides present in the Grindsted aquifer and the two
latter are sulfonamides (also part of the analysis by ALS Denmark). The screening results are outlined
in Table 7.1.
Benzene sulfonamide can also be a commercial produced sulfonamide but is described as a novel
sulfonamide, thus, the compound is not expected to have been produced at the Grindsted Factory
(Qadir et al., 2015), however, it can not be precluded that benzene sulfonamide exists as an impurity
from the production of sulfonamides. Yet, benzene sulfonamide is considered a degradation product
in this thesis. Quinones including p-benzoquinone are widely distributed in nature and are found
in a variety of plants, fungi and bacteria (Thomson, 1991). Furthermore, p-benzoquinone can be a
metabolite of benzene (Irons, 1985). Due to the wide occurrence in nature along with involvement in
a number of essential biological and chemical processes, p-benzoquinone is not considered the most
informative metabolite but was included to see if any tendency was observed along the plume.
None of the three metabolites included in the screening could be assigned a single parent compound
but benzene sulfonamide have an unequivocal relation to sulfonamides.
