Two out of the three metabolites included in the screening were detected in the samples which
strongly supports that degradation is occurring. The metabolite benzene sulfonamide was detected in
concentrations above 1 µg/L at all sampling locations except in screen GÅ3-F4 where the concentration
was below the detection limit. The highest concentration was detected centrally in the plume at well
114.1447 while the second-highest concentration was detected at GÅ1-F4 just west of the assigned
main contaminant discharge area. This is consistent with the observed reduced redox conditions in
these well screens as benzene sulfonamide is expected to originate from the reduction of sulfanilamide.
The metabolite p-benzoquinone was only detected in well 114.2508 at a concentration of 32.9 µg/L
while it was not possible to analyse for the compound in FG1-F5 and GÅ1-F4 (see Section 5.1.2).
Both, benzene sulfonamide and p-benzoquinone, are degradation products of sulfanilamide which
were detected in concentrations above 100 µg/L at all sampling locations apart from GÅ3-F4 where
the concentration was below the detection limit.
The concentration of the metabolite 2-aminopyrimidine was below the detection limit at all sampling
locations, while the parent compound, sulfadiazine, was detected in concentrations from <0.1 to 12.2
µg/L. The highest concentration of sulfadiazine was found at GÅ1-F4.
Assessment of metabolite concentrations with distance to source
No clear connection between distance to the factory site and concentration of benzene sulfonamide was
found, while it was not possible to assess the remaining metabolites as the concentrations were below
the detection limit or the compound had not been analysed. Also, the concentrations of the parent
compounds were not found to decrease towards the stream. It should be noted that this assessment is
based on very few data points and only represents a limited part of the screens and wells included in
the thesis. Besides, sulfanilamide can also be a metabolite of other sulfonamides.
7.1.2
Potential metabolites: sulfaguanidine, sulfanilamide, sulfanilic acid and
aniline
As stated in Table 3.4, sulfanilamide, sulfanilic acid and aniline are conﬁrmed metabolites of biodegra-
dation of sulfadiazine. These metabolites are expected to also originate from degradation of other hete-
rocyclic sulfonamides including sulfadimidine, sulfamethizole, and sulfathiazole as similar metabolites
were found by abiotic degradation of this group of sulfonamides (see Section 3.2.1). It is important
to highlight, that sulfanilic acid was predicted as a degradation product of all other key sulfonamides
by the EAWAG pathway prediction system but in the following assessment, only the heterocyclic
sulfonamides were assessed as its parent compounds. Furthermore, sulfaguanidine was also assessed
as a potential metabolite of microbial degradation of sulfonamides, even though it is only a con-
64
==PAGE:79==
CHAPTER 7. APPLICATION OF TOOLS FOR IN-SITU DEGRADATION ASSESSMENT
ﬁrmed metabolite of abiotic degradation and has a more complex molecular structure compared to
sulfanilamide and sulfanilic acid.
Based on the degradation pathways, sulfanilic acid is formed by oxidation (addition of a hydroxyl
group) while sulfanilamide is formed by reduction (from R-NH-R to NH2-R, i.e. from a C-N bond to
an N-H bond). Thus, the formation of sulfanilic acid is more likely under oxidising redox conditions
while sulfanilamide is likely formed under more reduced conditions. Based on literature and the
EAWAG pathway prediction system, sulfanilic acid can be formed directly from the other sulfonamides,
however, it can not be precluded that some sulfonamides are initially degraded to sulfanilamide under
reduced conditions and later to sulfanilic acid under more oxidising conditions.
The following assessment is based on data from water samples collected from year 2015 to 2020 at
the locations outlined in Figure 2.4 in Subsection 2.3.
Assessment of metabolite concentrations with distance to source
Figure 7.1 presents the concentrations of sulfaguanidine, sulfanilamide, sulfanilic acid and aniline at the
investigated locations. Note, that the y-axis is logarithmic to illustrate the variations in concentration
levels. The ﬁgure is structured with results from samples collected near the factory site to the left and
samples from the streambed and along the stream to the right, thus, the groundwater ﬂow towards the
stream is followed from left to right. Note, that the concentrations are summed over the associated
screens or samples at each location, thus, variations among the individual screens and samples are not
taken into consideration.
The concentrations of sulfanilamide, sulfaguanidine and sulfanilic acid are increasing with distance
towards the stream with the highest concentrations of the ﬁrst two compounds in the streambed (SB1-
8) and at GÅ1 for sulfanilic acid. This distribution suggests that these compounds may be related to
degradation of sulfonamides, however, the distribution is likely aﬀected be other attenuation processes
including retardation over the distance of more than 1000 m. These observations are not a direct proof
of degradation as none of the compounds can exclusively be regarded as degradation products.
The concentration of aniline is not observed to increase with distance to the factory area, with the
highest concentration at FG2.

If sulfaguanidine, sulfanilamide or sulfanilic acid are generated by degradation and are stable metabo-
lites, it will result in an increasing concentration of the compounds and a decreasing concentration
of the parent pharmaceuticals along a ﬂow line, with distance from the source. In this case, in-
creasing metabolite/parent pharmaceutical ratios will be observed. If the metabolites are degradable,
increasing metabolite/parent pharmaceutical ratios can also be observed as the concentration of the
parent pharmaceutical will still decrease, however, it depends on the degradation rates of the parent
compound and the metabolite, respectively.
In general, the metabolite/parent pharmaceutical ratios for all three potential metabolites are elevated
from the central part of the plume and downstream to Grindsted stream compared to near the factory
site. The ratios for sulfaguanidine are elevated at some locations, while the tendency for sulfanilamide
is more clear, especially the central part of the plume (FA1 and 114.1447) as well as in the streambed
(SB1-SB8). For sulfanilic acid, the metabolite/parent ratios are particularly elevated in the streambed
and along the stream in the western direction (GÅ1-GÅ3), while the ratios appear similar from the
factory site to the stream.

A similar pattern is observed in Figure 7.2, which shows the relative proportion of pharmaceutical

the investigated locations. This ﬁgure is structured similar to Figure 7.1, following the groundwater
ﬂow towards the stream from left to right. The relative proportions of sulfanilamide and sulfanilic
acid compared to the parent substances support that formation of sulfanilamide are more likely under
reduced conditions centrally in the plume while sulfanilic acid is likely formed under more oxidising
conditions along the stream.
These observations are not a direct proof of degradation as none of the compounds can exclusively
be regarded as degradation products as they are also commercially produced pharmaceuticals. It is
also important to mention that some of the calculated ratios include screens where only the associated
metabolites or parent compounds were detected.
7.2
Inherent tracers
Bromide and benzene were used as inherent tracers in an attempt to diﬀerentiate between in-situ
degradation and other attenuation processes. The assessment included two parts; (1) a correlation
matrix to investigate the correlation between the tracers and the pharmaceuticals detected at the study
site; and (2) ratios between the tracers and the individual pharmaceuticals to investigate whether a
systematic change in ratios exists for any of the compounds.
7.2.1
Correlation between tracers and key pharmaceuticals
A Spearman rank correlation matrix was designed to assess the correlation between the detected
concentrations of the tracers (bromide and benzene), sum of barbiturates, sum of sulfonamides,
sum of pharmaceutical compounds, aniline and the individual key pharmaceuticals (see list of key
compounds in Table 3.1). It is presumed that persistent pharmaceuticals will be correlated with the
inherent tracer given that they have similar transport properties, originate from the same contaminant
source and are not degradation products.
The correlation matrix is presented in Figure 7.3, while Table 7.3 summarises the interpretations of
selected correlation coeﬃcients. The number of data points ranged from 43 to 58, thus, the critical
value for the Spearman rank correlation coeﬃcient is rS = 0.306-0.355 at a signiﬁcance level of 0.01.
Figure 7.3: Spearman rank correlation matrix of the association between the tracers (bromide and
benzene) and the sum of barbiturates, sum of sulfonamides, sum of pharmaceutical compounds,
aniline and the individual key pharmaceuticals.
Based on the correlation matrix, bromide has a strong positive relationship to the sum of barbiturates
(rS = 0.61) and sum of pharmaceutical compounds (rS = 0.50) while the relationship between bromide
and the sum of sulfonamides (rS = 0.35) is moderate positive. Among the individual compounds,
bromide has a strong correlation with several key pharmaceuticals, mainly barbiturates. Bromide has
69
==PAGE:84==
CHAPTER 7. APPLICATION OF TOOLS FOR IN-SITU DEGRADATION ASSESSMENT
a very strong positive relationship to isopropyl barbituric acid (rS = 0.73) and sulfanilic acid (rS =
0.70), indicating that isopropyl barbituric acid and sulfanilic acid are very likely to follow bromide.
The strong positive correlation of sulfanilic acid with bromide argues against that the compound is
also a degradation product.
Benzene has a strong relationship to the sum of barbiturates (rS = 0.66), while the correlation co-
eﬃcients indicate very strong relationships to the sum of sulfonamides (rS = 0.77) and sum of
pharmaceutical compounds (rS = 0.78). Benzene is also very strongly correlated to the three key
barbiturates, 5-butylbarbiturate (rS = 0.78), amobarbital (rS = 0.73) and isobutyl barbituric acid (rS =
0.75) as well as meprobamate (rS = 0.73), while strong relationships exits between benzene and several
key pharmaceuticals, both sulfonamides (r=0.44-0.68), barbiturates (r=0.48-0.68) and ethyl urethane
(rS = 0.41).

The strong to very strong positive correlations of the sum of barbiturates and sum of pharmaceutical
compounds to the two tracers suggest that the compounds or at least the most dominating ones are
persistent.
A negative or negligible correlation between the tracers, bromide and benzene, and an individual
pharmaceutical indicates that the compound does not follow the tracer which may be caused by
degradation or generation of the compound. Weak to negligible correlations exist between bromide
and the pharmaceuticals; sulfadiazine, sulfadimidine and sulfathiazole, which is consistent with that
these three compounds are known to be biodegradable. Contradictory, sulfamethizole which are also
known to be biodegradable has a strong positive relationship to bromide, thus, further investigations
are needed to conclude if degradation of these sulfonamides occurs.
70
==PAGE:85==
CHAPTER 7. APPLICATION OF TOOLS FOR IN-SITU DEGRADATION ASSESSMENT
Scatter plots of the concentrations of pharmaceuticals and tracers
The data behind the correlation matrix presented in Figure 7.3 were assessed by scatter plots of the
concentration of benzene or bromide as a function of the concentrations of the individual pharma-
ceuticals. No linear correlations were observed while exponential correlations were found between;
bromide and isobutyl barbituric acid (R2 = 0.56), bromide and sulfanilic acid (R2 = 0.58), and benzene
and isobutyl barbituric acid (R2 = 0.54) (see the scatter plots in Appendix K). Thus, the concentrations
of these pharmaceuticals increase exponentially with the concentration of the tracer. In general, the
observations from the scatter plots were not found to provide any directly applicable information
about the degradation of the pharmaceuticals but pointed towards some trends which can be further
investigated.
7.2.2
Ratios between tracers and key pharmaceuticals along the plume
The ratio between the pharmaceuticals and tracers along the plume can be an indicator of degrada-
tion given that the pharmaceutical follows the tracer if it is non-degradable. If the pharmaceutical
compound is degraded, the concentration of the compound would decrease and result in a decreasing
pharmaceutical/tracer ratio along the plume. It should be noted that these observations are not a
direct proof of degradation as the ratios will be inﬂuenced by other factors including diﬀerences in
environmental behaviour. Thus, application of additional tools is necessary for further evidence of
degradation of the pharmaceuticals.
The pharmaceutical/tracer-ratios of the key pharmaceuticals and the potential tracers, benzene and
bromide, are presented in Appendix K. The ratio between aniline and the two tracers is also included
in the appendix.
Ratios between benzene and key pharmaceuticals
Based on the correlation matrix in Figure 7.3, benzene and the key barbiturates are likely to follow
one another. Therefore, the pharmaceutical/tracer ratios of benzene and the key barbiturates in each
well screen along the plume were determined. Among the eight key barbiturates, only the ratios of 5-
bytulbarbiturate and isobutyl barbituric acid were found to decrease from the factory site to Grindsted
stream. For the remaining barbiturates, the ratios were found to increase towards the stream with the
highest ratios at well site GÅ1.
The pharmaceutical/tracer ratios between benzene and the key sulfonamides were also determined.
Similar to the barbiturates, the pharmaceutical/tracer ratios of the majority of the sulfonamides in-
creased towards Grindsted stream. Among the eight key sulfonamides, decreasing ratios were observed
for three compounds; sulfadiazine, sulfadimidine and sulfathiazole. The observations are consistent
with the presumption that these three compounds may degrade and result in decreasing ratios as they
are among the sulfonamides known to be biodegradable (see further information in Section 3.2.2). The
pharmaceutical/tracer ratios of the remaining ﬁve sulfonamides were increasing with distance to the
factory site, with the highest ratios along the stream. Among the three potential degradation products
(sulfaguanidine, sulfanilamide and sulfanilic acid), the ratios of especially sulfanilic acid increased
towards the stream.
Ratios between bromide and key pharmaceuticals
The pharmaceutical/tracer ratios between bromide and the key pharmaceuticals were also determined
as bromide is highly correlated with several barbiturates and sulfonamides. It should be noted, that
bromide has not been analysed at well 114.2508, mlp-10 and in the samples (SB1-SB8) from the
streambed, thus, no ratios exist for the stream bank and streambed.
Among the barbiturates, clear decreasing ratios were observed for 5-allyl-5-isobutyl barbituric acid,
71
==PAGE:86==
CHAPTER 7. APPLICATION OF TOOLS FOR IN-SITU DEGRADATION ASSESSMENT
5-butylbarbiturate and isobutyl barbituric acid, while the remaining compounds had the highest ratios
centrally in the plume and showed no clear tendency. Concerning the sulfonamides, the ratios of
sulfadiazine and sulfathiazole were decreasing towards Grindsted stream, while the ratio of sulfanilic
acid was increasing.
Similar to the barbiturates, the remaining sulfonamide/bromide-ratios were
highest centrally in the plume.
Summary of pharmaceutical/tracer ratios
Among the sulfonamides, the pharmaceutical/tracer-ratios were found to decrease for sulfadiazine,
sulfadimidine (only with benzene as the tracer) and sulfathiazole which is consistent with that these
compounds are among the sulfonamides known to be biodegradable (see further information in Section
3.2.2). Additionally, the ratios between sulfanilic acid and the tracers were found to increase, which
indicates formation of this sulfonamide as a metabolite. However, the pharmaceutical/tracer ratios
also increased for sulfonamides which are not known to be potential degradation products including
sulfacetamide and sulfacarbamide.
The ratios of sulfanilic acid were mainly elevated at GÅ1, GÅ2 and GÅ3 indicating that the compound
is formed by oxidation under less reduced redox conditions, presuming that it is a metabolite.
7.3
Analysis for bacterial degraders of pharmaceuticals
The conducted microbial analysis of the groundwater samples determined the present biodiversity
at genus level, thus, the results covered microbial species known to be involved in a variety of
metabolisms. The following section will mainly assess microbes relevant for degradation of phar-
maceuticals, while an overview of the dominate microbial genera in the samples as well as a brief
assessment of the natural degradation potential of chlorinated ethenes and BTEXs are provided in
Appendix L. Note, that it is not known if the speciﬁc degrader species are present at the site as the
analysis is performed at genus level. Furthermore, the conducted analysis is quantitative, thus, the
present bacterial concentrations have not been determined.
7.3.1
General observations
The microbial biodiversity at the Grindsted site was dominated by anaerobic microbes, in particular
Dehalococcoides (see relative abundance and function in Appendix L). This dominance of anaerobic
microbes suggested predominant anaerobic conditions in the Grindsted aquifer which is consistent
with the observed redox conditions and previous studies of the site. Furthermore, the highest relative
abundance of methanogenic species (16 %) was detected in well screen FG1-F5, followed by 114.2508-
F1 (5%). This is consistent with the methane levels measured in these wells, however, all other
investigated wells are also expected to be anaerobic. Aerobic degradation processes may still occur at
the site, but aerobic microbes are not dominant (relative abundance <2% in all samples).
The microbial population and relative genus abundances were quite diﬀerent in the four samples anal-
ysed (see Figure L.1 in Appendix L), which may be a result of diﬀerent combinations of contaminants
and physio-chemical conditions present at the individual sampling site.
7.3.2
Degraders of pharmaceuticals
Three bacterial genera from the list of selected barbiturates and sulfonamides degraders from Table
3.2 and Table 3.4 in Chapter 3 were detected by the NGS analysis. These are Bacillus, Burkholderia,
and Pseudomonas which all cover a variety of bacterial species. The three detected bacterial genera
72
==PAGE:87==
CHAPTER 7. APPLICATION OF TOOLS FOR IN-SITU DEGRADATION ASSESSMENT
are recognised to degrade a broad spectrum of contaminants including PAHs and BTEXs and to be
involved in other processes such as iron reduction. Thus, the bacterial genera are not speciﬁc to
degradation of pharmaceuticals (Ghorbanzadeh et al., 2014; Kim et al., 2003; Morlett-Chávez et al.,
2010).
The relative abundances of each bacterial genera and its known pharmaceutical degradation target are
outlined in Table 7.4. It is important to highlight that the detected bacteria may also degrade other
pharmaceuticals not listed in the table as biodegradation of sulfonamides depends on similar degraders
and bacterial adaptation can occur as stated in Subsection 3.2.2.

In general, the relative abundances of the three bacteria were relatively low, ranging between 0.02 to
1.5 %. Thus, none of the degraders was among the dominant degrading bacteria (relative abundance
>2%) at the investigated locations which is expected given the predominant reduced redox conditions
as these bacterial genera are aerobic, yet Bacillus and Pseudomonas can adapt to anaerobic conditions.
Furthermore, sulfonamides are antibiotics and may have antimicrobial activity on some of the degrader
bacteria, preventing them from growing (Bhattacharjee, 2016a; Qin et al., 2016). The antimicrobial
properties combined with the lack of aerobiosis may be the reason behind the low relative abundances
of the degraders.
Figure 7.4 displays the relative abundance of (A) Bacillus, (B) Burkholderia and (C) Pseudomonas
at each sampling locations together with the detected concentrations of the associated degradation
targets. It was expected to observe negative correlations between the abundance of degrader bacteria
and the degradation target if the bacteria are degrading the pharmaceutical. No connections between
the concentration of sulfanilamide and the relative abundances of Bacillus and Burkholderia were
observed, while there was a positive connection between Pseudomonas and sulfanilic acid, which
indicates that sulfanilic acid is rather a metabolite than a target compound of Pseudomonas. The
deviation between sulfanilamide and its known bacterial degraders may be due to: (1) degradation of
other pharmaceuticals by the same bacteria, (2) diﬀerences among the degrader species in resistance
against the antimicrobial activity of sulfonamides, or (3) no species-speciﬁc for degradation of sul-
fanilamide are present. Furthermore, sulfanilamide and sulfanilic acid are also degradation products
of other sulfonamides which complicates the assessment. If the concentration of sulfanilic acid is
plotted together with the relative abundance of Burkholderia (Figure 7.4D), a positive correlation is
observed which indicates that sulfanilic acid is produced as the metabolite by Burkholderia, however,
additional studies are necessary. No connections between the relative abundance of Bacillus and any
of the barbiturates known to induce monooxygenase from Bacillus megaterium ATCC 14581 were
observed.

of sulfonamides and barbiturates are expected to be very low as they have not been identiﬁed in the
analysis and the relative abundances of the three detected degraders are low.
Abundance related to concentrations of bacteria
It can be discussed if it is correct to compare the relative abundances of bacteria between the sampling
locations when the total concentration of bacteria in each sample diﬀer from well to well. Hence, a
sample with low bacterial abundance may have a higher amount of bacteria than samples with higher
relative abundances if the total number of bacteria in the ﬁrst-mentioned sample is higher.
In samples collected from January 2020 to May 2020, the total concentrations of eubacteria were
measured in the investigated well screens (FG1-F5, 114.1447-F2, 114.2508-F1 and GÅ1-F4) by
Microbial Insights Europe (see concentrations is Table 7.4). These measurements were used to relate
the observed abundances to the number of bacteria at the four sampling locations. Figure 7.6 presents
the concentrations of the three bacterial genera, based on the total concentrations of bacteria. It is
observed, that the highest amount of relevant bacteria is detected in well screen 114.2508-F1, followed
by well screen GÅ1-F4 where highest relative abundances were detected using NGS.
Figure 7.6: Bacterial concentration of Bacillus, Burkholderia, and Pseudomonas based on the
determined total concentrations of eubacteria in samples collected from January 2020 to May 2020.
It should be noted, that the NGS analysis conducted in this thesis included both bacteria and archaea,
whereof the abundance of archaea ranged between 2.9 to 18.6% in the samples. No archaea have
been identiﬁed as degraders of pharmaceuticals in this project. Furthermore, the calculated bacterial
concentrations of Bacillus, Burkholderia, and Pseudomonas are above the detected concentration of
Dehalococcoides in the samples from January 2020 to May 2020, which are not consistent with results
76
==PAGE:91==
CHAPTER 7. APPLICATION OF TOOLS FOR IN-SITU DEGRADATION ASSESSMENT
from the NGS analysis where the relative abundance of Dehalococcoides was considerably higher
than the abundance of the three suspected pharmaceutical degraders. This suggests that determination
of bacterial concentration in the samples can vary and any conclusions are linked with uncertainty.
Yet, the importance of considering the diﬀerences in bacterial concentrations between the sampling
locations is stated.
7.4
Summary of ﬁndings
The main ﬁndings from the application of the three degradation assessment tools:
I. Presenceoftwodegradationproductsofsulfonamides, benzenesulfonamideandp-benzoquinone,
in the plume of pharmaceuticals, with the highest concentrations centrally in the plume and close
to Grindsted stream.
II. Increasing metabolite/parent pharmaceutical ratios for sulfaguanidine, sulfanilamide and sul-
fanilic acid along the plume, indicating formation of these compounds.
III. Increasing relative proportions of sulfaguanidine, sulfanilamide and sulfanilic acid at mole basis
along the plume, indicating formation of these compounds.
IV. Strong correlations between the concentration of the inherent tracers, bromide and benzene, and
several pharmaceutical compounds and substance groups, which argue against degradation of
these compounds.
V. Weak to negligible correlations between bromide and the pharmaceuticals; sulfadiazine, sul-
fadimidine and sulfathiazole, which is consistent with that these compounds are known to be
biodegradable.
VI. Decreasing pharmaceutical/tracer-ratios of sulfadiazine, sulfadimidine and sulfathiazole along
theplumewhichsupportsthatthesecompoundsarebiodegradable. Increasingpharmaceutical/tracer-
ratios of sulfanilic acid, indicating formation of the compound.
VII. 5-Allyl-5-isobutyl barbituric acid, 5-butylbarbiturate and isobutyl barbituric acid was pointing
towards as potentially biodegradable based on decreasing trends in pharmaceutical/tracer-ratios.
VIII. Presence of three bacterial genera (Bacillus, Burkholderia and Pseudomonas) of species able
to degrade barbiturates and sulfonamides. The relative abundances of the three genera are
low, ranging between 0.02 to 1.5 %, which are likely due to aerobiosis and the antimicrobial
properties of sulfonamides.
IX. Positive correlations between the measured concentrations of sulfanilic acid and the relative
abundances of Burkholderia and Pseudomonas, indicating that sulfanilic acid is produced as a
metabolite by species from these two bacterial genera.

Based on this study, the metabolite analysis and the bacterial degrader characterisation were the most
useful tools to assess the degradation and potential for degradation at the Grindsted site, while the
assessment of degradation by usage of inherent tracers was found to rather support the other tools than
documenting in-situ degradation. Inherent tracers can be used to point towards compounds which
may be subjects to degradation or are degradation products, based on the observed trends between the
compound and inherent tracer.
78
==PAGE:93==
Chapter 8
Evaluation of monitored natural attenuation
as a remediation approach to pharmaceutical
compounds
This chapter assesses monitored natural degradation as a viable remediation approach to the plume
of pharmaceuticals at the Grindsted site. The potential for biodegradation and documentation of
degradation at the site are evaluated, and a conceptual understanding of degradation in the plume is
proposed.
MNA is a remediation approach based on the reliance on natural attenuation processes to remediate
the contaminated site (Bjerg et al., 2011; Jørgensen et al., 2010; USEPA, 1999). The strategy can be
chosen as a stand-alone solution, as follow-up remediation, or as a complement to other remediation
techniques. Although the term MNA suggests that all attenuation processes are accepted, current
practice relies mainly on the only truly destructive process, degradation, to achieve long-term recovery.
MNA is a knowledge-based solution which involves a detailed investigation and demonstration to prove
the eﬃciency of the natural attenuation processes and reach the requested remediation. Furthermore,
long-term monitoring of the site is often needed to ensure suﬃcient contaminant reduction (Bjerg
et al., 2011; Rivett and Thornton, 2008).
Technical protocols have been developed to guide the choice of remediation by MNA for particular
contaminants including chlorinated ethenes (see Wiedemeier et al. (1998)). These protocols often
rely on the lines of evidence approach which refers to diﬀerent ways to prove or evaluate if MNA
is an acceptable remedial strategy at the site. In 1999, the United States Environmental Protection
Agency (US EPA) proposed three lines of evidence for the demonstration of natural attenuation
processes; (I) ﬁeld-scale documentation of mass removal processes, (II) demonstration of ﬁeld-scale
degradation based on in-situ indicators, and (III) supportive evidence such as microbial degradation
characterisation (Wiedemeier et al., 1999). In addition, decisions to utilize MNA should be supported
by a site characterization, where an understanding of the distribution of the groundwater contaminants
and the site hydrogeological data are critical elements.
The following sections will evaluate the potential for MNA as sole remediation approach to the plume
of pharmaceuticals at the Grindsted site. This thesis mainly focused on the second and third lines of
evidence by combining several in-situ degradation assessment tools, thus, the following sections do
not distinguish rigidly between line II and III.
79
==PAGE:94==
CHAPTER 8. EVALUATION OF MONITORED NATURAL ATTENUATION AS A
REMEDIATION APPROACH TO PHARMACEUTICAL COMPOUNDS
8.1
Evaluation of the biodegradation potential
The tools applied to assess the biodegradation potential in the Grindsted aquifer were mainly anal-
ysis for microbial degraders and knowledge on redox conditions. Three bacterial genera (Bacillus,
Burkholderia and Pseudomonas) of species able to degrade selected barbiturates and sulfonamides
were identiﬁed in the aquifer. The bacteria were detected at low relative abundances, however, their
presence indicates potential for biodegradation. Furthermore, positive correlations between the abun-
dance of the degraders, Burkholderia and Pseudomonas, and the concentrations of sulfanilic acid
were observed, indicating that sulfanilic acid is produced as a metabolite from their metabolisms.
Yet, no negative correlations between the relative abundances of the bacterial genera and their target
compounds were observed which was expected if they are degraded by the detected microbes. How-
ever, several of the target compounds (sulfanilamide and sulfanilic acid) are also degradation products
which complicate the interpretation of the results. Furthermore, it is important to highlight that the
detected bacterial genera are not speciﬁc for degradation of pharmaceuticals and only their presence
has been documented, not their activity.
The redox conditions in the aquifer were found to be anaerobic, with mainly iron-reducing conditions
from the factory site to Grindsted stream and some highly reduced zones with methanogenic conditions
near the factory site and in the main contaminant discharge area at the stream. These redox conditions
are promising for the natural degradation of chlorinated ethenes by anaerobic reductive dechlorination
which is supported by the detection of Dehalococcoides as the dominating bacterial genus at the site.
In contrast, the redox conditions are not favourable for degradation of barbiturates and sulfonamides as
the present degrader genera appear to perform aerobic metabolisms, though some species within the
genera can adapt to anaerobic conditions. The highest relative abundance of pharmaceutical degraders
was detected at GÅ1-F4 which is consistent with less reduced redox conditions at this well site. The
concentration of dissolved oxygen was also highest at this location during the manual measurements
of the ﬁeld parameters in December 2020. Figure 8.1 displays the total relative abundance of known
pharmaceutical degraders and the measured oxygen concentrations in the screens.
An expected
connection is observed, thus, the potential degradation of pharmaceutical compounds is likely higher
in the less reduced parts of the aquifer and where oxygen is present. Note, the concentrations of oxygen
are still less than 1 mg/L, indicating anaerobic conditions.
Figure 8.1: Relative abundance of known pharmaceutical degraders and the manually measured
concentration of dissolved oxygen.
If the plume of pharmaceuticals expands further along Grindsted stream in the western direction or
enters the hyporheic zone of the stream in the area west of the main contaminant discharge area, the
80
==PAGE:95==
CHAPTER 8. EVALUATION OF MONITORED NATURAL ATTENUATION AS A
REMEDIATION APPROACH TO PHARMACEUTICAL COMPOUNDS
potential for biodegradation is likely higher in these zones with more favourable redox conditions,
assuming that biodegradable pharmaceuticals and the associated bacterial degraders are present. It
should be noted, that the bacterial community typically need time to adapt to the contaminants
reaching them. A long lag-time, referring to the time before the bacteria adapt to the new environment
with the contaminants and starts actively utilizing the contaminants as growth-supporting electron
acceptors, can be of concern in relation to further spreading of the contaminants. This hypothesis
can be investigated by microbial analysis at well site GÅ2 to characterise the microbial composition
along the Grindsted Stream in the western direction where pharmaceutical compounds are the main
contaminants present. Furthermore, the predominance of sulfanilic acid in the wells along the stream
(GÅ1, GÅ2 and GÅ3) indicates less reduced redox conditions, assuming that sulfanilic acid is present
as a metabolite of oxidative degradation of sulfonamides.
To summarise, there is potential for degradation based on the presence of bacterial genera of relevant
pharmaceutical degraders in the Grindsted aquifer.
However, the evaluation of the degradation
potential is limited by the absence of information about presence of the speciﬁc degraders, their
activity and the bacterial concentrations at the site. Furthermore, the relevant bacterial genera were
detected at low abundances (<2%) as their growth are likely limited by the predominant anaerobic,
reduced redox conditions as well as the antimicrobial properties of sulfonamides. Hence, the potential
for degradation is expected to increase if the plume of pharmaceuticals spreads further along Grindsted
Stream in the western direction where the aquifer may be less reduced with higher concentrations of
dissolved oxygen. The degradation potential is also expected to be higher in the streambed, outside
the most reduced zones.
8.2
Demonstration of in-situ degradation
Degradation of pharmaceutical compounds in the Grindsted aquifer was evaluated using metabolite
analysis and assessment of inherent tracers. Two known degradation products of sulfonamides, benzene
sulfonamide and p-benzoquinone, were detected which strongly supports that degradation is occurring
at the Grindsted site. Especially, the presence of benzene sulfonamide documents degradation as this
metabolite is speciﬁc for sulfonamides while p-benzoquinone can not be assigned an unequivocal
relationship to sulfonamides. Furthermore, the detection of benzene sulfonamide is consistent with
the presence of the bacterial genera, Bacillus and Burkholderia, able to degrade sulfanilamide which
is the main parent compound of the metabolite. No correlation between the relative abundance of
Bacillus and Burkholderia and the detected concentrations of benzene sulfonamide was observed.
The occurrence of degradation of pharmaceuticals at the site was further supported by an assessment of
the three potential degradation products of sulfonamides; sulfaguanidine, sulfanilamide and sulfanilic
acid. The concentrations of these compounds were found to increase with distance to the contaminant
source at Grindsted factory site. In addition, the metabolite/parent pharmaceutical ratios for all three
compounds are elevated in the central part of the plume and towards Grindsted stream compared to
near the factory site. These observations are only indications of degradation as the three compounds
can not be regarded solely as degradation products since they are also used as active ingredients in
pharmaceuticals.
The analysis of inherent tracers was sorely an indicator of degradation as none of the compounds
included in the analysis can exclusively be regarded as degradation products.
The indicator for
degradation was decreasing ratios between the individual pharmaceuticals and the two inherent tracers,
bromide and benzene, along the plume. Sulfadiazine, sulfadimidine and sulfathiazole were found to
have decreasing ratios towards Grindsted stream indicating degradation which is consistent with that
81
==PAGE:96==
CHAPTER 8. EVALUATION OF MONITORED NATURAL ATTENUATION AS A
REMEDIATION APPROACH TO PHARMACEUTICAL COMPOUNDS
these compounds are known to be degradable. Furthermore, the ratios of sulfanilic acid were increasing
along the plume which is likely a result of generation of the compound during the degradation of other
sulfonamides. Concerning the key barbiturates, decreasing ratios were observed for 5-bytulbarbiturate
and isobutyl barbituric acid using both bromide and benzene which implies that these compounds are
degraded. Yet, the knowledge about degradability of barbiturates are limited and further investigations
are crucial.
To summarise, the occurrence of degradation of pharmaceutical compounds in the Grindsted aquifer
is strongly supported by the presence of two metabolites of sulfonamides, of which one has an
unequivocal relationship to sulfonamides. Furthermore, degradation of pharmaceuticals is likely to
take place due to detected increases in the concentrations of potential metabolites and decreases
in the pharmaceutical/tracer-ratios of compounds known to be degradable with distance from the
contaminant source area.
8.3
Conceptual understanding of degradation in the contaminant
plume
Based on the results from the applied degradation assessment tools as well as the described geology,
hydrogeology and contaminant distribution, a conceptual understanding of degradation of pharmaceu-
ticals in the Grindsted factory plume is proposed. The interpretation is given at the level of screens,
areas and the entire plume. Figure 8.2 gives an overview of the included well screens and areas as
wells as the location of two transect A and B.
Figure 8.2: Included (A) well screens, (B) areas and transects in the conceptual understanding of
degradation in the plume of pharmaceuticals.
It is important to highlight that the knowledge about the environmental behaviour of pharmaceuticals
including degradation is still limited.
Furthermore, ﬂuctuations in the concentration levels and
in dominating pharmaceuticals at the Grindsted site have previously been observed, which further
complicates the assessment.
Thus, the following is an interpretation of the site situation and is
considered speculative.

the location of each area in Figure 8.2). Table 8.2 presents the general understanding of degradation
in the diﬀerent zones, assuming that sulfanilamide and sulfanilic acid are both parent compounds and
metabolites and that the speciﬁc bacterial degraders are present and active at the site.

In general, it was complicated to state any general understanding of the ongoing processes within the

Interpretation of the entire plume
A number of sketches of the plume distribution are presented in Figure 8.3 based on the interpretations
at screen and area level as described above. It is important to highlight that these sketches do not take
the distribution over depth into consideration and is only constructed based on the locations stated in
Section 2.3, excluding the results from the stream water. The purpose of these sketches is to represent
the current understanding of the plume of pharmaceuticals.
Figure 8.4 presents the ﬂow ﬁeld behind the sketches which are based on the simulated groundwater
ﬂow paths and hydraulic equipotential lines by Balbarini et al. (2020). The groundwater ﬂow direction
in both the unconﬁned and semiconﬁned aquifer is towards the stream, however, their ﬂow ﬁelds
are diﬀerent which is expected to result in a plume discharging at angles close to 90° to the stream
and a plume continuing almost parallel to the stream in the western direction. Figure 8.5 displays
an interpretation of the vertical redox conditions in Transect A and B in the plume (see location of
transects in Figure 8.2), based on the results described in Section 6.4. Note, that the redox conditions
are simpliﬁed in the ﬁgure and more gradual transitions between zones are observed in reality.
Figure 8.3A sketches the distribution of total sulfonamides based in the most recent data prior to this
project and includes all compounds listed in Table F.1 in Appendix F. It is observed that the highest
concentration levels of sulfonamides were present from centrally in the plume and towards Grindsted
Stream. Centrally in the plume, the bulk of the contamination with sulfonamides is sulfanilamide,
which may also be present as a degradation product.
Thus, the concentration of sulfanilamide
is excluded in Figure 8.3B and is presented as individual compound in Figure 8.3C to illustrate
its importance in the central part of the plume.
Based on Figure 8.3C, sulfanilamide is mainly
present centrally in the plume and increases in concentration level towards the main contaminant
discharge area of Grindsted steam.
Another tendency of sulfanilamide is observed based on the
samples collected in November 2020 (Figure 8.3D), where concentrations above 500 µg/L were
present centrally in the plume and close to the stream in the western direction. At the main contaminant
discharge area, sulfanilamide was measured at a lower concentration level in November 2020 compared
to earlier measurements.
The distribution of sulfanilic acid is sketched in Figure 8.3E, while a
reassessment based on the measurements from November 2020 is presented in Figure 8.3F. Notable
higher concentrations were observed in the latter measurements, especially along the stream (>1000
µg/L) in the western direction and at the southeastern end of the factory site (>500 µg/L). It should
be mentioned, that Figure 8.3D and Figure 8.3F are reassessments of the distribution based on
only ﬁve measurement points. Yet, higher concentrations of pharmaceuticals including the potential
degradation products, sulfanilamide and sulfanilic acid, were observed in November 2020 compared
to previous measurements.
Figure 8.3G sketches the distribution of total barbiturates, with the highest concentration at the
southeastern end of the factory site and decreasing concentrations towards the stream and along the
stream in the western direction. In this study, no direct documentation of degradation of barbiturates
was obtained but it can not be excluded.

A simple conceptual model of degradation of sulfonamide in the plume is displayed in Figure 8.6,
assuming that degradation is occurring at the site and the speciﬁc bacterial degraders are present
and active. Degradation of sulfonamides is believed to occur to some extent from the southern part
of the factory site (FG1 and FG2) to the main contaminant discharge area and along the stream,
with decreasing degradation potential towards west. Degradation of sulfonamides to sulfanilamide
and benzene sulfonamide is believed to mainly occur centrally in the plume, while degradation to
sulfanilic acid likely occurs closer to Grindsted stream and close to the factory site. The concentration
of p-benzoquinone is elevated in the main contaminant discharge area while this metabolite was not
detected at the well site furthest to the west along the stream (GÅ3) and data lack at the closest
wells (GÅ1 and GÅ2) along the stream. However, as mentioned in Subsection 7.1.1, the presence of
p-benzoquinone can be caused be plants or degradation of benzene, thus, detection of the compound
can not only be attributed to degradation of sulfonamides.
Based on this project, it is concluded that neither entirely degradation nor non-destructive attenuation
processes can describe the distribution of sulfonamides. Furthermore, this project conﬁrms that the
plume of pharmaceuticals is complex and considerable variations in concentration levels of individual
compounds can occur within one year as observed along the stream in the western direction and at the
southeastern end of the factory site.
8.4
Evaluation of MNA as the remediation strategy
Several of the key steps for evaluating natural attenuation have been assessed in this thesis including
characterisation of the distribution of pharmaceutical compounds in the plume, demonstration of
in-situ degradation and application of supportive tools assessing the biodegradation potential of the
pharmaceuticals. However, in order to evaluate the viability of MNA as sole remediation solution to
the plume of pharmaceutical compounds from Grindsted factory site further qualiﬁcation is required,
though potential for the approach has been demonstrated.
This project can serve as background
information for future investigations.
The current scientiﬁc evidence supporting that MNA may be an acceptable remediation approach at
the site is the detection of degradation products of selected pharmaceuticals as well as the presence
of bacterial genera of species known to degrade pharmaceuticals. The main argument against relying
solely on MNA is that the plume of pharmaceuticals still composes an environmental risk as high
concentrations of pharmaceuticals (>1000 µg/L) are detected close to Grindsted Stream and in the
streambed. Other opposing arguments are that it is unknown if the speciﬁc degraders of the pharma-
ceuticals are present, if the bacterial degraders are active, and if the majority of the pharmaceuticals are
degradable under the present conditions in the plume. Furthermore, formation of metabolites during
degradation of pharmaceuticals and their environmental impacts are not well understood and it is un-
known whether the pharmaceuticals can fully degraded over time. The degradation rates of both parent
compounds and metabolites are also unknown, which are critical in evaluating the eﬃciency of the
natural attenuation processes to reach the remediation goals. In general, essential knowledge about the
degradation of pharmaceuticals is still limited, especially for barbiturates, N-N-diethylnicotinamide,
ethyl urethane and meprobamate.
88
==PAGE:103==
CHAPTER 8. EVALUATION OF MONITORED NATURAL ATTENUATION AS A
REMEDIATION APPROACH TO PHARMACEUTICAL COMPOUNDS

As mentioned in Chapter 2, the plume from Grindsted factory consists of various contaminants
including pharmaceutical compounds along with chlorinated ethenes and BTEX compounds. Thus,
to fully understand the potential of biodegradation of pharmaceuticals at the site and assess the
applicability of MNA as an eﬀective remedial strategy, the inﬂuence of the other contaminants
should be investigated.
This includes the inﬂuence of the degrader bacteria at species level in
the microbial community. Bacteria which are responsible for the biodegradation of BTEXs might
have a competitive inﬂuence on the pharmaceutical degraders e.g. in relation to electron acceptor
availability. Furthermore, the potential inhibitory eﬀect of the antimicrobial activity of sulfonamides
on the bacterial community should be examined. In general, it should be investigated if the presence
of the diﬀerent contaminants in the plume increase or hamper the biodegradation of the others in any
way.
Finally, investigations from sites contaminated with pharmaceuticals with diﬀerent and similar settings
could improve the level of knowledge for further evaluation of the potential of MNA at the Grindsted
site.
90
==PAGE:105==
Chapter 9
Conclusion
This project has contributed to the understanding of natural attenuation of pharmaceuticals in the
contaminant plume discharging from Grindsted factory site and given a preliminary evaluation of
MNA as a viable remediation strategy for the pharmaceuticals.
The bulk of the pharmaceutical contamination in the plume from the Grindsted factory site was
found to consist of 20 key pharmaceuticals including eight barbiturates, nine sulfonamides, N-N-
diethylnicotinamide, ethyl urethane and meprobamate.
The potential for biodegradation of these
compounds was assessed by reviewing existing literature, consulting biodegradation databases as well
as mapping of functional groups. Microbial degradation of sulfonamides was found to occur under
both aerobic and anaerobic conditions by a variety of bacteria, whereof some have been identiﬁed at
bacterial strain-level. Information about the biodegradability of barbiturates was more contradicting,
with only a few compound-speciﬁc metabolites and a single bacterial degrader species identiﬁed. No
metabolites of ethyl urethane, meprobamate and N-N-diethylnicotinamide were described, and only
meprobamate was mentioned as biodegradable.
The distribution and composition of pharmaceuticals in the multiple contaminant plume discharging
from the Grindsted factory site towards Grindsted stream were interpreted based on the most recent
data. The highest concentrations of pharmaceuticals (>1000 µg/L in a single screen or sample) were
observed at the southeastern part of the plume near the factory site and at the earlier proposed main
contaminant discharge area of the stream as well as along the stream in the western direction. Based
on earlier and newly acquired chemical data, it was conﬁrmed that the plume of pharmaceuticals is
complex with diﬀerences in dominating substance groups in the plume and over depth as well as
considerable changes in concentration levels of individual compounds within a few years.
The geological, hydrological and redox conditions at the site were assessed to formulate a basis for
later interpretations of the potential for biodegradation. A determination of the vertical hydraulic
gradients and horizontal hydraulic conductivities in selected wells veriﬁed the properties of the
Miocene sand deposits already suggested in previous studies of the site. The redox conditions in the
aquifer were assessed to be anaerobic, with mainly iron-reducing conditions from the factory site to
Grindsted stream and some highly reduced zones with methanogenic conditions near the factory site
and in the main contaminant discharge area at the stream. The conditions were less reduced in the
western direction along the stream. The predominant redox conditions are believed to be favourable
for reductive degradation pathways of the pharmaceuticals while oxidative degradation pathways are
expected to be limited by the redox conditions.
Various tools for in-situ degradation assessment were reviewed for the information they provide and
their applicability in assessing degradation of pharmaceutical compounds. A combination of three
tools was applied to assess the potential for biodegradation and to document the degradation at the
91
==PAGE:106==
CHAPTER 9. CONCLUSION
Grindsted site. The tools were analysis for metabolites, characterisation of microbial degraders and
use of inherent tracers.
An analytical method for determination of three metabolites of sulfonamides was developed and
implemented in the laboratory. The analysis revealed that two degradation products of sulfonamides,
benzene sulfonamide and p-benzoquinone, were present in the aquifer.
Especially, the presence
of benzene sulfonamide is a direct measure for degradation of sulfonamides.
Furthermore, the
occurrence of pharmaceutical degradation in the plume was supported by increasing metabolite/parent
pharmaceutical ratios along the plume for selected sulfonamides, likely to also exist as metabolites.
The applied metabolite analysis was concluded to be a strong tool for documentation of degradation and
indication of degradation pathways, however, the included metabolites were not compound-speciﬁc
and only provided evidence for degradation of sulfonamides.
Bromide and benzene were applied as inherent tracers. Trends in the pharmaceutical/tracer-ratios
supported that degradation of selected sulfonamides, known to be biodegradable, occur while other
sulfonamides are formed as metabolites. Degradation of selected barbiturates was also indicated
but only limited knowledge about their biodegradability exist to support these observations. Among
the remaining key pharmaceuticals, several compounds were pointed towards as subjects for further
investigations, based on the trends in ratios. Usage of inherent tracers was found to rather support the
other tools than documenting in-situ degradation.
The characterisation of bacterial diversity supported the potential for degradation of sulfonamides as
three bacterial genera (Bacillus, Burkholderia and Pseudomonas) of species able to degrade selected
barbiturates and sulfonamides were observed in the aquifer. The relative abundances of the bacteria
ranged between 0.02 to 1.5 %, with the highest abundance west of the main contaminant discharge
area at the stream. The low relative abundances are likely due to lack of aerobiosis in the aquifer and
the antimicrobial properties of sulfonamides. The evaluation of the degradation potential was limited
by the absence of information on the presence of speciﬁc degraders, their activity and the bacterial
concentrations at the site. Hence, supporting work is required for further assessment of the potential
for biodegradation.
A simple conceptual model of the degradation of pharmaceuticals was proposed based on the ﬁndings
from the applied degradation assessment tools as well as the described geology, hydrogeology and
contaminant distribution. This model implied that degradation of sulfonamides occurs in the plume
from the southern part of the Grindsted factory site towards Grindsted stream and along the stream,
with decreasing degradation potential towards west. Degradation products formed by reduction such
as sulfanilamide and benzene sulfonamide are mainly generated centrally in the plume while sulfanilic
acid and other metabolites from oxidative processes are mainly formed closer to Grindsted Stream
and close to the factory site.
The compiled results of this project formed the basis for an evaluation of the potential for MNA as
the recommended remediation strategy. Potential for MNA was implied by the presence of bacterial
genera of species known to degrade pharmaceuticals and determination of relevant metabolites,
however, several circumstances argued against the viability of this remediation approach. The main
counterargument was that high concentrations of pharmaceuticals are continuously discharging to
Grindsted stream. Furthermore, favourable conditions for degradation of the compounds concerning
speciﬁc microorganisms and redox conditions have not yet been demonstrated and the degradation
of the majority of the pharmaceuticals are still not well understood. To conclude, MNA is not a
viable strategy for remediation of the plume of pharmaceuticals based on the ﬁndings in this thesis,
however, further investigations are required to fully evaluate its potential.
Recommendations for
further investigations have been provided.
92
==PAGE:107==
Chapter 10
Recommendations for further investigations
Based on the work conducted in this thesis, this section will list recommendations for further work
with assessment of the degradation of pharmaceuticals in the plume from the factory site in Grindsted.
I. Acquire data around the microbial composition and presence of metabolites at additional sam-
pling locations in the plume from Grindsted factory to support the stated evaluations of the
potential and occurrence of biodegradation of pharmaceuticals. It is suggested to include well
site FG2 and GÅ2 in the evaluation. Well site FG2 is suggested because a diﬀerent pharma-
ceutical composition, dominated by sulfonamides, is observed at this site compared to well site
FG1. Furthermore, inclusion of this well can contribute to extending the knowledge about the
aquifer close to the factory site. Well site GÅ2, which is located in the western direction along
Grindsted stream, is suggested as the highest relative abundance of pharmaceutical degraders
was present at well site GÅ1 and the redox conditions was assessed to be less reduced along
the stream. Thus, this well site could be investigated to examine how the microbial composition
changes along the stream and under less reduced conditions.
Furthermore, it is recommended to investigated other screens in the already included wells (FG1,
114.1447, GÅ1 and GÅ3) to extend the knowledge around degradation over depth in the plume.
II. Conduct a more speciﬁc screening of the microbial composition in the samples, only including
genera considered to have a direct inﬂuence on the biodegradation of pharmaceuticals. Ideally,
the samples are characterised for the speciﬁc bacterial degraders and the functional gene(s)
capable of the degradation. Initially, it is recommended to test a more speciﬁc screening on a
sample from GÅ1-F4 where the highest abundance of pharmaceutical degraders was detected
and later extend the analysis to the three remaining sampling locations (well screen FG1-
F5, 114.1447-F2 and 114.2508-F1). In addition, it is recommended to determine the total
concentration of bacteria in each sample for quantiﬁcation of the current microbial results.
An additional recommendation is to extract large amounts of groundwater (>2L) for microbial
characterisations at the Grindsted site or in other groundwater systems to secure that the concen-
tration of DNA is above the detection limit in the samples. At the investigated locations at the
Grindsted site, it is possible to extract large amounts of groundwater within a manageable time
frame while at other locations or sites it may be a challenge. Thus, the amount of groundwater
sampled should be decided based on the speciﬁc site conditions.
III. Develop and reﬁne the determination of metabolites, which could not be analyzed in this project
due to interference from the sample matrix. It is suggested to optimize the LSMSMS positive and
negative electrospray method for benzoquinone, hydroquinone and phenylenediamine as these
compounds have already been examined and their commercial standards have been purchased
during earlier implementation of the analysis method in the laboratory. The remaining metabo-
93
==PAGE:108==
CHAPTER 10. RECOMMENDATIONS FOR FURTHER INVESTIGATIONS
lites listed in 5.3, which are covered by other analyses, originates from parent compounds found
at concentrations below 100 µg/L at the Grindsted site. The metabolite, 2-aminopyrimidine, of
sulfadiazine was below the detection limit at all sampling locations in the metabolite screening
conducted in this thesis. Thus, detection of other metabolites of sulfadiazine is deemed unlikely
at the current detection limit of 0.1 µg/L.
IV. Extend the knowledge around the degradation of barbiturates, N-N-diethylnicotinamide, ethyl
urethaneandmeprobamateconcerningbiodegradability, degradationpathways, potentialmetabo-
lites and speciﬁc microbial degraders. Initially, it is recommended to study the pharmaceuticals
which were pointed towards in the assessment using inherent tracers. Information about the
degradation of sulfonamides can also be improved concerning e.g. degradation pathways.
V. Improve the conceptual understanding of the plume of pharmaceuticals by application of other
assessment tools including statistics. Statistical tools may improve the understanding of the
distribution of pharmaceuticals contaminants in the plume by grouping the wells according to
similarities in contaminant composition and concentration levels as well as combining it with
groundwater ﬂow models suggested in previous studies (see a possible statistical approach in
Balbarini et al. (2020)).
Installation of new wells between the factory site and Grindsted stream is also recommended
to improve the understanding of the pharmaceutical plume. The westward migration of a deep
plume suggested in previous studies (eg. Frederiksen (2017) and Balbarini et al. (2020)) is still
not well described and it is unknown if and where this plume discharges to Grindsted stream.
Thus, it is recommended to install wells closer to the stream in this area. Furthermore, it is
also suggested to extend the transect along Grindsted stream in the eastern direction (east of
well 114.1448) with new wells and investigate the content of pharmaceuticals to examine the
distribution of the pharmaceutical plume in this direction.
Besides, the linkage between the pharmaceutical compounds present in the plume and the ones
detected in the stream water of Grindsted stream is not well-understood and further investigations
are needed. It is also recommended to assess the already available data for pharmaceuticals in
the stream water in more details.
VI. Conduct CSIA investigations at the Grindsted site to support the evaluation of biodegradation
of pharmaceutical compounds. A collaboration between Helmholtz Centre for Environmental
Research (UFZ) and Technical University of Denmark (DTU) has been established to develop and
apply CSIA for selected sulfonamides. The time frame of the project is that the development of
necessary CSIA protocols are performed in year 2021 and ﬁeld application starts soon thereafter.
VII. Investigate the relation between the degradation of chlorinated ethenes, BTEXs and pharmaceu-
ticals and the possible impact of one another. The current distribution and known degradation
of chlorinated ethenes and BTEXs at the site can be outlined based on the existing studies
and the trends in data could be identiﬁed using statistical approaches, including correlation
matrices, principal component analysis and hierarchal cluster analysis (see possible approach in
Balbarini et al. (2020)). Aspects considered interesting to investigate includes; (1) if the BTEX
compounds aﬀect the redox conditions in a favourable or unfavourable way for degradation of
pharmaceuticals; (2) if the bacteria involved in the biodegradation of BTEXs have a competitive
inﬂuence on the pharmaceutical degraders; and (3) whether the pharmaceutical substances,
mainly sulfonamides, have antimicrobial activity on certain degrader bacteria, preventing them
from growing. The latter two aspects are suggested to be supported by laboratory experiments
including laboratory microcosms based on site material from the Grindsted Factory plume.

Appendix B
Field and laboratory methods
In total, three ﬁeld investigations at the Grindsted site was conducted during Autumn 2020. Slug tests
for estimation of hydraulic conductivity were performed in October 2020 as a part of the project by
Haugsted and Lade (2020) where assistance during the ﬁeldwork and later data treatment is a part of
this thesis. Thus, the methodology of slug testing and the following data treatment are described. The
vertical hydraulic heads were also sounded in the investigated wells in connection with slug testing.
At the ﬁeld investigations on November 4th to 5th and on December 1st to 2nd, the purpose was
to collect groundwater samples for microbial characterisation by Orvion, chemical analyses by ALS
Denmark and metabolite screening by DTU Environment. In addition, the latter ﬁeld investigation
also included sounding of vertical hydraulic heads at wells FG1, 114.1447, 114.2508, GÅ1 and GÅ3.
The following sections describe the ﬁeld methods, laboratory analyses and subsequent data treatment.
Table 5.1 in Chapter 5 provides an overview of the conducted activities at each ﬁeld investigations and
the included wells.
B.1
Determination of sampling locations
The ﬁeld investigation covered the area from the factory site to Grindsted stream as well as a stretch
along the stream in the western direction. Five sampling locations were selected; four within the plume
(high concentrations of pharmaceuticals) and one location outside the main plume (low concentrations
of pharmaceuticals). The following criteria were used to select the four wells and associated screens
to sample within the plume:
1. Best possible coverage of the plume and the varying compound composition
2. High concentrations of potential degradation products (sulfaguanidine, sulfanilamide, sulfanilic
acid and aniline) in previous studies
3. High concentrations of pharmaceuticals in previous studies
4. QuantArray-chlor analysis in previous studies
5. Previous concentration measurements of bromide and lithium
Table B.1 provides information about the selected sampling locations including well ID, DGU no.,
screen depth, screen diameter, and dominating pharmaceutical group. The locations of the selected
wells are presented in Figure B.1.

Note, that wells and screens for slug testing and well sounding in October 2020 were selected on other
criteria described by Haugsted and Lade (2021).
B.2
Field methods
B.2.1
Collection of samples
All selected wells were purged with a MP1 pump before sampling to obtain a valid sample. Typically,
wells are purged until the ﬁeld parameters (oxygen, hydraulic conductivity, pH and temperature) are
118
==PAGE:133==
APPENDIX B. FIELD AND LABORATORY METHODS
stable, however, no associated equipment was available at the ﬁeld investigation in November 2020.
Thus, each well was purged three to four times the amount of water contained initially inside the well.
Afterwards, the groundwater samples were collected in the associated vials and bottles using the MP1
pump. The equipment and setup for purging and sampling are shown in Figure B.2.
At the ﬁeld investigation in December 2020, the ﬁeld parameters were measured during purging and
the stability of the parameters were observed. Yet, the wells were still purged three to four times the
volume of water inside the well prior to sampling.
Figure B.2: Overview of setup and equipment used for purging and sampling of the wells.
Samples for chemical analysis
Samples for chemical analysis were collected on November 4th to 5th at all sampling locations.
Sampling equipment for the following analysis packages was provided by ALS; Grindsted package
A (pharmaceuticals), Grindsted package B (anilines), chlorinated solvents and degradation products,
hydrocarbons (including BTEX) and main constituents (well control). Three 1 L bottles were ﬁlled
for analyses of hydrocarbons, pharmaceuticals and aniline, while three 40 glass vials of water were
collected with no air bubbles for the analysis of chlorinated ethenes and degradation products. Diﬀerent
types of vials and bottles were ﬁlled for analysis of main constituents including redox parameters,
NVOC, metals, methane, carbon dioxide and sulﬁde. It was stated on the sampling equipment if these
samples needed to be ﬁltered or collect without air bubbles.
All samples were stored in a cooling box and collected by ALS on November 5th at the study site.
Samples for microbial analyses
The ﬁrst samples for microbial analysis were collected on November 4th to 5th at all sampling locations.
Sampling bottles of 600 mL with preservative liquid were supplied by Orvion. The bottles were ﬁlled
completely with groundwater but not overﬂowed before they were closed and swivel several times to
mix with the preservative liquid. There was no need for refrigeration after sampling and bottles were
shipped back to Orvion in the Netherlands on November 6th. Unfortunately, the analyses of the ﬁrst
samples were not successful due to very low DNA concentrations and a second sampling round was
necessary.
On December 1st to 2nd, new samples were collected at the same sampling locations. Unlike the ﬁrst
sampling round, 2000 mL of groundwater were collected at each location and the sampling bottles did
119
==PAGE:134==
APPENDIX B. FIELD AND LABORATORY METHODS
not contain any preservative liquid. In addition, the samples were shipped directly to Orvion from the
sampling site in styrofoam containers with cooling elements.
Samples for metabolite screening
Samples for internal analyses at DTU Environment were collected on November 4th to 5th and on
December 1st to 2nd at all sampling locations. In the ﬁrst sampling round, three 40 ml glass vials
of groundwater were collected at each location while 1000 mL was collected at each location in the
second sampling round. After the sampling round, the samples were frozen as the following analyses
were not developed at the times of sampling. During the freezing of the samples, the majority of the
glass vials and bottles cracked but is was assumed to have limited eﬀect on the ﬁnal analysis results.
B.2.2
Well sounding
Prior to purging and collection of samples on the 2nd of December, all screens at the sampling locations
were sounding using a Solinst water level meter, Model 101. The depth from the top of the well pipe
to the water level was recorded.
The same procedure was used in the sounding performed in connection with slug testing.
B.2.3
Slug tests
A slug test is a controlled ﬁeld experiment to estimate the hydraulic properties of aquifers. The water
level in the investigated well is caused to change suddenly (rise or fall) and the response in hydraulic
head over time in the well is measured. The obtained data can be used to estimate the hydraulic
conductivity of the associated aquifer system (Cheremisinoﬀ, 1997; Hvorslev, 1951).
Typically, slug tests are classiﬁed as either rising-head or falling-head tests depending on the direction
of water-level recovery in the investigated well. In this project, a falling-head slug test technique called
vacuum slug test was applied. A vacuum was induced inside the well by a vacuum cleaner which raised
the water level, and the change in head over time upon release was measured by a pressure transducer
immersed in the well. The technique behind vacuum slug testing is described in more details in Hinsby
et al. (1992) and Nardo and Perna (2017). The setup used at the study site is displayed in Figure B.3.
To correctly realise the vacuum, a kitchen cloth around the lower part of the bladder was used to seal
the well (see Figure B.3B). Pictures of each part of the equipment can be found in Nardo and Perna
(2017).
120
==PAGE:135==
APPENDIX B. FIELD AND LABORATORY METHODS
Figure B.3: Overview of setup for slug testing.
Slug tests were performed in 12 screens at four wells sites; FG1, FG2, FG3 and FA1. The selected
screens are displayed in Table 6.1 in Subsection 6.1. Three slug tests were performed at each screen
to account for the precision of the method.
B.3
Laboratory work
B.3.1
Screening for metabolites at DTU Environment
Details about the analysis were provided in a note by Mikael E. Olsson from DTU Environment which
also performed the analyses. The following descriptions are based on the provided note and personal
communication with Mikael E. Olsson.
Principle
The sulfonamides, sulfanilamide and sulfadiazine, and selected degradation products hereof (2-
aminopyimidine, hydroquinone, benzoquinone, p-phenylenediamine and benzene sulfonamide) were
analyzed by High Performance liquid Chromatography – Triple Quadrupole Mass Spectrometry (1290
Inﬁnity II- 6470 LC-QQQ, Agilent Technologies).
Preparation of the samples
Prior to the analysis, the samples needed to thaw out as they were frozen. As the glass vials and bottles
had cracked during the freezing, the 40 mL vials were placed in glass beakers during thawing while
the larger bottles (1 L) were placed in larger plastic containers as no large glass beakers were available.
During thawing, it is essential to get the aqueous phase and any compound sticking to the glass or lid
as a consequence of freezing mixed thoroughly again. It was determined that the broken vials and
bottles had limited inﬂuence on the quality of the samples, thus, they are assumed to be representative
for the analysis.
After thawing, the samples were ﬁltered using using 0.22 µm ﬁlters and each sample was concentrated
by solid phase extraction using Oasis™PRiME HLB and 100 mL of the sample.
Chromatography
Chromatographic separation was achieved on a 2.1 x 100 mm, 1.8 micron reversed phase column
(Zorbax EclipsePlus C18 RR, Agilent Technologies), with 0.1% formic acid as aqueous phase (A) and
121
==PAGE:136==
APPENDIX B. FIELD AND LABORATORY METHODS
acetonitrile with 0.1% formic acid as mobile phase (B) using the following gradient: 100% A for 2.5
min at then up to 80% B in 6 min with column regeneration time for 3 min. The ﬂow was kept at 0.5
mL/min and the column temperature at 35 °C. For each analysis, 60 µL sample was injected.
Detection
Detection was achieved in ESI+ mode (except for hydroquinone) using dynamic Multiple Reaction
Monitoring (dMRM) with the source temperature at 350 °C, 10 L/min nebulizer gas ﬂow at 35 psi
and the sheath gas at 400 °C at 12 L/min and the capillary voltage at 4500 V (500 V for ESI-). The
collision energy voltage (CID), fragmentor voltage (FRAGM) and cell acceleration (Cell Acc) voltage
were optimized for each compound (see MSMS settings in Table B.2).
Table B.2: Overview of MSMS settings for EPI+.
