B.3.2
Microbial analysis by Orvion
The microbial analysis were carried out by the environmental biotechnology company Orvion. The
presence of diﬀerent organisms in the samples were characterised using Next Generation Sequencing
(NGS).
Initially, the ﬁve samples were prepared for sequencing. The DNA of each sample was isolated using
QIAamp Powerfecal pro DNA kit from QIAGEN. The principle behind the kit is to capture the total
genomic DNA on a silica membrane. The DNA can then be washed and eluted from the membrane,
whereafter the sample is ready for NGS (see further details in the kit handbook by QIAGEN (2020)).
Afterwards, the samples were prepared with the Oxford Nanopore Technology SQK-LSK109 kit which
is a ligation sequencing kit which was used to prepare the sequencing libraries (see further details at
the website of Oxford Nanopore Technology (nd)). The sequencing preparation was successfully for
all samples except the reference sample from screen GÅ3-F4 where the concentration of DNA was
below the detection limit after ﬁltering and DNA extraction using all (2000 mL) sampled groundwater.
The four remaining samples were sequenced and the raw data were base called, whereof the latter is a
process by which an order of nucleotides in the template is inferred during the sequencing reaction. The
base-calling approach depend on the NGS technology. In the illumina technology, the ﬂuorescently
labeled reversible terminators have a unique color for each base (Ledergerber and Dessimoz, 2011).
The exact NGS technology used by Orvion is unknown.
Lastly, the sequencing results are compared against a database to identify the microbial genera. The
database used by Orvion for identiﬁcation is custom made by the company, but it based on existing
microbial databases including Reference Sequence (RefSeq) database, the Nucleotide (nt) database,
122
==PAGE:137==
APPENDIX B. FIELD AND LABORATORY METHODS
Microbial Database for Activated Sludge (MiDAS) and Whole Genome Shotgun (WGS). The applied
database included only bacteria and archaea. All data were processed in an interactive ﬁle showing
the composition of the biodiversity. The results from the chemical analysis of samples collected in
November 2020 were included in the interactive ﬁle.
B.4
Data processing of slug test data
The software AQTESOLV was employed for analysis and interpretation of the raw ﬁeld data from the
slug tests to determine the hydraulic conductivity. The raw data ﬁles were adjusted to express head
above static water level [m asl] prior to import of the data to the software. Besides, if necessary, the
data were converted to falling head curves, which means that measurements obtained using pressure
for initial displacement were inverted with respect to the static water level.
Based on the type of aquifer, diﬀerent solutions were applied to estimate hydraulic conductivity. At
the study site in Grindsted, well screens are located both above and below the succession of clay
and lignite present between 25- and 15-m asl. Based on the type of aquifer, diﬀerent solutions were
applied to estimate hydraulic conductivity. At the study site in Grindsted, well screens are located both
above and below the succession of clay and lignite present between 25- and 15-m asl. As described
in Section 2.2.1, this layer is thickest close to the stream and gets thinner towards the factory site. In
Balbarini et al. (2020), it is stated that the lignite layer is so thin from well 114.1447 and closer to
the factory site that only an unconﬁned aquifer exists. Thus, in the processing of slug test data from
well site FG1, FG2 and FG3 it is assumed that the aquifer is unconﬁned. It is debatable whether the
succession of clay and lignite is conﬁning at FA1 as minor pressure gradient (0.25-0.3 m) exists across
the boundary. Keeping this in mind, the aquifer at well site FA1 is assumed to be conﬁned below the
lignite layer and unconﬁned above it.
In addition, each screen should be classiﬁed according to the forces dominating the system. This is
based on the response measured during the slug test which may be described as either overdamped or
underdamped. Examples of each type of responses are shown in Figure B.4.
Figure B.4: Screen shots from AQTESOLV showing examples of (A) overdamped (well FG3-F3)
and (B) underdamped (well FA1-F4) slug test responses.
Overdamped responses are where the water level decreases consistently with time, while underdamped
responses are oscillatory. The underdamped behaviour is caused by inertial eﬀects and is sometimes
123
==PAGE:138==
APPENDIX B. FIELD AND LABORATORY METHODS
observed in high conductivity aquifers. Both types of responses were observed in the slug testing at
Grindsted. Table B.3 summarises the solutions employed to analyse data from each type of aquifer.
Table B.3: Summary of the four aquifer types observed at the Grindsted site, and the corresponding
solutions suggested by Duﬃeld (2004) and previous investigations of the Grindsted aquifer.
Conﬁning conditions
Response
Solution
Unconﬁned
Overdamped
Bouwer-Rice (1976)
Unconﬁned
Underdamped
Springer-Gelhar (1991)
Conﬁned
Overdamped
Hvorslev (1951)
Conﬁned
Underdamped
Butler (1998), McElwee-Zenner (1998)
The Bouwer-Rice (1976) solution is most suitable for the unconﬁned overdamped wells in the Grindsted
area, based on previous validation by Frederiksen (2017), while the solution from Hvorslev (1951) is
preferred for the conﬁned overdamped wells (Duﬃeld, 2004). Both solutions are semi-analytical and
are valid for both fully and partially penetrating wells, whereof all investigated wells belong to the latter
category. Besides, both models employ a quasi-steady-state model that ignores elastic storage in the
aquifer (Duﬃeld, 2004). Regarding unconﬁned underdamped wells, the solution by Springer-Gelhar
(1991) is preferred, while both the solution by Butler (1998) and the solution by McElwee-Zenner
(1998) are suitable in the case of conﬁned underdamped wells (Duﬃeld, 2004). Thus, the results from
the latter two solutions are averaged to obtain one K-value. The solution by Springer-Gelhar (1991)
is an extension of Bouwer-Rice (1976), while the solutions by Butler (1998) and McElwee-Zenner
(1998) are extensions of the Hvorslev solution. Alike the Bouwer-Rice and Hvorslev solutions, all
three solutions also employ a quasi-steady state model but they are only valid for partially penetrating
wells.
The experiment methods and data analysis used to determine the hydraulic conductivity in this project
are based on several assumptions including a homogeneous aquifer of uniform thickness and inﬁnite
areal extent (Duﬃeld, 2004). This is hardly fulﬁlled for any aquifer, however, the Grindsted aquifer is
considered to be in reasonable accordance with this ideal. Besides, the models assume that the initial
displacement of the water level in the investigated well occurs instantaneously, which is not fulﬁlled
during a vacuum slug test as it takes in the order of half a minute to establish the vacuum. However,
since the method is a part of the standard methods, this inconsistency is not considered critical.

Appendix G
Summary of dominating pharmaceuticals in
the plume
Figure G.1 and Figure G.2 presents the dominating barbiturates and the dominating sulfonamides
at the investigated locations, respectively. Figure G.3 shows where sulfanilic acid, ethyl urethane,
meprobamate or N-N-diethylnicotinamide are detected in maximum concentrations above 10 µg/L.
Only the three compounds with the highest maximum concentrations at each location are included in
the ﬁgures and the only compounds with a detected concentration above 10 µg/L are considered. The
compounds are listed at each location according to their maximum concentrations, from highest to
lowest. Note, that the concentrations levels at several locations vary considerably over depth.
With regards to the barbiturates, many of the same compounds are dominating at the investigated
locations and all compounds, except aethallymal, are also considered key compounds (see Chapter 3).
Allobarbital is one of the dominating barbiturates at all locations except FA1 and 114.2508. At these
two locations, amobarbital is the dominating barbiturate. Amobarbital is also one of the dominating
compounds at 114.2568. Apart from allobarbital, isopropyl barbituric acid and 5-butylbarbiturate are
dominating at the southern part of the factory site and at the stream bank. In the streambed and at the
stream bank, isobutyl barbituric acid is one of the dominating compounds. Barbital is also dominating
at several locations, especially at the two northern well sites (FG2 and FG3) close to the factory and
towards the stream, however, the compound was not detected in the streambed.
Similar to the barbiturates, almost the same sulfonamides are detected at all investigated locations
and all are considered key compounds (see Chapter 3). At some of the western locations (FG3, GÅ2
and GÅ3), sulfonamides are not detected in concentrations above 10 µg/L. At the southern part of
the factory site (FG1 and FG2), the contamination is dominated by sulfathiazole and sulfadimidine.
Further downstream and towards the stream, sulfanilamide, sulfacetamide and sulfadimine are the
dominating compounds. In the streambed, the highest concentrations of sulfonamides are detected,
with sulfacarbamide as the dominating compound. At GÅ1, sulfacarbamide is also dominating.
Meprobamate is detected at all locations except at well site FG3 and is dominating at almost all locations
where none of the other compounds (ethyl urethane, N-N-diethylnicotinamide and sulfanilic acid) is
detected in concentrations above 10 µg/L. Ethyl urethane is dominating at well site FG1 and FA1, but
is also one of the dominating compounds towards the stream at well 114.2568 and 114.2508. In the
streambed, sulfanilic acid is the dominating compound while N-N-diethylnicotinamide is primarily
found in the western part of the plume from well site FG3 to GÅ1.

Appendix K
Supplementary information - Inherent
tracers
K.1
Scatter plots for selected selected key pharmaceuticals
The following ﬁgure displays the concentration of benzene or bromide (x) as a function of the
concentrations of selected individual pharmaceuticals (y) with a the base-10 logarithmic y-axis. This
was done to test the exponential relationships, as the variable y is an exponential function of x if log(y)
is a linear function of x. Figure K.1 presents the scatter plots between; bromide and isobutyl barbituric
acid, bromide and sulfanilic acid, and benzene and isobutyl barbituric acid.

152
==PAGE:167==
APPENDIX L. SUPPLEMENTARY INFORMATION - MICROBIAL CHARACTERISATION
L.2
Identiﬁcation of natural degradation potential
The potential for microbial degradation of the diﬀerent contaminant groups in the Grindsted aquifer
was assessed based on the presence of bacteria involved in the degradation as well as the correlation
with degradation target compounds and degradation products.
L.2.1
Chlorinated ethenes
Dehalococcoides is a well-known genus of bacteria involved in the anaerobic degradation of chlo-
rinated ethenes and is the most abundant bacteria present at the Grindsted site. It is detected at
abundances ranging from 2.0 to 23.7% of the total biodiversity, with the highest abundance at well
114.2508. The distribution of Dehalococcoides in the ﬁve investigated well screens is consistent with
the microbial data from March to May 2020. The abundance of Dehalococcoides is clearly correlated
with vinyl chloride (Figure L.2A) and ethene (Figure L.2B).
Figure L.2: Abundance of Dehalococcoides and the concentrations of (A) vinylchloride and (B)
ethene, and (C) the abundance of Geobacter and the concentration of iron in the four analysed
samples.
153
==PAGE:168==
APPENDIX L. SUPPLEMENTARY INFORMATION - MICROBIAL CHARACTERISATION
Other genera involved in dehalogenation (anaerobic) were also detected among the dominant bacterial
genera, although at considerably lower abundances. One of these is Geobacter, which are an iron-
reducing genus known for its dehalogenation ability. Geobacter is detected at a higher abundance (4.8
%) than Dehalococcoides at GÅ1-F4, which is consistent with the highest concentration of iron (80
mg/L) detected at this location (Figure L.2C). Bacteria involved in aerobic degradation of chlorinated
ethenes were almost not detected at the site (abundances below 0.2%). Further information about the
degradation of chlorinated ethenes in the Grindsted area is found in Balbarini et al. (2018); Rønde
et al. (2017) and Ottosen et al. (2020). More details about the degradation of chlorinated ethenes
and the involved bacteria are given in Bradley and Chapelle (2010); Lee et al. (2008) and Tiehm and
Schmidt (2011), among other literature.
L.2.2
BTEX compounds
Several bacteria able to degrade BTEX compound were detected in all four samples from the Grindsted
site. The total abundance of BTEX degraders were around 2-7% at the locations, with Geobacter
as the dominating bacteria. Geobacter were mostly detected at 114.2508-F1 and GÅ1-F4 and are
known to degrade toluene. However, no clear connection between the abundance of Geobacter and
the concentration of toluene was observed (Figure L.3A).
Figure L.3: (A) Abundance of Geobacter and the concentration of toluene. (B) Total abundance of
benzene anaerobic degraders, excluding Geobacter, and the concentration of benzene.
154
==PAGE:169==
APPENDIX L. SUPPLEMENTARY INFORMATION - MICROBIAL CHARACTERISATION
If Geobacter was excluded, the total abundance of bacteria known to degrade benzene under anaerobic
conditions was around 1-2%, with the highest abundances in 114.2508-F1 and GÅ1-F4.
Figure
L.3B presents the total abundance of benzene anaerobic degraders along with the detected benzene
concentration in the four analysed samples, however, no correlation between the abundance and
concentration were observed.
Aerobic BTEX degradation is not dominant at the site, but bacterial genera are found at up to 2%
abundance in well GÅ1-F4, mostly Pseudomonas.
155
